D. Howells\r\n\r\nAuthor Of \xe2\x80\x9cThe Coast Of Bohemia\xe2\x80\x9d, \xe2\x80\x9cThe Quality Of Mercy\xe2\x80\x9d,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA Hazard Of New Fortunes\xe2\x80\x9d Etc.\r\n\r\nHarper & Brothers Publishers\r\n\r\nNew York And London\r\n\r\n1908\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nA TRAVELER FROM ALTRURIA\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nI\r\n\r\n\r\nI confess that with all my curiosity to meet an Altrurian, I was in no\r\nhospitable mood toward the traveler when he finally presented himself,\r\npursuant to the letter of advice sent me by the friend who introduced him.\r\nIt would be easy enough to take care of him in the hotel; I had merely to\r\nengage a room for him, and have the clerk tell him his money was not good\r\nif he tried to pay for anything. But I had swung fairly into my story; its\r\npeople were about me all the time; I dwelt amid its events and places, and\r\nI did not see how I could welcome my guest among them, or abandon them for\r\nhim. Still, when he actually arrived, and I took his hand as he stepped\r\nfrom the train, I found it less difficult to say that I was glad to see\r\nhim than I expected. In fact, I was glad, for I could not look upon his\r\nface without feeling a glow of kindness for him. I had not the least\r\ntrouble in identifying him, for he was so unlike all the Americans who\r\ndismounted from the train with him, and who all looked hot, worried, and\r\nanxious. He was a man no longer young, but in what we call the heyday of\r\nlife, when our own people are so absorbed in making provision for the\r\nfuture that they may be said not to live in the present at all. This\r\nAltrurian\xe2\x80\x99s whole countenance, and especially his quiet, gentle eyes,\r\nexpressed a vast contemporaneity, with bounds of leisure removed to the\r\nend of time; or, at least, this was the effect of something in them which\r\nI am obliged to report in rather fantastic terms. He was above the middle\r\nheight, and he carried himself vigorously. His face was sunburned, or\r\nsea-burned, where it was not bearded; and, although I knew from my\r\nfriend\xe2\x80\x99s letter that he was a man of learning and distinction in his own\r\ncountry, I should never have supposed him a person of scholarly life, he\r\nwas so far from sicklied over with anything like the pale cast of thought.\r\nWhen he took the hand I offered him in my half-hearted welcome he gave it\r\na grasp that decided me to confine our daily greetings to something much\r\nless muscular.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me have your bag,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, as we do when we meet people at the train,\r\nand he instantly bestowed a rather heavy valise upon me, with a smile in\r\nhis benignant eyes, as if it had been the greatest favor. \xe2\x80\x9cHave you got\r\nany checks?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, in very good English, but with an accent new to me, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nbought two.\xe2\x80\x9d He gave them to me, and I passed them to our hotel porter,\r\nwho was waiting there with the baggage-cart. Then I proposed that we\r\nshould walk across the meadow to the house, which is a quarter of a mile\r\nor so from the station. We started, but he stopped suddenly and looked\r\nback over his shoulder. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, you needn\xe2\x80\x99t be troubled about your trunks,\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cThe porter will get them to the house all right. They\xe2\x80\x99ll be in your\r\nroom by the time we get there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut he\xe2\x80\x99s putting them into the wagon himself,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; he always does that. He\xe2\x80\x99s a strong young fellow. He\xe2\x80\x99ll manage it.\r\nYou needn\xe2\x80\x99t--\xe2\x80\x9d I could not finish saying he need not mind the porter; he\r\nwas rushing back to the station, and I had the mortification of seeing him\r\ntake an end of each trunk and help the porter toss it into the wagon; some\r\nlighter pieces he put in himself, and he did not stop till all the baggage\r\nthe train had left was disposed of.\r\n\r\nI stood holding his valise, unable to put it down in my embarrassment at\r\nthis eccentric performance, which had been evident not to me alone, but to\r\nall the people who arrived by the train, and all their friends who came\r\nfrom the hotel to meet them. A number of these passed me on the tally-ho\r\ncoach; and a lady, who had got her husband with her for over Sunday, and\r\nwas in very good spirits, called gayly down to me: \xe2\x80\x9cYour friend seems fond\r\nof exercise!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered, dryly; the sparkling repartee which ought to have come\r\nto my help failed to show up. But it was impossible to be vexed with the\r\nAltrurian when he returned to me, unruffled by his bout with the baggage\r\nand serenely smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI fancied that good fellow was ashamed of my\r\nhelping him. I hope it didn\xe2\x80\x99t seem a reflection upon him in any way before\r\nyour people? I ought to have thought of that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI guess we can make it right with him. I dare say he felt more surprised\r\nthan disgraced. But we must make haste a little now; your train was half\r\nan hour late, and we shall not stand so good a chance for supper if we are\r\nnot there pretty promptly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, with evasive lightness, \xe2\x80\x9cfirst come, first served, you\r\nknow. That\xe2\x80\x99s human nature.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it?\xe2\x80\x9d he returned, and he looked at me as one does who suspects another\r\nof joking.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d I retorted; but I hurried to add: \xe2\x80\x9cBesides, I want to\r\nhave time after supper to show you a bit of our landscape. I think you\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nenjoy it.\xe2\x80\x9d I knew he had arrived in Boston that morning by steamer, and I\r\nnow thought it high time to ask him: \xe2\x80\x9cWell, what do you think of America,\r\nanyway?\xe2\x80\x9d I ought really to have asked him this the moment he stepped from\r\nthe train.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m intensely interested,\xe2\x80\x9d and I perceived that he spoke\r\nwith a certain reservation. \xe2\x80\x9cAs the most advanced country of its time,\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve always been very curious to see it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe last sentence raised my dashed spirits again, and I said, confidently:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must find our system of baggage-checks delightful.\xe2\x80\x9d I said this\r\nbecause it is one of the first things we brag of to foreigners, and I had\r\nthe habit of it. \xe2\x80\x9cBy-the-way,\xe2\x80\x9d I ventured to add, \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you meant to\r\nsay you _brought_ two checks when I asked you for them at the train just\r\nnow? But you really said you _bought_ them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d the Altrurian replied, \xe2\x80\x9cI gave half a dollar apiece for them at the\r\nstation in Boston. I saw other people doing it,\xe2\x80\x9d he explained, noting my\r\nsurprise. \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it the custom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m happy to say it isn\xe2\x80\x99t yet, on most of our roads. They were tipping\r\nthe baggage-man, to make sure that he checked their baggage in time and\r\nput it on the train. I had to do that myself when I came up; otherwise it\r\nmight have got along here some time next day. But the system is perfect.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe poor man looked quite worn out,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, \xe2\x80\x9cand I am glad I\r\ngave him something. He seemed to have several hundred pieces of baggage to\r\nlook after, and he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t embarrassed like your porter by my helping him\r\nput my trunks into the car. May I confess that the meanness of the\r\nstation, its insufficient facilities, its shabby waiting-rooms, and its\r\nwhole crowded and confused appearance gave me rather a bad impression?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know,\xe2\x80\x9d I had to own, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s shameful; but you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have found\r\nanother station in the city so bad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose this particular road is too\r\npoor to employ more baggage-men or build new stations; they seemed rather\r\nshabby all the way up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, no,\xe2\x80\x9d I was obliged to confess, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s one of the richest roads in\r\nthe country. The stock stands at about 180. But I\xe2\x80\x99m really afraid we shall\r\nbe late to supper if we don\xe2\x80\x99t get on,\xe2\x80\x9d I broke off; though I was not\r\naltogether sorry to arrive after the porter had disposed of the baggage. I\r\ndreaded another display of active sympathy on the part of my strange\r\ncompanion; I have often felt sorry myself for the porters of hotels, but I\r\nhave never thought of offering to help them handle the heavy trunks that\r\nthey manage.\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian was delighted with the hotel; and in fact it did look\r\nextremely pretty, with its branching piazzas full of well-dressed people,\r\nand its green lawns where the children were playing. I led the way to the\r\nroom which I had taken for him next my own; it was simply furnished, but\r\nit was sweet with matting, fresh linen, and pure whitewashed walls. I\r\nflung open the window-blinds and let him get a glimpse of the mountains\r\npurpling under the sunset, the lake beneath, and the deeply foliaged\r\nshores.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGlorious! glorious!\xe2\x80\x9d he sighed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d I modestly assented. \xe2\x80\x9cWe think that\xe2\x80\x99s rather fine.\xe2\x80\x9d He stood\r\ntranced before the window, and I thought I had better say: \xe2\x80\x9cWell, now I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t give you much time to get the dust of travel off; the dining-room\r\ndoors close at eight, and we must hurry down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll be with you in a moment,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, pulling off his coat.\r\n\r\nI waited impatiently at the foot of the stairs, avoiding the question I\r\nmet on the lips and in the eyes of my acquaintance. The fame of my\r\nfriend\xe2\x80\x99s behavior at the station must have spread through the whole place;\r\nand everybody wished to know who he was. I answered simply he was a\r\ntraveler from Altruria; and in some cases I went further and explained\r\nthat the Altrurians were peculiar.\r\n\r\nIn much less time than it seemed my friend found me; and then I had a\r\nlittle compensation for my suffering in his behalf. I could see that,\r\nwhatever people said of him, they felt the same mysterious liking at\r\nsight of him that I had felt. He had made a little change in his dress,\r\nand I perceived that the women thought him not only good-looking but\r\nwell-dressed. They followed him with their eyes as we went into the\r\ndining-room, and I was rather proud of being with him, as if I somehow\r\nshared the credit of his clothes and good looks. The Altrurian himself\r\nseemed most struck with the head-waiter, who showed us to our places, and\r\nwhile we were waiting for our supper I found a chance to explain that he\r\nwas a divinity student from one of the fresh-water colleges, and was\r\nserving here during his summer vacation. This seemed to interest my friend\r\nso much that I went on to tell him that many of the waitresses, whom he\r\nsaw standing there subject to the order of the guests, were country\r\nschool-mistresses in the winter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, that is as it should be,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cthat is the kind of thing I\r\nexpected to meet with in America.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d I responded, in my flattered national vanity, \xe2\x80\x9cif America means\r\nanything at all it means the honor of work and the recognition of personal\r\nworth everywhere. I hope you are going to make a long stay with us. We\r\nlike to have travelers visit us who can interpret the spirit of our\r\ninstitutions as well as read their letter. As a rule Europeans never quite\r\nget our point of view. Now a great many of these waitresses are ladies, in\r\nthe true sense of the word--selfrespectful, intelligent, refined, and fit\r\nto grace--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was interrupted by the noise my friend made in suddenly pushing back his\r\nchair and getting to his feet. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re not\r\nill, I hope?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut he did not hear me. He had run half down the dining-hall toward the\r\nslender young girl who was bringing us our supper. I had ordered rather\r\ngenerously, for my friend had owned to a good appetite, and I was hungry\r\nmyself with waiting for him, so that the tray the girl carried was piled\r\nup with heavy dishes. To my dismay I saw, rather than heard at that\r\ndistance, the Altrurian enter into a polite controversy with her, and\r\nthen, as if overcoming all her scruples by sheer strength of will, possess\r\nhimself of the tray and make off with it toward our table. The poor child\r\nfollowed him, blushing to her hair; the head-waiter stood looking\r\nhelplessly on; the guests, who at that late hour were fortunately few,\r\nwere simply aghast at the scandal; the Altrurian alone seemed to think\r\nhis conduct the most natural thing in the world. He put the tray on the\r\nside-table near us, and in spite of our waitress\xe2\x80\x99s protests insisted upon\r\narranging the little bird-bath dishes before our plates. Then at last he\r\nsat down, and the girl, flushed and tremulous, left the room, as I could\r\nnot help suspecting, to have a good cry in the kitchen. She did not come\r\nback, and the head-waiter, who was perhaps afraid to send another in her\r\nplace, looked after our few wants himself. He kept a sharp eye on my\r\nfriend, as if he were not quite sure he was safe, but the Altrurian\r\nresumed the conversation with all that lightness of spirits which I\r\nnoticed in him after he helped the porter with the baggage. I did not\r\nthink it the moment to take him to task for what he had just done; I was\r\nnot even sure that it was the part of a host to do so at all, and between\r\nthe one doubt and the other I left the burden of talk to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a charming young creature!\xe2\x80\x9d he began. \xe2\x80\x9cI never saw anything prettier\r\nthan the way she had of refusing my help, absolutely without coquetry or\r\naffectation of any kind. She is, as you said, a perfect lady, and she\r\ngraces her work, as I am sure she would grace any exigency of life. She\r\nquite realizes my ideal of an American girl, and I see now what the spirit\r\nof your country must be from such an expression of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI wished to tell him that while a country school-teacher who waits at\r\ntable in a summer hotel is very much to be respected in her sphere, she is\r\nnot regarded with that high honor which some other women command among us;\r\nbut I did not find this very easy, after what I had said of our esteem for\r\nlabor; and while I was thinking how I could hedge, my friend went on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI liked England greatly, and I liked the English, but I could not like\r\nthe theory of their civilization or the aristocratic structure of their\r\nsociety. It seemed to me iniquitous, for we believe that inequality and\r\niniquity are the same in the last analysis.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this I found myself able to say: \xe2\x80\x9cYes, there is something terrible,\r\nsomething shocking, in the frank brutality with which Englishmen affirm\r\nthe essential inequality of men. The affirmation of the essential equality\r\nof men was the first point of departure with us when we separated from\r\nthem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian. \xe2\x80\x9cHow grandly it is expressed in your\r\nglorious Declaration!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you have read our Declaration of Independence, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEvery Altrurian has read that,\xe2\x80\x9d answered my friend.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d I went on smoothly, and I hoped to render what I was going to say\r\nthe means of enlightening him without offence concerning the little\r\nmistake he had just made with the waitress, \xe2\x80\x9cof course we don\xe2\x80\x99t take that\r\nin its closest literality.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you know it was rather the political than the social traditions of\r\nEngland that we broke with, in the Revolution.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow is that?\xe2\x80\x9d he returned. \xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t you break with monarchy and nobility,\r\nand ranks and classes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, we broke with all those things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I found them a part of the social as well as the political structure\r\nin England. You have no kings or nobles here. Have you any ranks or\r\nclasses?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, not exactly in the English sense. Our ranks and classes, such as we\r\nhave, are what I may call voluntary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I understand. I suppose that from time to time certain ones among you\r\nfeel the need of serving, and ask leave of the commonwealth to subordinate\r\nthemselves to the rest of the state and perform all the lowlier offices in\r\nit. Such persons must be held in peculiar honor. Is it something like\r\nthat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, no, I can\xe2\x80\x99t say it\xe2\x80\x99s quite like that. In fact I think I\xe2\x80\x99d better\r\nlet you trust to your own observation of our life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I\xe2\x80\x99m sure,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, with a simplicity so fine that it was\r\na long time before I could believe it quite real, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I shall approach\r\nit so much more intelligently with a little instruction from you. You say\r\nthat your social divisions are voluntary. But do I understand that those\r\nwho serve among you do not wish to do so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose they would serve if they could help it,\xe2\x80\x9d I replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurely,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, with a look of horror, \xe2\x80\x9cyou don\xe2\x80\x99t mean that\r\nthey are slaves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no! oh no!\xe2\x80\x9d I said; \xe2\x80\x9cthe war put an end to that. We are all free now,\r\nblack and white.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut if they do not wish to serve, and are not held in peculiar honor for\r\nserving--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see that my word \xe2\x80\x98voluntary\xe2\x80\x99 has misled you,\xe2\x80\x9d I put in. \xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t the\r\nword exactly. The divisions among us are rather a process of natural\r\nselection. You will see, as you get better acquainted with the workings of\r\nour institutions, that there are no arbitrary distinctions here but the\r\nfitness of the work for the man and the man for the work determines the\r\nsocial rank that each one holds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, that is fine!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Altrurian, with a glow of enthusiasm. \xe2\x80\x9cThen\r\nI suppose that these intelligent young people who teach school in winter\r\nand serve at table in the summer are in a sort of provisional state,\r\nwaiting for the process of natural selection to determine whether they\r\nshall finally be teachers or waiters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it might be stated in some such terms,\xe2\x80\x9d I assented, though I was not\r\naltogether easy in my mind. It seemed to me that I was not quite candid\r\nwith this most candid spirit. I added: \xe2\x80\x9cYou know we are a sort of\r\nfatalists here in America. We are great believers in the doctrine that it\r\nwill all come out right in the end.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t wonder at that,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, \xe2\x80\x9cif the process of\r\nnatural selection works so perfectly among you as you say. But I am afraid\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand this matter of your domestic service yet. I believe you\r\nsaid that all honest work is honored in America. Then no social slight\r\nattaches to service, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I can\xe2\x80\x99t say that, exactly. The fact is, a certain social slight\r\ndoes attach to service, and that is one reason why I don\xe2\x80\x99t quite like to\r\nhave students wait at table. It won\xe2\x80\x99t be pleasant for them to remember it\r\nin after-life, and it won\xe2\x80\x99t be pleasant for their children to remember\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen the slight would descend?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think it would. One wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like to think one\xe2\x80\x99s father or mother had\r\nbeen at service.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian said nothing for a moment. Then he remarked: \xe2\x80\x9cSo it seems\r\nthat while all honest work is honored among you, there are some kinds of\r\nhonest work that are not honored so much as others.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause some occupations are more degrading than others.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why?\xe2\x80\x9d he persisted, as I thought, a little unreasonably.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cI think I must leave you to imagine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid I can\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, sadly. \xe2\x80\x9cThen, if domestic service is\r\ndegrading in your eyes, and people are not willing servants among you, may\r\nI ask why any are servants?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a question of bread-and-butter. They are obliged to be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is, they are forced to do work that is hateful and disgraceful to\r\nthem because they cannot live without?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, not at all liking this sort of pursuit, and feeling\r\nit fair to turn even upon a guest who kept it up. \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it so with you in\r\nAltruria?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was so once,\xe2\x80\x9d he admitted, \xe2\x80\x9cbut not now. In fact, it is like a waking\r\ndream to find one\xe2\x80\x99s self in the presence of conditions here that we\r\noutlived so long ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was an unconscious superiority in this speech that nettled me, and\r\nstung me to retort: \xe2\x80\x9cWe do not expect to outlive them. We regard them as\r\nfinal, and as indestructibly based in human nature itself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, with a delicate and caressing courtesy, \xe2\x80\x9chave I\r\nsaid something offensive?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all,\xe2\x80\x9d I hastened to answer. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is not surprising that you did not\r\nget our point of view exactly. You will by-and-by, and then, I think, you\r\nwill see that it is the true one. We have found that the logic of our\r\nconvictions could not be applied to the problem of domestic service. It is\r\neverywhere a very curious and perplexing problem. The simple old solution\r\nof the problem was to own your servants; but we found that this was not\r\nconsistent with the spirit of our free institutions. As soon as it was\r\nabandoned the anomaly began. We had outlived the primitive period when the\r\nhousekeeper worked with her domestics and they were her help, and were\r\ncalled so; and we had begun to have servants to do all the household work,\r\nand to call them so. This state of things never seemed right to some of\r\nour purest and best people. They fancied, as you seem to have done, that\r\nto compel people through their necessities to do your hateful drudgery,\r\nand to wound and shame them with a name which every American instinctively\r\nresents, was neither republican nor Christian. Some of our thinkers tried\r\nto mend matters by making their domestics a part of their families; and in\r\nthe life of Emerson you\xe2\x80\x99ll find an amusing account of his attempt to have\r\nhis servant eat at the same table with himself and his wife. It wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwork. He and his wife could stand it, but the servant couldn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI paused, for this was where the laugh ought to have come in. The\r\nAltrurian did not laugh, he merely asked, \xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, because the servant knew, if they didn\xe2\x80\x99t, that they were a whole\r\nworld apart in their traditions, and were no more fit to associate than\r\nNew-Englanders and New-Zealanders. In the mere matter of education--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I thought you said that these young girls who wait at table here were\r\nteachers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I beg your pardon; I ought to have explained. By this time it had\r\nbecome impossible, as it now is, to get American girls to take service\r\nexcept on some such unusual terms as we have in a summer hotel; and the\r\ndomestics were already ignorant foreigners, fit for nothing else. In such\r\na place as this it isn\xe2\x80\x99t so bad. It is more as if the girls worked in a\r\nshop or a factory. They command their own time, in a measure, their hours\r\nare tolerably fixed, and they have one another\xe2\x80\x99s society. In a private\r\nfamily they would be subject to order at all times, and they would have no\r\nsocial life. They would be in the family, out not of it. American girls\r\nunderstand this, and so they won\xe2\x80\x99t go out to service in the usual way.\r\nEven in a summer hotel the relation has its odious aspects. The system of\r\ngiving fees seems to me degrading to those who have to take them. To offer\r\na student or a teacher a dollar for personal service--it isn\xe2\x80\x99t right, or I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t make it so. In fact, the whole thing is rather anomalous with us.\r\nThe best that you can say of it is that it works, and we don\xe2\x80\x99t know what\r\nelse to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I don\xe2\x80\x99t see yet,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, \xe2\x80\x9cjust why domestic service is\r\ndegrading in a country where all kinds of work are honored.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, my dear fellow, I have done my best to explain. As I intimated\r\nbefore, we distinguish; and in the different kinds of labor we distinguish\r\nagainst domestic service. I dare say it is partly because of the loss of\r\nindependence which it involves. People naturally despise a dependant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Altrurian, with that innocence of his which I was\r\nbeginning to find rather trying.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d I retorted. \xe2\x80\x9cBecause it implies weakness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd is weakness considered despicable among you?\xe2\x80\x9d he pursued.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn every community it is despised practically, if not theoretically,\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\ntried to explain. \xe2\x80\x9cThe great thing that America has done is to offer the\r\nrace an opportunity--the opportunity for any man to rise above the rest\r\nand to take the highest place, if he is able.\xe2\x80\x9d I had always been proud of\r\nthis fact, and I thought I had put it very well, but the Altrurian did not\r\nseem much impressed by it.\r\n\r\nHe said: \xe2\x80\x9cI do not see how it differs from any country of the past in\r\nthat. But perhaps you mean that to rise carries with it an obligation\r\nto those below \xe2\x80\x98If any is first among you, let him be your servant.\xe2\x80\x99 Is it\r\nsomething like that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it is not quite like that,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered, remembering how very little\r\nour self-made men as a class had done for others. \xe2\x80\x9cEvery one is expected\r\nto look out for himself here. I fancy that there would be very little\r\nrising if men were expected to rise for the sake of others, in America.\r\nHow is it with you in Altruria?\xe2\x80\x9d I demanded, hoping to get out of a\r\ncertain discomfort I felt in that way. \xe2\x80\x9cDo your risen men generally devote\r\nthemselves to the good of the community after they get to the top?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no rising among us,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with what seemed a perception of\r\nthe harsh spirit of my question; and he paused a moment before he asked in\r\nhis turn: \xe2\x80\x9cHow do men rise among you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat would be rather a long story,\xe2\x80\x9d I replied. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, putting it in the\r\nrough, I should say that they rose by their talents, their shrewdness,\r\ntheir ability to seize an advantage and turn it to their own account.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd is that considered noble?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is considered smart. It is considered at the worst far better than a\r\ndead level of equality. Are all men equal in Altruria? Are they all alike\r\ngifted or beautiful, or short or tall?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, they are only equal in duties and in rights. But, as you said just\r\nnow, that is a very long story. Are they equal in nothing here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are equal in opportunities.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d breathed the Altrurian, \xe2\x80\x9cI am glad to hear that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI began to feel a little uneasy, and I was not quite sure that this last\r\nassertion of mine would hold water. Everybody but ourselves had now left\r\nthe dining-room, and I saw the head-waiter eying us impatiently. I pushed\r\nback my chair and said: \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sorry to seem to hurry you, but I should like\r\nto show you a very pretty sunset effect we have here before it is too\r\ndark. When we get back, I want to introduce you to a few of my friends. Of\r\ncourse, I needn\xe2\x80\x99t tell you that there is a good deal of curiosity about\r\nyou, especially among the ladies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I found that the case in England, largely. It was the women who\r\ncared most to meet me. I understand that in America society is managed\r\neven more by women than it is in England.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s entirely in their hands,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, with the satisfaction we all feel\r\nin the fact. \xe2\x80\x9cWe have no other leisure class. The richest men among us are\r\ngenerally hard workers; devotion to business is the rule; but, as soon as\r\na man reaches the point where he can afford to pay for domestic service,\r\nhis wife and daughters expect to be released from it to the cultivation of\r\ntheir minds and the enjoyment of social pleasures. It\xe2\x80\x99s quite right.\r\nThat is what makes them so delightful to foreigners. You must have heard\r\ntheir praises chanted in England. The English find our men rather stupid,\r\nI believe; but they think our women are charming.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I was told that the wives of their nobility were sometimes\r\nAmericans,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian. \xe2\x80\x9cThe English think that you regard such\r\nmarriages as a great honor, and that they are very gratifying to your\r\nnational pride.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I suppose that is so in a measure,\xe2\x80\x9d I confessed. \xe2\x80\x9cI imagine that it\r\nwill not be long before the English aristocracy derives as largely from\r\nAmerican millionaires as from kings\xe2\x80\x99 mistresses. Not,\xe2\x80\x9d I added,\r\nvirtuously, \xe2\x80\x9cthat we approve of aristocracy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I understand that,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall hope to get your\r\npoint of view in this matter more distinctly by-and-by. As yet, I\xe2\x80\x99m a\r\nlittle vague about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I can gradually make it clear to you,\xe2\x80\x9d I returned.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nII\r\n\r\nWe left the hotel, and I began to walk my friend across the meadow toward\r\nthe lake. I wished him to see the reflection of the afterglow in its still\r\nwaters, with the noble lines of the mountain-range that glassed itself\r\nthere; the effect is one of the greatest charms of that lovely region, the\r\nsojourn of the sweetest summer in the world, and I am always impatient to\r\nshow it to strangers.\r\n\r\nWe climbed the meadow wall and passed through a stretch of woods to a path\r\nleading down to the shore, and, as we loitered along in the tender gloom\r\nof the forest, the music of the hermit-thrushes rang all round us like\r\ncrystal bells, like silver flutes, like the drip of fountains, like the\r\nchoiring of still-eyed cherubim. We stopped from time to time and\r\nlistened, while the shy birds sang unseen in their covert of shadows; but\r\nwe did not speak till we emerged from the trees and suddenly stood upon\r\nthe naked knoll overlooking the lake.\r\n\r\nThen I explained: \xe2\x80\x9cThe woods used to come down to the shore here, and we\r\nhad their mystery and music to the water\xe2\x80\x99s edge; but last winter the owner\r\ncut the timber off. It looks rather ragged now.\xe2\x80\x9d I had to recognize the\r\nfact, for I saw the Altrurian staring about him over the clearing in a\r\nkind of horror. It was a squalid ruin, a graceless desolation, which not\r\neven the pitying twilight could soften. The stumps showed their hideous\r\nmutilation everywhere; the brush had been burned, and the fires had\r\nscorched and blackened the lean soil of the hill-slope and blasted it with\r\nsterility. A few weak saplings, withered by the flames, drooped and\r\nstraggled about; it would be a century before the forces of nature could\r\nrepair the waste.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou say the owner did this?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian. \xe2\x80\x9cWho is the owner?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it does seem too bad,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered, evasively. \xe2\x80\x9cThere has been a\r\ngood deal of feeling about it. The neighbors tried to buy him off before\r\nhe began the destruction, for they knew the value of the woods as an\r\nattraction to summer-boarders; the city cottagers, of course, wanted to\r\nsave them, and together they offered for the land pretty nearly as much as\r\nthe timber was worth. But he had got it into his head that the land here\r\nby the lake would sell for building lots if it was cleared, and he could\r\nmake money on that as well as on the trees; and so they had to go. Of\r\ncourse, one might say that he was deficient in public spirit, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nblame him, altogether.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d the Altrurian assented, somewhat to my surprise, I confess.\r\n\r\nI resumed: \xe2\x80\x9cThere was no one else to look after his interests, and it was\r\nnot only his right but his duty to get the most he could for himself and\r\nhis own, according to his best light. That is what I tell people when they\r\nfall foul of him for his want of public spirit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe trouble seems to be, then, in the system that obliges each man to be\r\nthe guardian of his own interests. Is that what you blame?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I consider it a very perfect system. It is based upon individuality,\r\nand we believe that individuality is the principle that differences\r\ncivilized men from savages, from the lower animals, and makes us a nation\r\ninstead of a tribe or a herd. There isn\xe2\x80\x99t one of us, no matter how much he\r\ncensured this man\xe2\x80\x99s want of public spirit, but would resent the slightest\r\ninterference with his property rights. The woods were his; he had the\r\nright to do what he pleased with his own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo I understand you that, in America, a man may do what is wrong with his\r\nown?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe may do anything with his own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo the injury of others?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, not in person or property. But he may hurt them in taste and\r\nsentiment as much as he likes. Can\xe2\x80\x99t a man do what he pleases with his own\r\nin Altruria?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, he can only do right with his own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd if he tries to do wrong, or what the community thinks is wrong?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen the community takes his own from him.\xe2\x80\x9d Before I could think of\r\nanything to say to this he went on: \xe2\x80\x9cBut I wish you would explain to me\r\nwhy it was left to this man\xe2\x80\x99s neighbors to try and get him to sell his\r\nportion of the landscape?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, bless my soul!\xe2\x80\x9d I exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cwho else was there? You wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have\r\nexpected to take up a collection among the summer-boarders?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have been so unreasonable; but I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean that. Was\r\nthere no provision for such an exigency in your laws? Wasn\xe2\x80\x99t the state\r\nempowered to buy him off at the full value of his timber and his land?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly not,\xe2\x80\x9d I replied. \xe2\x80\x9cThat would be rank paternalism.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt began to get dark, and I suggested that we had better be going back\r\nto the hotel. The talk seemed already to have taken us away from all\r\npleasure in the prospect; I said, as we found our way through the rich,\r\nbalsam-scented twilight of the woods, where one joy-haunted thrush was\r\nstill singing: \xe2\x80\x9cYou know that in America the law is careful not to meddle\r\nwith a man\xe2\x80\x99s private affairs, and we don\xe2\x80\x99t attempt to legislate personal\r\nvirtue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut marriage,\xe2\x80\x9d he said--\xe2\x80\x9csurely you have the institution of marriage?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was really annoyed at this. I returned, sarcastically; \xe2\x80\x9cYes, I am glad\r\nto say that there we can meet your expectation; we have marriage, not only\r\nconsecrated by the church, but established and defended by the state.\r\nWhat has that to do with the question?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you consider marriage,\xe2\x80\x9d he pursued, \xe2\x80\x9cthe citadel of morality, the\r\nfountain of all that is pure and good in your private life, the source of\r\nhome and the image of heaven?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are some marriages,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, with a touch of our national humor,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthat do not quite fill the bill, but that is certainly our ideal of\r\nmarriage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen why do you say that you have not legislated personal virtue in\r\nAmerica?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have laws, I believe, against theft and murder,\r\nand slander and incest, and perjury and drunkenness?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, certainly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen it appears to me that you have legislated honesty, regard for human\r\nlife, regard for character, abhorrence of unnatural vice, good faith, and\r\nsobriety. I was told on the train coming up, by a gentleman who was\r\nshocked at the sight of a man beating his horse, that you even had laws\r\nagainst cruelty to animals.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and I am happy to say that they are enforced to such a degree that a\r\nman cannot kill a cat cruelly without being punished for it.\xe2\x80\x9d The\r\nAltrurian did not follow up his advantage, and I resolved not to be\r\noutdone in magnanimity. \xe2\x80\x9cCome, I will own that you have the best of me on\r\nthose points. I must say you\xe2\x80\x99ve trapped me very neatly, too; I can enjoy a\r\nthing of that kind when it\xe2\x80\x99s well done, and I frankly knock under. But I\r\nhad in mind something altogether different when I spoke. I was thinking of\r\nthose idealists who want to bind us hand and foot and render us the slaves\r\nof a state where the most intimate relations of life shall be penetrated\r\nby legislation and the very hearthstone shall be a tablet of laws.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t marriage a rather intimate relation of life?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Altrurian.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I understood that gentleman on the train to say that you had laws\r\nagainst cruelty to children, and societies established to see them\r\nenforced. You don\xe2\x80\x99t consider such laws an invasion of the home, do you, or\r\na violation of its immunities? I imagine,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the\r\ndifference between your civilization and ours is only one of degree, after\r\nall, and that America and Altruria are really one at heart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI thought his compliment a bit hyperbolical, but I saw that it was\r\nhonestly meant, and as we Americans are first of all patriots, and vain\r\nfor our country before we are vain for ourselves, I was not proof against\r\nthe flattery it conveyed to me civically if not personally.\r\n\r\nWe were now drawing near the hotel, and I felt a certain glow of pleasure\r\nin its gay effect on the pretty knoll where it stood. In its artless and\r\naccidental architecture it was not unlike one of our immense coastwise\r\nsteamboats. The twilight had thickened to dusk, and the edifice was\r\nbrilliantly lighted with electrics, story above story, which streamed into\r\nthe gloom around like the lights of saloon and state-room. The corner of\r\nwood making into the meadow hid the station; there was no other building\r\nin sight; the hotel seemed riding at anchor on the swell of a placid sea.\r\nI was going to call the Altrurian\xe2\x80\x99s attention to this fanciful resemblance\r\nwhen I remembered that he had not been in our country long enough to have\r\nseen a Fall River boat, and I made toward the house without wasting the\r\ncomparison upon him. But I treasured it up in my own mind, intending some\r\nday to make a literary use of it.\r\n\r\nThe guests were sitting in friendly groups about the piazzas or in rows\r\nagainst the walls, the ladies with their gossip and the gentlemen with\r\ntheir cigars. The night had fallen cool after a hot day, and they all had\r\nthe effect of having cast off care with the burden of the week that was\r\npast, and to be steeping themselves in the innocent and simple enjoyment\r\nof the hour. They were mostly middle-aged married folk, but some were old\r\nenough to have sons and daughters among the young people who went and came\r\nin a long, wandering promenade of the piazzas, or wove themselves through\r\nthe waltz past the open windows of the great parlor; the music seemed one\r\nwith the light that streamed far out on the lawn flanking the piazzas.\r\nEvery one was well-dressed and comfortable and at peace, and I felt that\r\nour hotel was in some sort a microcosm of the republic.\r\n\r\nWe involuntarily paused, and I heard the Altrurian murmur: \xe2\x80\x9cCharming,\r\ncharming! This is really delightful!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d I returned, with a glow of pride. \xe2\x80\x9cOur hotel here is a\r\ntype of the summer hotel everywhere; it\xe2\x80\x99s characteristic in not having\r\nanything characteristic about it; and I rather like the notion of the\r\npeople in it being so much like the people in all the others that you\r\nwould feel yourself at home wherever you met such a company in such a\r\nhouse. All over the country, north and south, wherever you find a group of\r\nhills or a pleasant bit of water or a stretch of coast, you\xe2\x80\x99ll find some\r\nsuch refuge as this for our weary toilers. We began to discover some time\r\nago that it would not do to cut open the goose that laid our golden eggs,\r\neven if it looked like an eagle, and kept on perching on our banners just\r\nas if nothing had happened. We discovered that, if we continued to kill\r\nourselves with hard work, there would be no Americans pretty soon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cHow delightfully you put it! How quaint! How\r\npicturesque! Excuse me, but I can\xe2\x80\x99t help expressing my pleasure in it. Our\r\nown humor is so very different.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d I said; \xe2\x80\x9cwhat is your humor like?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could hardly tell you, I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid; I\xe2\x80\x99ve never been much of a humorist\r\nmyself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain a cold doubt of something ironical in the man went through me, but I\r\nhad no means of verifying it, and so I simply remained silent, waiting for\r\nhim to prompt me if he wished to know anything further about our national\r\ntransformation from bees perpetually busy into butterflies occasionally\r\nidle. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd when you had made that discovery?\xe2\x80\x9d he suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, we\xe2\x80\x99re nothing if not practical, you know, and as soon as we made\r\nthat discovery we stopped killing ourselves and invented the summer\r\nresort. There are very few of our business or professional men now who\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t take their four or five weeks\xe2\x80\x99 vacation. Their wives go off early in\r\nthe summer, and, if they go to some resort within three or four hours of\r\nthe city, the men leave town Saturday afternoon and run out, or come up,\r\nand spend Sunday with their families. For thirty-eight hours or so a hotel\r\nlike this is a nest of happy homes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is admirable,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are truly a practical\r\npeople. The ladies come early in the summer, you say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sometimes in the beginning of June.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do they come for?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Altrurian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat for? Why, for rest!\xe2\x80\x9d I retorted, with some little temper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I thought you told me awhile ago that as soon as a husband could\r\nafford it he relieved his wife and daughters from all household work.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo he does.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen what do the ladies wish to rest from?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom care. It is not work alone that kills. They are not relieved from\r\nhousehold care even when they are relieved from household work. There is\r\nnothing so killing as household care. Besides, the sex seems to be born\r\ntired. To be sure, there are some observers of our life who contend that\r\nwith the advance of athletics among our ladies, with boating and bathing,\r\nand lawn-tennis and mountain-climbing and freedom from care, and these\r\nlong summers of repose, our women are likely to become as superior to the\r\nmen physically as they now are intellectually. It is all right. We should\r\nlike to see it happen. It would be part of the national joke.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, have you a national joke?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Altrurian. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, of course! You\r\nhave so much humor. I wish you could give me some notion of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it is rather damaging to any joke to explain it,\xe2\x80\x9d I replied, \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\nyour only hope of getting at ours is to live into it. One feature of it is\r\nthe confusion of foreigners at the sight of our men\xe2\x80\x99s willingness to\r\nsubordinate themselves to our women.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t find that very bewildering,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian. \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to\r\nme a generous and manly trait of the American character. I\xe2\x80\x99m proud to say\r\nthat it is one of the points at which your civilization and our own touch.\r\nThere can be no doubt that the influence of women in your public affairs\r\nmust be of the greatest advantage to you; it has been so with us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI turned and stared at him, but he remained insensible to my astonishment,\r\nperhaps because it was now too dark for him to see it. \xe2\x80\x9cOur women have no\r\ninfluence in public affairs,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, quietly, after a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey haven\xe2\x80\x99t? Is it possible? But didn\xe2\x80\x99t I understand you to imply just\r\nnow that your women were better educated than your men?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I suppose that, taking all sorts and conditions among us, the women\r\nare as a rule better schooled, if not better educated.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, apart from the schooling, they are not more cultivated?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn a sense you might say they were. They certainly go in for a lot of\r\nthings: art and music, and Browning and the drama, and foreign travel and\r\npsychology, and political economy and Heaven knows what all. They have\r\nmore leisure for it; they have all the leisure there is, in fact; our\r\nyoung men have to go into business. I suppose you may say our women are\r\nmore cultivated than our men; yes, I think there\xe2\x80\x99s no questioning that.\r\nThey are the great readers among us. We poor devils of authors would be\r\nbadly off if it were not for our women. In fact, no author could make a\r\nreputation among us without them. American literature exists because\r\nAmerican women appreciate it and love it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut surely your men read books?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome of them; not many, comparatively. You will often hear a complacent\r\nass of a husband and father say to an author: \xe2\x80\x98My wife and daughters know\r\nyour books, but I can\xe2\x80\x99t find time for anything but the papers nowadays. I\r\nskim them over at breakfast, or when I\xe2\x80\x99m going in to business on the\r\ntrain.\xe2\x80\x99 He isn\xe2\x80\x99t the least ashamed to say that he reads nothing but the\r\nnewspapers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you think that it would be better for him to read books?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, in the presence of four or five thousand journalists with drawn\r\nscalping-knives I should not like to say so. Besides, modesty forbids.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but, really,\xe2\x80\x9d the Altrurian persisted, \xe2\x80\x9cyou think that the literature\r\nof a book is more carefully pondered than the literature of a daily\r\nnewspaper?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose even the four or five thousand journalists with drawn\r\nscalping-knives would hardly deny that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd it stands to reason, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t it, that the habitual reader of\r\ncarefully pondered literature ought to be more thoughtful than the readers\r\nof literature which is not carefully pondered and which they merely skim\r\nover on their way to business?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe we began by assuming the superior culture of our women, didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwe? You\xe2\x80\x99ll hardly find an American that isn\xe2\x80\x99t proud of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, \xe2\x80\x9cif your women are generally better schooled\r\nthan your men, and more cultivated and more thoughtful, and are relieved\r\nof household work in such great measure, and even of domestic cares, why\r\nhave they no part in your public affairs?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI laughed, for I thought I had my friend at last. \xe2\x80\x9cFor the best of all\r\npossible reasons: they don\xe2\x80\x99t want it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, that\xe2\x80\x99s no reason,\xe2\x80\x9d he returned. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t they want it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, out of all patience, \xe2\x80\x9cI think I must let you ask the\r\nladies themselves,\xe2\x80\x9d and I turned and moved again toward the hotel, but the\r\nAltrurian gently detained me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me,\xe2\x80\x9d he began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no,\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98The feast is set, the guests are met,\r\n    May\xe2\x80\x99st hear the merry din.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nCome in and see the young people dance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait,\xe2\x80\x9d he entreated; \xe2\x80\x9ctell me a little more about the old people first.\r\nThis digression about the ladies has been very interesting, but I thought\r\nyou were going to speak of the men here. Who are they, or, rather, what\r\nare they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, as I said before, they are all business men and professional men;\r\npeople who spend their lives in studies and counting-rooms and offices,\r\nand have come up here for a few weeks or a few days of well-earned repose.\r\nThey are of all kinds of occupations: they are lawyers and doctors, and\r\nclergymen and merchants, and brokers and bankers. There\xe2\x80\x99s hardly any\r\ncalling you Won\xe2\x80\x99t find represented among them. As I was thinking\r\njust now, our hotel is a sort of microcosm of the American republic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am most fortunate in finding you here, where I can avail myself of your\r\nintelligence in making my observations of your life under such\r\nadvantageous circumstances. It seems to me that with your help I might\r\npenetrate the fact of American life, possess myself of the mystery of your\r\nnational joke, without stirring beyond the piazza of your hospitable\r\nhotel,\xe2\x80\x9d said my friend. I doubted it, but one does not lightly put aside a\r\ncompliment like that to one\xe2\x80\x99s intelligence, and I said I should be very\r\nhappy to be of use to him. He thanked me, and said: \xe2\x80\x9cThen, to begin with,\r\nI understand that these gentlemen are here because they are all\r\noverworked.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course. You can have no conception of how hard our business men and\r\nour professional men work. I suppose there is nothing like it anywhere\r\nelse in the world. But, as I said before, we are beginning to find that we\r\ncannot burn the candle at both ends and have it last long. So we put one\r\nend out for a little while every summer. Still, there are frightful wrecks\r\nof men strewn all along the course of our prosperity, wrecks of mind and\r\nbody. Our insane asylums are full of madmen who have broken under the\r\ntremendous strain, and every country in Europe abounds in our dyspeptics.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n I was rather proud of this terrible fact; there is no doubt but we\r\nAmericans are proud of overworking ourselves; Heaven knows why.\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian murmured: \xe2\x80\x9cAwful! Shocking!\xe2\x80\x9d But I thought somehow he had\r\nnot really followed me very attentively in my celebration of our national\r\nviolation of the laws of life and its consequences. \xe2\x80\x9cI am glad,\xe2\x80\x9d he went\r\non, \xe2\x80\x9cthat your business men and professional men are beginning to realize\r\nthe folly and wickedness of overwork. Shall I find some of your other\r\nweary workers here, too?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat other weary workers?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked in turn, for I imagined I had gone\r\nover pretty much the whole list.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, \xe2\x80\x9cyour mechanics and day laborers, your\r\niron-moulders and glass-blowers, your miners and farmers, your printers\r\nand mill-operatives, your trainmen and quarry-hands. Or do they prefer to\r\ngo to resorts of their own?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIII\r\n\r\nIt was not easy to make sure of such innocence as prompted this inquiry of\r\nmy Altrurian friend. The doubt whether he could really be in earnest was\r\nsomething that I had already felt; and it was destined to beset me, as it\r\ndid now, again and again. My first thought was that, of course, he was\r\ntrying a bit of cheap irony on me, a mixture of the feeble sarcasm and\r\nfalse sentiment that makes us smile when we find it in the philippics of\r\nthe industrial agitators. For a moment I did not know but I had fallen\r\nvictim to a walking delegate on his vacation, who was employing his summer\r\nleisure in going about the country in the guise of a traveler from\r\nAltruria, and foisting himself upon people who would have had nothing to\r\ndo with him in his real character. But in another moment I perceived that\r\nthis was impossible. I could not suppose that the friend who had\r\nintroduced him to me would be capable of seconding so poor a joke, and,\r\nbesides, I could not imagine why a walking delegate should wish to address\r\nhis clumsy satire to me particularly. For the present, at least, there was\r\nnothing for it but to deal with this inquiry as if it were made in good\r\nfaith and in the pursuit of useful information. It struck me as grotesque;\r\nbut it would not have been decent to treat it as if it were so. I was\r\nobliged to regard it seriously, and so I decided to shirk it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat opens up rather a large field, which lies somewhat\r\noutside of the province of my own activities. You know, I am a writer of\r\nromantic fiction, and my time is so fully occupied in manipulating the\r\ndestinies of the good old-fashioned hero and heroine, and trying always to\r\nmake them end in a happy marriage, that I have hardly had a chance to look\r\nmuch into the lives of agriculturists or artisans; and, to tell you the\r\ntruth, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what they do with their leisure. I\xe2\x80\x99m pretty certain,\r\nthough, you won\xe2\x80\x99t meet any of them in this hotel; they couldn\xe2\x80\x99t afford it,\r\nand I fancy they would find themselves out of their element among our\r\nguests. We respect them thoroughly; every American does, and we know that\r\nthe prosperity of the country rests with them; we have a theory that they\r\nare politically sovereign, but we see very little of them, and we don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nassociate with them. In fact, our cultivated people have so little\r\ninterest in them socially that they don\xe2\x80\x99t like to meet them, even in\r\nfiction; they prefer refined and polished ladies and gentlemen, whom they\r\ncan have some sympathy with; and I always go to the upper classes for my\r\ntypes. It won\xe2\x80\x99t do to suppose, though, that we are indifferent to the\r\nworking classes in their place. Their condition is being studied a good\r\ndeal just now, and there are several persons here who will be able to\r\nsatisfy your curiosity on the points you have made, I think. I will\r\nintroduce you to them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian did not try to detain me this time. He said he should be\r\nvery glad indeed to meet my friends, and I led the way toward a little\r\ngroup at the corner of the piazza. They were men whom I particularly\r\nliked, for one reason or another; they were intelligent and open-minded,\r\nand they were thoroughly American. One was a banker; another was a\r\nminister; there was a lawyer, and there was a doctor; there was a\r\nprofessor of political economy in one of our colleges; and there was a\r\nretired manufacturer--I do not know what he used to manufacture: cotton or\r\niron, or something like that. They all rose politely as I came up with my\r\nAltrurian, and I fancied in them a sensation of expectancy created by the\r\nrumor of his eccentric behavior which must have spread through the hotel.\r\nBut they controlled this if they had it, and I could see, as the light\r\nfell upon his face from a spray of electrics on the nearest pillar, that\r\nsort of liking kindle in theirs which I had felt myself at first sight of\r\nhim.\r\n\r\nI said, \xe2\x80\x9cGentlemen, I wish to introduce my friend, Mr. Homos,\xe2\x80\x9d and then I\r\npresented them severally to him by name. We all sat down, and I explained:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Homos is from Altruria. He is visiting our country for the first\r\ntime, and is greatly interested in the working of our institutions. He has\r\nbeen asking me some rather hard questions about certain phases of our\r\ncivilization; and the fact is that I have launched him upon you because I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t feel quite able to cope with him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey all laughed civilly at this sally of mine, but the professor asked,\r\nwith a sarcasm that I thought I hardly merited, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat point in our polity\r\ncan be obscure to the author of \xe2\x80\x98Glove and Gauntlet\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98Airs and\r\nGraces\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey all laughed again, not so civilly, I felt, and then the banker asked\r\nmy friend: \xe2\x80\x9cIs it long since you left Altruria?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems a great while ago,\xe2\x80\x9d the Altrurian answered, \xe2\x80\x9cbut it is really\r\nonly a few weeks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou came by way of England, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; there is no direct line to America,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat seems rather odd,\xe2\x80\x9d I ventured, with some patriotic grudge.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, the English have direct lines everywhere,\xe2\x80\x9d the banker instructed me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe tariff has killed our shipbuilding,\xe2\x80\x9d said the professor. No one took\r\nup this firebrand, and the professor added: \xe2\x80\x9cYour name is Greek, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it,\r\nMr. Homos?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; we are of one of the early Hellenic families,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd do you think,\xe2\x80\x9d asked the lawyer, who, like most lawyers, was a lover\r\nof romance, and was well read in legendary lore especially, \xe2\x80\x9cthat there is\r\nany reason for supposing that Altruria is identical with the fabled\r\nAtlantis\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I can\xe2\x80\x99t say that I do. We have no traditions of a submergence of the\r\ncontinent, and there are only the usual evidences of a glacial epoch which\r\nyou find everywhere to support such a theory. Besides, our civilization is\r\nstrictly Christian, and dates back to no earlier period than that of the\r\nfirst Christian commune after Christ. It is a matter of history with us\r\nthat one of these communists, when they were dispersed, brought the Gospel\r\nto our continent; he was cast away on our eastern coast on his way to\r\nBritain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, we know that,\xe2\x80\x9d the minister intervened, \xe2\x80\x9cbut it is perfectly\r\nastonishing that an island so large as Altruria should have been lost to\r\nthe knowledge of the rest of the world ever since the beginning of our\r\nera. You would hardly think that there was a space of the ocean\xe2\x80\x99s surface\r\na mile square which had not been traversed by a thousand keels since\r\nColumbus sailed westward.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t. And I wish,\xe2\x80\x9d the doctor suggested in his turn, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\r\nMr. Homos would tell us something about his country, instead of asking us\r\nabout ours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d I coincided, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure we should all find it a good deal easier. At\r\nleast I should; but I brought our friend up in the hope that the professor\r\nwould like nothing better than to train a battery of hard facts upon a\r\ndefenceless stranger.\xe2\x80\x9d Since the professor had given me that little stab,\r\nI was rather anxious to see how he would handle the desire for information\r\nin the Altrurian which I had found so prickly.\r\n\r\nThis turned the laugh on the professor, and he pretended to be as curious\r\nabout Altruria as the rest, and said he would rather hear of it. But the\r\nAltrurian said: \xe2\x80\x9cI hope you will excuse me. Sometime I shall be glad to\r\ntalk of Altruria as long as you like; or, if you will come to us, I shall\r\nbe still happier to show you many things that I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t make you\r\nunderstand at a distance. But I am in America to learn, not to teach, and\r\nI hope you will have patience with my ignorance. I begin to be afraid that\r\nit is so great as to seem a little incredible. I have fancied in my friend\r\nhere,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, with a smile toward me, \xe2\x80\x9ca suspicion that I was not\r\nentirely single in some of the inquiries I have made, but that I had some\r\nulterior motive, some wish to censure or satirize.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, not at all,\xe2\x80\x9d I protested, for it was not polite to admit a conjecture\r\nso accurate. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are so well satisfied with our condition that we have\r\nnothing but pity for the darkened mind of the foreigner, though we believe\r\nin it fully: we are used to the English tourist.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMy friends laughed, and the Altrurian continued: \xe2\x80\x9cI am very glad to hear\r\nit, for I feel myself at a peculiar disadvantage among you. I am not only\r\na foreigner, but I am so alien to you in all the traditions and habitudes\r\nthat I find it very difficult to get upon common ground with you. Of\r\ncourse, I know theoretically what you are, but to realize it practically\r\nis another thing. I had read so much about America and understood so\r\nlittle that I could not rest without coming to see for myself. Some of the\r\napparent contradictions were so colossal--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have everything on a large scale here,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker, breaking off\r\nthe ash of his cigar with the end of his little finger, \xe2\x80\x9cand we rather\r\npride ourselves on the size of our inconsistencies, even. I know something\r\nof the state of things in Altruria, and, to be frank with you, I will say\r\nthat it seems to me preposterous. I should say it was impossible, if it\r\nwere not an accomplished fact; but I always feel bound to recognize the\r\nthing done. You have hitched your wagon to a star, and you have made the\r\nstar go; there is never any trouble with wagons, but stars are not easily\r\nbroken to harness, and you have managed to get yours well in hand. As I\r\nsaid, I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe in you, but I respect you.\xe2\x80\x9d I thought this charming,\r\nmyself; perhaps because it stated my own mind about Altruria so exactly\r\nand in terms so just and generous.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPretty good,\xe2\x80\x9d said the doctor, in a murmur of satisfaction, at my ear,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cfor a bloated bond-holder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d I whispered back, \xe2\x80\x9cI wish I had said it. What an American way of\r\nputting it! Emerson would have liked it himself. After all, he was our\r\nprophet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe must have thought so from the way we kept stoning him,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\ndoctor, with a soft laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich of our contradictions,\xe2\x80\x9d asked the banker, in the same tone of\r\ngentle bonhomie, \xe2\x80\x9chas given you and our friend pause just now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian answered, after a moment: \xe2\x80\x9cI am not sure that it is a\r\ncontradiction, for as yet I have not ascertained the facts I was seeking.\r\nOur friend was telling me of the great change that had taken place\r\nin regard to work, and the increased leisure that your professional people\r\nare now allowing themselves; and I was asking him where your working-men\r\nspent their leisure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe went over the list of those he had specified, and I hung my head in\r\nshame and pity; it really had such an effect of mawkish sentimentality.\r\nBut my friends received it in the best possible way. They did not laugh;\r\nthey heard him out, and then they quietly deferred to the banker, who made\r\nanswer for us all:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I can be almost as brief as the historian of Iceland in his chapter\r\non snakes: those people have no leisure to spend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcept when they go out on a strike,\xe2\x80\x9d said the manufacturer, with a\r\ncertain grim humor of his own; I never heard anything more dramatic than\r\nthe account he once gave of the way he broke up a labor union. \xe2\x80\x9cI have\r\nseen a good many of them at leisure then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d the doctor chimed in, \xe2\x80\x9cand in my younger days, when I necessarily\r\nhad a good deal of charity practice, I used to find them at leisure when\r\nthey were \xe2\x80\x98laid off.\xe2\x80\x99 It always struck me as such a pretty euphemism. It\r\nseemed to minify the harm of the thing so. It seemed to take all the\r\nhunger and cold and sickness out of the fact. To be simply \xe2\x80\x98laid off\xe2\x80\x99 was\r\nso different from losing your work and having to face beggary or\r\nstarvation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose people,\xe2\x80\x9d said the professor, \xe2\x80\x9cnever put anything by. They are\r\nwasteful and improvident, almost to a man; and they learn nothing by\r\nexperience, though they know as well as we do that it is simply a question\r\nof demand and supply, and that the day of overproduction is sure to come,\r\nwhen their work must stop unless the men that give them work are willing\r\nto lose money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen them lose it, sometimes, rather than shut down,\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\nmanufacturer remarked; \xe2\x80\x9close it hand over hand, to keep the men at work;\r\nand then as soon as the tide turned the men would strike for higher wages.\r\nYou have no idea of the ingratitude of those people.\xe2\x80\x9d He said this toward\r\nthe minister, as if he did not wish to be thought hard; and, in fact, he\r\nwas a very kindly man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the minister, \xe2\x80\x9cthat is one of the most sinister features of\r\nthe situation. They seem really to regard their employers as their\r\nenemies. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know how it will end.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know how it would end if I had my way,\xe2\x80\x9d said the professor. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be any labor unions, and there wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be any strikes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is all very well,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lawyer, from that judicial mind which I\r\nalways liked in him, \xe2\x80\x9cas far as the strikes are concerned, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nunderstand that the abolition of the unions would affect the impersonal\r\nprocess of \xe2\x80\x98laying off.\xe2\x80\x99 The law of demand and supply I respect as much as\r\nany one--it\xe2\x80\x99s something like the constitution; but, all the same, I should\r\nobject extremely to have my income stopped by it every now and then. I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nprobably not so wasteful as a working-man generally is; still, I haven\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlaid by enough to make it a matter of indifference to me whether my income\r\nwent on or not. Perhaps the professor has.\xe2\x80\x9d The professor did not say, and\r\nwe all took leave to laugh. The lawyer concluded: \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see how those\r\nfellows stand it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey don\xe2\x80\x99t, all of them,\xe2\x80\x9d said the doctor. \xe2\x80\x9cOr their wives and children\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t. Some of them die.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder,\xe2\x80\x9d the lawyer pursued, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat has become of the good old American\r\nfact that there is always work for those who are willing to work? I notice\r\nthat wherever five thousand men strike in the forenoon, there are five\r\nthousand men to take their places in the afternoon--and not men who are\r\nturning their hands to something new, but men who are used to doing the\r\nvery thing the strikers have done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is one of the things that teach the futility of strikes,\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\nprofessor made haste to interpose, as if he had not quite liked to appear\r\naverse to the interests of the workman; no one likes to do that. \xe2\x80\x9cIf there\r\nwere anything at all to be hoped from them, it would be another matter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but that isn\xe2\x80\x99t the point, quite,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lawyer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy-the-way, what is the point?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, with my humorous lightness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I supposed,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker, \xe2\x80\x9cit was the question how the working\r\nclasses amused their elegant leisure. But it seems to be almost anything\r\nelse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe all applauded the neat touch, but the Altrurian eagerly entreated:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no; never mind that now. That is a matter of comparatively little\r\ninterest. I would so much rather know something about the status of the\r\nworking-man among you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean his political status? It\xe2\x80\x99s that of every other citizen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t mean that. I suppose that in America you have learned, as we have\r\nin Altruria, that equal political rights are only means to an end, and as\r\nan end have no value or reality. I meant the economic status of the\r\nworking-man, and his social status.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI do not know why we were so long girding up our loins to meet this simple\r\nquestion. I myself could not have hopefully undertaken to answer it; but\r\nthe others were each in their way men of affairs, and practically\r\nacquainted with the facts, except perhaps the professor; but he had\r\ndevoted a great deal of thought to them, and ought to have been qualified\r\nto make some sort of response. But even he was silent; and I had a vague\r\nfeeling that they were all somehow reluctant to formulate their knowledge,\r\nas if it were uncomfortable or discreditable. The banker continued to\r\nsmoke quietly on for a moment; then he suddenly threw his cigar away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like to free my mind of cant,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with a short laugh, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen I can\r\nafford it, and I propose to cast all sorts of American cant out of it in\r\nanswering your question. The economic status of the working-man among us\r\nis essentially the same as that of the working-man all over the civilized\r\nworld. You will find plenty of people here, especially about election\r\ntime, to tell you differently, but they will not be telling you the truth,\r\nthough a great many of them think they are. In fact, I suppose most\r\nAmericans honestly believe because we have a republican form of\r\ngovernment, and manhood suffrage, and so on, that our economic conditions\r\nare peculiar, and that our working-man has a status higher and better than\r\nthat of the working-man anywhere else. But he has nothing of the kind. His\r\ncircumstances are better, and provisionally his wages are higher, but it\r\nis only a question of years or decades when his circumstances will be the\r\nsame and his wages the same as the European working-man\xe2\x80\x99s. There is\r\nnothing in our conditions to prevent this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I understood from our friend here,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, nodding\r\ntoward me, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you had broken only with the political tradition of\r\nEurope in your Revolution; and he has explained to me that you do not hold\r\nall kinds of labor in equal esteem; but--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat kind of labor did he say we did hold in esteem?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the banker.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I understood him to say that if America meant anything at all it\r\nmeant the honor of work, but that you distinguished and did not honor some\r\nkinds of work so much as others; for instance, domestic service, or\r\npersonal attendance of any kind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe banker laughed again. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, he drew the line there, did he? Well, we\r\nall have to draw the line somewhere. Our friend is a novelist, and I will\r\ntell you in strict confidence that the line he has drawn is imaginary. We\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t honor any kind of work any more than any other people. If a fellow\r\ngets up, the papers make a great ado over his having been a woodchopper or\r\na bobbin-boy, or something of that kind, but I doubt if the fellow himself\r\nlikes it; he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t if he\xe2\x80\x99s got any sense. The rest of us feel that it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n_infra dig._, and hope nobody will find out that we ever worked with our\r\nhands for a living. I\xe2\x80\x99ll go further,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker, with the effect of\r\nwhistling prudence down the wind, \xe2\x80\x9cand I will challenge any of you to\r\ngainsay me from his own experience or observation. How does esteem usually\r\nexpress itself? When we wish, to honor a man, what do we do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsk him to dinner,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lawyer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly. We offer him some sort of social recognition. Well, as soon as a\r\nfellow gets up, if he gets up high enough, we offer him some sort of\r\nsocial recognition; in fact, all sorts; but upon condition that he has\r\nleft off working with his hands for a living. We forgive all you please to\r\nhis past on account of the present. But there isn\xe2\x80\x99t a working-man, I\r\nventure to say, in any city or town, or even large village, in the whole\r\nlength and breadth of the United States who has any social recognition, if\r\nhe is still working at his trade. I don\xe2\x80\x99t mean, merely, that he is\r\nexcluded from rich and fashionable society, but from the society of the\r\naverage educated and cultivated people. I\xe2\x80\x99m not saying he is fit for it;\r\nbut I don\xe2\x80\x99t care how intelligent and agreeable he might be--and some of\r\nthem are astonishingly intelligent, and so agreeable in their tone of mind\r\nand their original way of looking at things that I like nothing better\r\nthan to talk with them--all of our invisible fences are up against him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe minister said: \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if that sort of exclusiveness is quite\r\nnatural? Children seem to feel no sort of social difference among\r\nthemselves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can hardly go to children for a type of social order,\xe2\x80\x9d the professor\r\nsuggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue,\xe2\x80\x9d the minister meekly admitted. \xe2\x80\x9cBut somehow there is a protest in\r\nus somewhere against these arbitrary distinctions--something that\r\nquestions whether they are altogether right. We know that they must be,\r\nand always have been, and always will be, and yet--well, I will confess\r\nit--I never feel at peace when I face them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker, \xe2\x80\x9cif you come to the question of right and wrong,\r\nthat is another matter. I don\xe2\x80\x99t say it\xe2\x80\x99s right. I\xe2\x80\x99m not discussing that\r\nquestion; though I\xe2\x80\x99m certainly not proposing to level the fences; I should\r\nbe the last to take my own down. I say simply that you are no more likely\r\nto meet a working-man in American society than you are to meet a colored\r\nman. Now you can judge,\xe2\x80\x9d he ended, turning directly to the Altrurian, \xe2\x80\x9chow\r\nmuch we honor labor.
