And I hope I have indirectly satisfied your curiosity\r\nas to the social status of the working-man among us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe were all silent.\r\n\r\nPerhaps the others were occupied like myself in trying to recall some\r\ninstance of a working-man whom they had met in society, and perhaps we\r\nsaid nothing because we all failed.\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian spoke at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have been so very full and explicit that I feel as if it were almost\r\nunseemly to press any further inquiry; but I should very much like to know\r\nhow your working-men bear this social exclusion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure I can\xe2\x80\x99t say,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the banker. \xe2\x80\x9cA man does not care much to\r\nget into society until he has something to eat, and how to get that is\r\nalways the first question with the working-man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like it yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, certainly, I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like it myself. I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t complain of not\r\nbeing asked to people\xe2\x80\x99s houses, and the working-men don\xe2\x80\x99t; you can\xe2\x80\x99t do\r\nthat; but I should feel it an incalculable loss. We may laugh at the\r\nemptiness of society, or pretend to be sick of it, but there is no doubt\r\nthat society is the flower of civilization, and to be shut out from it is\r\nto be denied the best privilege of a civilized man. There are society\r\nwomen--we have all met them--whose graciousness and refinement of presence\r\nare something of incomparable value; it is more than a liberal education\r\nto have been admitted to it, but it is as inaccessible to the working-man\r\nas--what shall I say? The thing is too grotesquely impossible for any sort\r\nof comparison. Merely to conceive of its possibility is something that\r\npasses a joke; it is a kind of offence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain we were silent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d the banker continued, \xe2\x80\x9chow the notion of our social\r\nequality originated, but I think it has been fostered mainly by the\r\nexpectation of foreigners, who argued it from our political equality.\r\nAs a matter of fact, it never existed, except in our poorest and most\r\nprimitive communities, in the pioneer days of the West and among the\r\ngold-hunters of California. It was not dreamed of in our colonial society,\r\neither in Virginia or Pennsylvania or New York or Massachusetts; and the\r\nfathers of the republic, who were mostly slave-holders, were practically\r\nas stiff-necked aristocrats as any people of their day. We have not a\r\npolitical aristocracy, that is all; but there is as absolute a division\r\nbetween the orders of men and as little love, in this country as in any\r\ncountry on the globe. The severance of the man who works for his living\r\nwith his hands from the man who does not work for his living with his\r\nhands is so complete, and apparently so final, that nobody even imagines\r\nanything else, not even in fiction. Or, how is that?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, turning to\r\nme. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you fellows still put the intelligent, high-spirited, handsome\r\nyoung artisan, who wins the millionaire\xe2\x80\x99s daughter, into your books? I\r\nused sometimes to find him there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou might still find him in the fiction of the weekly story-papers; but,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n I was obliged to own, \xe2\x80\x9che would not go down with my readers. Even in the\r\nstory-paper fiction he would leave off working as soon as he married the\r\nmillionaire\xe2\x80\x99s daughter, and go to Europe, or he would stay here and become\r\na social leader, but he would not receive working-men in his gilded\r\nhalls.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe others rewarded my humor with a smile, but the banker said: \xe2\x80\x9cThen I\r\nwonder you were not ashamed of filling our friend up with that stuff about\r\nour honoring some kinds of labor. It is true that we don\xe2\x80\x99t go about openly\r\nand explicitly despising any kind of honest toil--people don\xe2\x80\x99t do that\r\nanywhere now; but we contemn it in terms quite as unmistakable. The\r\nworking-man acquiesces as completely as anybody else. He does not remain a\r\nworking-man a moment longer that he can help; and after he gets up, if he\r\nis weak enough to be proud of having been one, it is because he feels that\r\nhis low origin is a proof of his prowess in rising to the top against\r\nunusual odds. I don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose there is a man in the whole civilized\r\nworld--outside of Altruria, of course---who is proud of working at a\r\ntrade, except the shoemaker Tolstoy, and is a count, and he does not make\r\nvery good shoes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe all laughed again: those shoes of Count Tolstoy\xe2\x80\x99s are always such an\r\ninfallible joke.\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian, however, was cocked and primed with another question; he\r\ninstantly exploded it: \xe2\x80\x9cBut are all the working-men in America eager to\r\nrise above their condition? Is there none willing to remain among the mass\r\nbecause the rest could not rise with him, and from the hope of yet\r\nbringing labor to honor?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe banker answered: \xe2\x80\x9cI never heard of any. No, the American ideal is not\r\nto change the conditions for all, but for each to rise above the rest if\r\nhe can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think it is really so bad as that?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the minister, timidly.\r\n\r\nThe banker answered: \xe2\x80\x9cBad? Do you call that bad? I thought it was very\r\ngood. But, good or bad, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think you\xe2\x80\x99ll find it deniable, if you\r\nlook into the facts. There may be working-men willing to remain so for\r\nother working-men\xe2\x80\x99s sake, but I have never met any--perhaps because the\r\nworking-man never goes into society.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe unfailing question of the Altrurian broke the silence which ensued:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre there many of your working-men who are intelligent and agreeable--of\r\nthe type you mentioned a moment since?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker, \xe2\x80\x9cI had better refer you to one of our friends\r\nhere, who has had a great deal more to do with them than I have. He is a\r\nmanufacturer, and he has had to do with all kinds of work-people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, for my sins,\xe2\x80\x9d the manufacturer assented; and he added: \xe2\x80\x9cThey are\r\noften confoundedly intelligent, though I haven\xe2\x80\x99t often found them very\r\nagreeable, either in their tone of mind or their original way of looking\r\nat things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe banker amiably acknowledged his thrust, and the Altrurian asked: \xe2\x80\x9cAh,\r\nthey are opposed to your own?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we have the same trouble here that you must have heard of in\r\nEngland. As you know now that the conditions are the same here, you won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbe surprised at the fact.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the conditions,\xe2\x80\x9d the Altrurian pursued--\xe2\x80\x9cdo you expect them always to\r\ncontinue the same?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d said the manufacturer. \xe2\x80\x9cWe can\xe2\x80\x99t expect them to\r\nchange of themselves, and I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t know how to change them. It was\r\nexpected that the rise of the trusts and the syndicates would break the\r\nunions, but somehow they haven\xe2\x80\x99t. The situation remains the same. The\r\nunions are not cutting one another\xe2\x80\x99s throats now any more than we are. The\r\nwar is on a larger scale--that\xe2\x80\x99s all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen let me see,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, \xe2\x80\x9cwhether I clearly understand the\r\nsituation as regards the working-man in America. He is dependent upon the\r\nemployer for his chance to earn a living, and he is never sure of this. He\r\nmay be thrown out of work by his employer\xe2\x80\x99s disfavor or disaster, and his\r\nwillingness to work goes for nothing; there is no public provision of work\r\nfor him; there is nothing to keep him from want nor the prospect of\r\nanything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are all in the same boat,\xe2\x80\x9d said the professor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut some of us have provisioned ourselves rather better and can generally\r\nweather it through till we are picked up,\xe2\x80\x9d the lawyer put in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am always saying the working-man is improvident,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the\r\nprofessor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are the charities,\xe2\x80\x9d the minister suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut his economical status,\xe2\x80\x9d the Altrurian pursued, \xe2\x80\x9cis in a state of\r\nperpetual uncertainty, and to save himself in some measure he has\r\norganized, and so has constituted himself a danger to the public peace?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA very great danger,\xe2\x80\x9d said the professor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI guess we can manage him,\xe2\x80\x9d the manufacturer remarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd socially he is non-existent?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian turned with this question to the banker, who said: \xe2\x80\x9cHe is\r\ncertainly not in society.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen,\xe2\x80\x9d said my guest, \xe2\x80\x9cif the working-man\xe2\x80\x99s wages are provisionally so\r\nmuch better here than in Europe, why should they be discontented? What is\r\nthe real cause of their discontent?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI have always been suspicious, in the company of practical men, of an\r\natmosphere of condescension to men of my calling, if nothing worse. I\r\nfancy they commonly regard artists of all kinds as a sort of harmless\r\neccentrics, and that literary people they look upon as something droll, as\r\nweak and soft, as not quite right. I believed that this particular group,\r\nindeed, was rather abler to conceive of me as a rational person than most\r\nothers, but I knew that if even they had expected me to be as reasonable\r\nas themselves they would not have been greatly disappointed if I were not;\r\nand it seemed to me that I had put myself wrong with them in imparting to\r\nthe Altrurian that romantic impression that we hold labor in honor here. I\r\nhad really thought so, but I could not say so now, and I wished to\r\nretrieve myself somehow. I wished to show that I was a practical man, too,\r\nand so I made answer: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the cause of the working-man\xe2\x80\x99s discontent?\r\nIt is very simple: the walking delegate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIV\r\n\r\nI suppose I could not have fairly claimed any great originality for my\r\nnotion that the walking delegate was the cause of the labor troubles: he\r\nis regularly assigned as the reason of a strike in the newspapers, and is\r\nreprobated for his evil agency by the editors, who do not fail to read the\r\nworking-men many solemn lessons and fervently warn them against him, as\r\nsoon as the strike begins to go wrong--as it nearly always does. I\r\nunderstand from them that the walking delegate is an irresponsible tyrant,\r\nwho emerges from the mystery that habitually hides him and from time to\r\ntime orders a strike in mere rancor of spirit and plenitude of power, and\r\nthen leaves the working-men and their families to suffer the consequences,\r\nwhile he goes off somewhere and rolls in the lap of luxury, careless of\r\nthe misery he has created. Between his debauches of vicious idleness and\r\nhis accesses of baleful activity he is employed in poisoning the mind of\r\nthe working-men against his real interests and real friends. This is\r\nperfectly easy, because the American working-man, though singularly shrewd\r\nand sensible in other respects, is the victim of an unaccountable\r\nobliquity of vision which keeps him from seeing his real interests and\r\nreal friends--or, at least, from knowing them when he sees them.\r\n\r\nThere could be no doubt, I thought, in the mind of any reasonable person\r\nthat the walking delegate was the source of the discontent among our\r\nproletariate, and I alleged him with a confidence which met the approval\r\nof the professor, apparently, for he nodded, as if to say that I had hit\r\nthe nail on the head this time; and the minister seemed to be freshly\r\nimpressed with a notion that could not be new to him. The lawyer and the\r\ndoctor were silent, as if waiting for the banker to speak again; but he\r\nwas silent, too. The manufacturer, to my chagrin, broke into a laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nafraid,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with a sardonic levity which surprised me, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\xe2\x80\x99ll have\r\nto go a good deal deeper than the walking delegate. He\xe2\x80\x99s a symptom; he\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t the disease. The thing keeps on and on, and it seems to be always\r\nabout wages; but it isn\xe2\x80\x99t about wages at the bottom. Some of those fellows\r\nknow it and some of them don\xe2\x80\x99t, but the real discontent is with the whole\r\nsystem, with the nature of things. I had a curious revelation on that\r\npoint the last time I tried to deal with my men as a union. They were\r\nalways bothering me about this and about that, and there was no end to the\r\nbickering. I yielded point after point, but it didn\xe2\x80\x99t make any difference.\r\nIt seemed as if the more I gave the more they asked. At last I made up my\r\nmind to try to get at the real inwardness of the matter, and I didn\xe2\x80\x99t wait\r\nfor their committee to come to me--I sent for their leading man, and said\r\nI wanted to have it out with him. He wasn\xe2\x80\x99t a bad fellow, and when I got\r\nat him, man to man that way, I found he had sense, and he had ideas--it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nno use pretending those fellows are fools; he had thought about his side\r\nof the question, anyway. I said: \xe2\x80\x98Now what does it all mean? Do you want\r\nthe earth, or don\xe2\x80\x99t you? When is it going to end?\xe2\x80\x99 I offered him something\r\nto take, but he said he didn\xe2\x80\x99t drink, and we compromised on cigars. \xe2\x80\x98Now\r\nwhen is it going to end?\xe2\x80\x99 said I, and I pressed it home, and wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t let\r\nhim fight off from the point. \xe2\x80\x98Do you mean when is it all going to end?\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsaid he. \xe2\x80\x98Yes,\xe2\x80\x99 said I, \xe2\x80\x98all. I\xe2\x80\x99m sick of it. If there\xe2\x80\x99s any way out I\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nlike to know it.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Well,\xe2\x80\x99 said he, \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you, if you want to know.\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s all going to end when you get the same amount of money for the same\r\namount of work as we do.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe all laughed uproariously. The thing was deliciously comical; and\r\nnothing, I thought, attested the Altrurian\xe2\x80\x99s want of humor like his\r\nfailure to appreciate this joke. He did not even smile in asking: \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nwhat did you say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the manufacturer, with cosey enjoyment, \xe2\x80\x9cI asked him if\r\nthe men would take the concern and run it themselves.\xe2\x80\x9d We laughed again;\r\nthis seemed even better than the other joke. \xe2\x80\x9cBut he said, \xe2\x80\x98No\xe2\x80\x99; they\r\nwould not like to do that. And then I asked him just what they would like,\r\nif they could have their own way, and he said they would like to have me\r\nrun the business, and all share alike. I asked him what was the sense of\r\nthat, and why, if I could do something that all of them put together\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do, I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be paid more than all of them put together; and\r\nhe said that if a man did his best he ought to be paid as much as the best\r\nman. I asked him if that was the principle their union was founded on, and\r\nhe said, \xe2\x80\x98Yes,\xe2\x80\x99 that the very meaning of their union was the protection of\r\nthe weak by the strong and the equalization of earnings among all who do\r\ntheir best.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe waited for the manufacturer to go on, but he made a dramatic pause at\r\nthis point, as if to let it sink into our minds; and he did not speak\r\nuntil the Altrurian prompted him with the question, \xe2\x80\x9cAnd what did you\r\nfinally do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI saw there was only one way out for me, and I told the fellow I did not\r\nthink I could do business on that principle. We parted friends, but the\r\nnext Saturday I locked them out and smashed their union. They came back,\r\nmost of them--they had to--but I\xe2\x80\x99ve treated with them ever since \xe2\x80\x98as\r\nindividuals.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd they\xe2\x80\x99re much better off in your hands than they were in the union,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said the professor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know about that,\xe2\x80\x9d said the manufacturer, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I\xe2\x80\x99m sure I am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe laughed with him, all but the minister, whose mind seemed to have\r\ncaught upon some other point, and who sat absently by.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd is it your opinion, from what you know of the working-man generally,\r\nthat they all have this twist in their heads?\xe2\x80\x9d the professor asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have, until they begin to rise. Then they get rid of it mighty soon.\r\nLet a man save something--enough to get a house of his own, and take a\r\nboarder or two, and perhaps have a little money at interest--and he sees\r\nthe matter in another light.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think he sees it more clearly?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the minister.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe sees it differently.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you think?\xe2\x80\x9d the minister pursued, turning to the lawyer. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are\r\nused to dealing with questions of justice--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRather more with questions of law, I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid,\xe2\x80\x9d the other returned,\r\npleasantly, putting his feet together before him and looking down at them\r\nin a way he had. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, still, I have a great interest in questions of\r\njustice, and I confess that I find a certain wild equity in this\r\nprinciple, which I see nobody could do business on. It strikes me as\r\nidyllic--it\xe2\x80\x99s a touch of real poetry in the rough-and-tumble prose of our\r\neconomic life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe referred this to me as something I might appreciate in my quality of\r\nliterary man, and I responded in my quality of practical man: \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncertainly more rhyme than reason in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe turned again to the minister:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose the ideal of the Christian state is the family?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope so,\xe2\x80\x9d said the minister, with the gratitude that I have seen people\r\nof his cloth show when men of the world conceded premises which the world\r\nusually contests; it has seemed to me pathetic.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd if that is the case, why, the logic of the postulate is that the\r\nprosperity of the weakest is the sacred charge and highest happiness of\r\nall the stronger. But the law has not recognized any such principle, in\r\neconomics at least, and if the labor unions are based upon it they are\r\noutlaw, so far as any hope of enforcing it is concerned; and it is bad for\r\nmen to feel themselves outlaw. How is it,\xe2\x80\x9d the lawyer continued, turning\r\nto the Altrurian, \xe2\x80\x9cin your country? We can see no issue here, if the first\r\nprinciple of organized labor antagonizes the first principle of business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand precisely yet what the first principle of business\r\nis,\xe2\x80\x9d returned my guest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, that raises another interesting question,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lawyer. \xe2\x80\x9cOf\r\ncourse, every business man solves the problem practically according to his\r\ntemperament and education, and I suppose that on first thoughts every\r\nbusiness man would answer you accordingly. But perhaps the personal\r\nequation is something you wish to eliminate from the definition.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill, I would rather not venture upon it first,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lawyer.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cProfessor, what should you say was the first principle of business?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBuying in the cheapest market and selling in the dearest,\xe2\x80\x9d the professor\r\npromptly answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will pass the parson and the doctor and the novelist as witnesses of\r\nno value. They can\xe2\x80\x99t possibly have any cognizance of the first principle\r\nof business; their affair is to look after the souls and bodies and\r\nfancies of other people. But what should you say it was?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked the\r\nbanker.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should say it was an enlightened conception of one\xe2\x80\x99s own interests.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe manufacturer had no hesitation in answering: \xe2\x80\x9cThe good of Number One,\r\nfirst, last, and all the time. There may be a difference of opinion about\r\nthe best way to get at it; the long way may be the better, or the short\r\nway; the direct way or the oblique way, or the purely selfish way, or the\r\npartly selfish way; but if you ever lose sight of that end you might as\r\nwell shut up shop. That seems to be the first law of nature, as well as\r\nthe first law of business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, we mustn\xe2\x80\x99t go to nature for our morality,\xe2\x80\x9d the minister protested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe were not talking of morality,\xe2\x80\x9d said the manufacturer; \xe2\x80\x9cwe were talking\r\nof business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis brought the laugh on the minister, but the lawyer cut it short:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, I don\xe2\x80\x99t really see why the trades-unions are not as\r\nbusiness-like as the syndicates in their dealings with all those outside\r\nof themselves. Within themselves they practise an altruism of the highest\r\norder, but it is a tribal altruism; it is like that which prompts a Sioux\r\nto share his last mouthful with a starving Sioux, and to take the scalp of\r\na starving Apache. How is it with your trades-unions in Altruria?\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nasked my friend.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have no trades-unions in Altruria,\xe2\x80\x9d he began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHappy Altruria!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the professor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe had them formerly,\xe2\x80\x9d the Altrurian went on, \xe2\x80\x9cas you have them now. They\r\nclaimed, as I suppose yours do, that they were forced into existence by\r\nthe necessities of the case; that without union the working-man was unable\r\nto meet the capitalist on anything like equal terms, or to withstand his\r\nencroachments and oppressions. But to maintain themselves they had to\r\nextinguish industrial liberty among the working-men themselves, and they\r\nhad to practise great cruelties against those who refused to join them or\r\nwho rebelled against them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey simply destroy them here,\xe2\x80\x9d said the professor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lawyer, from his judicial mind, \xe2\x80\x9cthe great syndicates\r\nhave no scruples in destroying a capitalist who won\xe2\x80\x99t come into them or\r\nwho tries to go out. They don\xe2\x80\x99t club him or stone him, but they under-sell\r\nhim and freeze him out; they don\xe2\x80\x99t break his head, but they bankrupt him.\r\nThe principle is the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t interrupt Mr. Homos,\xe2\x80\x9d the banker entreated. \xe2\x80\x9cI am very curious to\r\nknow just how they got rid of labor unions in Altruria.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe had syndicates, too, and finally we had the _reductio ad absurdum_--we\r\nhad a federation of labor unions find a federation of syndicates, that\r\ndivided the nation into two camps. The situation was not only impossible,\r\nbut it was insupportably ridiculous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI ventured to say: \xe2\x80\x9cIt hasn\xe2\x80\x99t become quite so much of a joke with us yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it in a fair way to become so?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the doctor; and he turned to\r\nthe lawyer: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat should you say was the logic of events among us for the\r\nlast ten or twenty years?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s nothing so capricious as the logic of events. It\xe2\x80\x99s like a woman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nreasoning--you can\xe2\x80\x99t tell what it\xe2\x80\x99s aimed at, or where it\xe2\x80\x99s going to fetch\r\nup; all that you can do is to keep out of the way if possible. We may come\r\nto some such condition of things as they have in Altruria, where the faith\r\nof the whole nation is pledged to secure every citizen in the pursuit of\r\nhappiness; or we may revert to some former condition, and the master may\r\nagain own the man; or we may hitch and joggle along indefinitely, as we\r\nare doing now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut come, now,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker, while he laid a caressing touch on the\r\nAltrurian\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder, \xe2\x80\x9cyou don\xe2\x80\x99t mean to say honestly that everybody works\r\nwith his hands in Altruria?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, certainly. We are mindful, as a whole people, of the divine law--\xe2\x80\x98In\r\nthe sweat of thy brow shalt thou eat bread.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the capitalists? I\xe2\x80\x99m anxious about Number One, you see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have none.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI forgot, of course. But the lawyers, the doctors, the parsons, the\r\nnovelists?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey all do their share of hand-work.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lawyer said: \xe2\x80\x9cThat seems to dispose of the question of the working-man\r\nin society. But how about your minds? When do you cultivate your minds?\r\nWhen do the ladies of Altruria cultivate their minds, if they have to do\r\ntheir own work, as I suppose they do? Or is it only the men who work, if\r\nthey happen to be the husbands and fathers of the upper classes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian seemed to be sensible of the kindly scepticism which\r\npersisted in our reception of his statements, after all we had read of\r\nAltruria. He smiled indulgently, and said: \xe2\x80\x9cYou mustn\xe2\x80\x99t imagine that work\r\nin Altruria is the same as it is here. As we all work, the amount that\r\neach one need do is very little, a few hours each day at the most, so that\r\nevery man and woman has abundant leisure and perfect spirits for the\r\nhigher pleasures which the education of their whole youth has fitted them\r\nto enjoy. If you can understand a state of things where the sciences and\r\narts and letters are cultivated for their own sake, and not as a means of\r\nlivelihood--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lawyer, smiling, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid we can\xe2\x80\x99t conceive of that. We\r\nconsider the pinch of poverty the highest incentive that a man can have.\r\nIf our gifted friend here,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, indicating me, \xe2\x80\x9cwere not kept like a\r\ntoad under the harrow, with his nose on the grindstone, and the poorhouse\r\nstaring him in the face--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s sake,\xe2\x80\x9d I cried out, \xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t mix your metaphors so, anyway!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it were not for that and all the other hardships that literary men\r\nundergo--\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x98Toil, envy, want, the patron and the jail\xe2\x80\x99--\r\n\r\nhis novels probably wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be worth reading.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, as if he did not quite follow this joking; and,\r\nto tell the truth, I never find the personal thing in very good taste.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will understand, then, how extremely difficult it is for me to\r\nimagine a condition of things like yours--although I have it under my very\r\neyes--where the money consideration is the first consideration.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, excuse me,\xe2\x80\x9d urged the minister; \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think that\xe2\x80\x99s quite the\r\ncase.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg your pardon,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, sweetly; \xe2\x80\x9cyou can see how easily\r\nI go astray.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d the banker interposed, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you are so far out in\r\nwhat you say. If you had said that money was always the first motive, I\r\nshould have been inclined to dispute you, too; but when you say that money\r\nis the first consideration, I think you are quite right. Unless a man\r\nsecures his financial basis for his work, he can\xe2\x80\x99t do his work. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnonsense to pretend otherwise. So the money consideration is the first\r\nconsideration. People here have to live by their work, and to live they\r\nmust have money. Of course, we all recognize a difference in the\r\nqualities, as well as in the kinds, of work. The work of the laborer may\r\nbe roughly defined as the necessity of his life; the work of the business\r\nman as the means, and the work of the artist and scientist as the end.\r\nWe might refine upon these definitions and make them closer, but they\r\nwill serve for illustration as they are. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think there can be\r\nany question as to which is the highest kind of work; some truths are\r\nself-evident. He is a fortunate man whose work is an end, and every\r\nbusiness man sees this, and owns it to himself, at least when he meets\r\nsome man of an aesthetic or scientific occupation. He knows that this\r\nluckier fellow has a joy in his work which he can never feel in business;\r\nthat his success in it can never be embittered by the thought that it is\r\nthe failure of another; that if he does it well, it is pure good; that\r\nthere cannot be any competition in it--there can be only a noble\r\nemulation, as far as the work itself is concerned. He can always look up\r\nto his work, for it is something above him; and a business man often has\r\nto look down upon his business, for it is often beneath him, unless he is\r\na pretty low fellow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI listened to all this in surprise; I knew that the banker was a\r\ncultivated man, a man of university training, and that he was a reader and\r\na thinker; but he had always kept a certain reserve in his talk, which he\r\nnow seemed to have thrown aside for the sake of the Altrurian, or because\r\nthe subject had a charm that lured him out of himself. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, now,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ncontinued, \xe2\x80\x9cthe question is of the money consideration, which is the first\r\nconsideration with us all: does it, or doesn\xe2\x80\x99t it degrade the work, which\r\nis the life, of those among us whose work is the highest? I understand\r\nthat this is the misgiving which troubles you in view of our conditions?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian assented, and I thought it a proof of the banker\xe2\x80\x99s innate\r\ndelicacy that he did not refer the matter, so far as it concerned the\r\naesthetic life and work, to me; I was afraid he was going to do so. But he\r\ncourteously proposed to keep the question impersonal, and he went on to\r\nconsider it himself: \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose any one can satisfy you fully.\r\nBut I should say that it put such men under a double strain, and perhaps\r\nthat is the reason why so many of them break down in a calling that is\r\ncertainly far less exhausting than business. On one side, the artist is\r\nkept to the level of the working-man, of the animal, of the creature whose\r\nsole affair is to get something to eat and somewhere to sleep. This is\r\nthrough his necessity. On the other side, he is exalted to the height of\r\nbeings who have no concern but with the excellence of their work, which\r\nthey were born and divinely authorized to do. This is through his purpose.\r\nBetween the two, I should say that he got mixed, and that his work shows\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNone of the others said anything, and, since I had not been personally\r\nappealed to, I felt the freer to speak. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you will suppose me to be\r\nspeaking from observation rather than experience--\xe2\x80\x9d I began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy all means,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker, \xe2\x80\x9cgo on;\xe2\x80\x9d and the rest made haste in\r\nvarious forms to yield me the word.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should say that such a man certainly got mixed, but that his work kept\r\nitself pure from the money consideration, as it were, in spite of him. A\r\npainter or actor, or even a novelist, is glad to get all he can for his\r\nwork, and, such is our fallen nature, he does get all he knows how to get:\r\nbut, when he has once fairly passed into his work, he loses himself in it.\r\nHe does not think whether it will pay or not, whether it will be popular\r\nor not, but whether he can make it good or not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that is conceivable,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker. \xe2\x80\x9cBut wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t he rather do\r\nsomething he would get less for, if he could afford it, than the thing he\r\nknows he will get more for? Doesn\xe2\x80\x99t the money consideration influence his\r\nchoice of subject?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOddly enough, I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe it does,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered, after a moment\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nreflection. \xe2\x80\x9cA man makes his choice once for all when he embraces the\r\naesthetic life, or, rather, it is made for him; no other life seems\r\npossible. I know there is a general belief that an artist does the kind of\r\nthing he has made go because it pays; but this only shows the prevalence\r\nof business ideals. If he did not love to do the thing he does, he could\r\nnot do it well, no matter how richly it paid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad to hear it,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker, and he added to the Altrurian:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo, you see, we are not so bad as one would think. We are illogically\r\nbetter, in fact.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d the other assented. \xe2\x80\x9cI knew something of your literature as well as\r\nyour conditions before I left home, and I perceived that by some anomaly\r\nthe one was not tainted by the other. It is a miraculous proof of the\r\ndivine mission of the poet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the popular novelist,\xe2\x80\x9d the lawyer whispered in my ear, but loud\r\nenough for the rest to hear, and they all testified their amusement at my\r\ncost.\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian, with his weak sense of humor, passed the joke. \xe2\x80\x9cIt shows no\r\nsigns of corruption from greed, but I can\xe2\x80\x99t help thinking that, fine as it\r\nis, it might have been much finer if the authors who produced it had been\r\nabsolutely freed to their work, and had never felt the spur of need.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre they absolutely freed to it in Altruria?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the professor. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nunderstood you that everybody had to work for his living in Altruria.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a mistake. Nobody works for his living in Altruria; he works for\r\nothers\xe2\x80\x99 living.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, that is precisely what our working-men object to doing here,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nthe manufacturer. \xe2\x80\x9cIn that last interview of mine with the walking\r\ndelegate he had the impudence to ask me why my men should work for my\r\nliving as well as their own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe couldn\xe2\x80\x99t imagine that you were giving them the work to do--the very\r\nmeans of life,\xe2\x80\x9d said the professor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, that\xe2\x80\x99s the last thing those fellows want to think of.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps,\xe2\x80\x9d the Altrurian suggested, \xe2\x80\x9cthey might not have found it such a\r\nhardship to work for your living if their own had been assured, as it is\r\nwith us. If you will excuse my saying it, we should think it monstrous in\r\nAltruria for any man to have another\xe2\x80\x99s means of life in his power; and in\r\nour condition it is hardly imaginable. Do you really have it in your power\r\nto take away a man\xe2\x80\x99s opportunity to earn a living?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe manufacturer laughed uneasily. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is in my power to take away his\r\nlife; but I don\xe2\x80\x99t habitually shoot my fellow-men, and I never dismissed a\r\nman yet without good reason.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I beg your pardon,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t dream of accusing\r\nyou of such inhumanity. But, you see, our whole system is so very\r\ndifferent that, as I said, it is hard for me to conceive of yours, and I\r\nam very curious to understand its workings. If you shot your fellow-man,\r\nas you say, the law would punish you; but if, for some reason that you\r\ndecided to be good, you took away his means of living, and he actually\r\nstarved to death--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen the law would have nothing to do with it,\xe2\x80\x9d the professor replied for\r\nthe manufacturer, who did not seem ready to answer. \xe2\x80\x9cBut that is not the\r\nway things fall out. The man would be supported in idleness, probably,\r\ntill he got another job, by his union, which would take the matter up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I thought that our friend did not employ union labor,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the\r\nAltrurian.\r\n\r\nI found all this very uncomfortable, and tried to turn the talk back to a\r\npoint that I felt curious about: \xe2\x80\x9cBut in Altruria, if the literary class\r\nis not exempt from the rule of manual labor, where do they find time and\r\nstrength to write?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you must realize that our manual labor is never engrossing or\r\nexhausting. It is no more than is necessary to keep the body in health. I\r\ndo not see how you remain well here, you people of sedentary occupations.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, we all take some sort of exercise. We walk several hours a day, or we\r\nrow, or we ride a bicycle, or a horse, or we fence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut to us,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the Altrurian, with a growing frankness which\r\nnothing but the sweetness of his manner would have excused, \xe2\x80\x9cexercise for\r\nexercise would appear stupid. The barren expenditure of force that began\r\nand ended in itself, and produced nothing, we should--if you will excuse\r\nmy saying so--look upon as childish, if not insane or immoral.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nV\r\n\r\nAt this moment the lady who had hailed me so gayly from the top of the\r\ncoach while I stood waiting for the Altrurian to help the porter with the\r\nbaggage, just after the arrival of the train, came up with her husband to\r\nour little group and said to me: \xe2\x80\x9cI want to introduce my husband to you.\r\nHe adores your books.\xe2\x80\x9d She went on much longer to this effect, while the\r\nother men grinned round and her husband tried to look as if it were all\r\ntrue, and her eyes wandered to the Altrurian, who listened gravely. I knew\r\nperfectly well that she was using her husband\xe2\x80\x99s zeal for my fiction to\r\nmake me present my friend; but I did not mind that, and I introduced him\r\nto both of them. She took possession of him at once and began walking him\r\noff down the piazza, while her husband remained with me, and the members\r\nof our late conference drifted apart. I was not sorry to have it broken up\r\nfor the present; it seemed to me that it had lasted quite long enough, and\r\nI lighted a cigar with the husband, and we strolled together in the\r\ndirection his wife had taken.\r\n\r\nHe began, apparently in compliment to literature in my person: \xe2\x80\x9cYes, I\r\nlike to have a book where I can get at it when we\xe2\x80\x99re not going out to the\r\ntheatre, and I want to quiet my mind down after business. I don\xe2\x80\x99t care\r\nmuch what the book is; my wife reads to me till I drop off, and then she\r\nfinishes the book herself and tells me the rest of the story. You see,\r\nbusiness takes it out of you so! Well, I let my wife do most of the\r\nreading, anyway. She knows pretty much everything that\xe2\x80\x99s going in that\r\nline. We haven\xe2\x80\x99t got any children, and it occupies her mind. She\xe2\x80\x99s up to\r\nall sorts of things--she\xe2\x80\x99s artistic, and she\xe2\x80\x99s musical, and she\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndramatic, and she\xe2\x80\x99s literary. Well, I like to have her. Women are funny,\r\nanyway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was a good-looking, good-natured, average American of the money-making\r\ntype; I believe he was some sort of a broker, but I do not quite know what\r\nhis business was. As we walked up and down the piazza, keeping a discreet\r\nlittle distance from the corner where his wife had run off to with her\r\ncapture, he said he wished he could get more time with her in the\r\nsummer--but he supposed I knew what business was. He was glad she could\r\nhave the rest, anyway; she needed it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy-the-way,\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, \xe2\x80\x9cwho is this friend of yours? The women are all\r\ncrazy about him, and it\xe2\x80\x99s been an even thing between my wife and Miss\r\nGroundsel which would fetch him first. But I\xe2\x80\x99ll bet on my wife every time,\r\nwhen it comes to a thing like that. He\xe2\x80\x99s a good-looking fellow--some kind\r\nof foreigner, I believe; pretty eccentric, too, I guess. Where is\r\nAltruria, anyway?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI told him, and he said: \xe2\x80\x9cOh yes. Well, if we are going to restrict\r\nimmigration, I suppose we sha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t see many more Altrurians, and we\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nbetter make the most of this one. Heigh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI do not know why this innocent pleasantry piqued me to say: \xe2\x80\x9cIf I\r\nunderstand the Altrurians, my dear fellow, nothing could induce them to\r\nemigrate to America. As far as I can make out, they would regard it very\r\nmuch as we should regard settling among the Eskimos.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that so?\xe2\x80\x9d asked my new acquaintance, with perfect good temper. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally, I can\xe2\x80\x99t say, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I\xe2\x80\x99ve explicit authority for my\r\nstatement.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are worse than the English used to be,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nthat there were any foreigners who looked at us in that light now. I\r\nthought the war settled all that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI sighed. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are a good many things that the war didn\xe2\x80\x99t settle so\r\ndefinitely as we\xe2\x80\x99ve been used to thinking, I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid. But, for that\r\nmatter, I fancy an Altrurian would regard the English as a little lower in\r\nthe scale of savagery than ourselves even.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that so? Well, that\xe2\x80\x99s pretty good on the English, anyway,\xe2\x80\x9d said my\r\ncompanion, and he laughed with an easy satisfaction that I envied him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear!\xe2\x80\x9d his wife called to him from where she was sitting with the\r\nAltrurian, \xe2\x80\x9cI wish you would go for my shawl. I begin to feel the air a\r\nlittle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll go if you\xe2\x80\x99ll tell me where,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, and he confided to me, \xe2\x80\x9cNever\r\nknows where her shawl is one-quarter of the time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I think I left it in the office somewhere. You might ask at the\r\ndesk; or perhaps it\xe2\x80\x99s in the rack by the dining-room door--or maybe up in\r\nour room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought so,\xe2\x80\x9d said her husband, with another glance at me, as if it were\r\nthe greatest fun in the world, and he started amiably off.\r\n\r\nI went and took a chair by the lady and the Altrurian, and she began at\r\nonce: \xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m so glad you\xe2\x80\x99ve come! I have been trying to enlighten Mr.\r\nHomos about some of the little social peculiarities among us that he finds\r\nso hard to understand. He was just now,\xe2\x80\x9d the lady continued, \xe2\x80\x9cwanting to\r\nknow why all the natives out here were not invited to go in and join our\r\nyoung people in the dance, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve been trying to tell him that we\r\nconsider it a great favor to let them come and take up so much of the\r\npiazza and look in at the windows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe gave a little laugh of superiority, and twitched her pretty head in\r\nthe direction of the young country girls and country fellows who were\r\nthronging the place that night in rather unusual numbers. They were well\r\nenough looking, and, as it was Saturday night, they were in their best. I\r\nsuppose their dress could have been criticised; the young fellows were\r\nclothed by the ready-made clothing-store, and the young girls after their\r\nown devices from the fashion papers; but their general effect was good,\r\nand their behavior was irreproachable; they were very quiet--if anything,\r\ntoo quiet. They took up a part of the piazza that was yielded them by\r\ncommon usage, and sat watching the hop inside, not so much enviously, I\r\nthought, as wistfully; and for the first time it struck me as odd that\r\nthey should have no part in the gayety. I had often seen them there\r\nbefore, but I had never thought it strange they should be shut out. It had\r\nalways seemed quite normal, but now, suddenly, for one baleful moment, it\r\nseemed abnormal. I suppose it was the talk we had been having about the\r\nworking-men in society which caused me to see the thing as the Altrurian\r\nmust have seen it; but I was, nevertheless, vexed with him for having\r\nasked such a question, after he had been so fully instructed upon the\r\npoint. It was malicious of him, or it was stupid. I hardened my heart, and\r\nanswered: \xe2\x80\x9cYou might have told him, for one thing, that they were not\r\ndancing because they had not paid the piper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen the money consideration enters even into your social pleasures?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n asked the Altrurian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery much. Doesn\xe2\x80\x99t it with you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe evaded this question, as he evaded all straightforward questions\r\nconcerning his country: \xe2\x80\x9cWe have no money consideration, you know. But do\r\nI understand that all your social entertainments are paid for by the\r\nguests?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, not so bad as that, quite. There are a great many that the host\r\npays for. Even here, in a hotel, the host furnishes the music and the room\r\nfree to the guests of the house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd none are admitted from the outside?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, people are welcome from all the other hotels and boarding-houses\r\nand the private cottages. The young men are especially welcome; there are\r\nnot enough young men in the hotel to go round, you see.\xe2\x80\x9d In fact, we could\r\nsee that some of the pretty girls within were dancing with other girls;\r\nhalf-grown boys were dangling from the waists of tall young ladies and\r\nwaltzing on tiptoe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t that rather droll?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Altrurian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s grotesque!\xe2\x80\x9d I said, and I felt ashamed of it. \xe2\x80\x9cBut what are you to\r\ndo? The young men are hard at work in the cities, as many as can get work\r\nthere, and the rest are out West, growing up with the country. There are\r\ntwenty young girls for every young man at all the summer resorts in the\r\nEast.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what would happen if these young farmers--I suppose they are\r\nfarmers--were invited in to take part in the dance?\xe2\x80\x9d asked my friend.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that is impossible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally, Mrs. Makely, I think I shall have to give him back to you,\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\nThe lady laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cI am not sure that I want him back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes,\xe2\x80\x9d the Altrurian entreated, with unwonted perception of the humor.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know that I must be very trying with my questions; but do not abandon\r\nme to the solitude of my own conjectures. They are dreadful!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I won\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lady, with another laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I will try to\r\ntell you what would happen if those farmers, or farm-hands, or whatever\r\nthey are, were asked in. The mammas would be very indignant, and the young\r\nladies would be scared, and nobody would know what to do, and the dance\r\nwould stop.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen the young ladies prefer to dance with one another and with little\r\nboys--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, they prefer to dance with young men of their own station; they would\r\nrather not dance at all than dance with people beneath them. I don\xe2\x80\x99t say\r\nanything against these natives here; they are very civil and decent. But\r\nthey have not the same social traditions as the young ladies; they would\r\nbe out of place with them, and they would feel it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I can see that they are not fit to associate with them,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nAltrurian, with a gleam of commonsense that surprised me, \xe2\x80\x9cand that as\r\nlong as your present conditions endure they never can be. You must excuse\r\nthe confusion which the difference between your political ideals and your\r\neconomic ideals constantly creates in me. I always think of you\r\npolitically first, and realize you as a perfect democracy; then come these\r\nother facts, in which I cannot perceive that you differ from the\r\naristocratic countries of Europe in theory, or practice. It is very\r\npuzzling. Am I right in supposing that the effect of your economy is to\r\nestablish insuperable inequalities among you, and to forbid the hope of\r\nthe brotherhood which your policy proclaims?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Makely looked at me as if she were helpless to grapple with his\r\nmeaning, and, for fear of worse, I thought best to evade it. I said: \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t believe that anybody is troubled by those distinctions. We are used\r\nto them, and everybody acquiesces in them, which is a proof that they are\r\na very good thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Makely now came to my support. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Americans are very high-spirited,\r\nin every class, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe one of those nice farm-boys would like\r\nbeing asked in any better than the young ladies. You can\xe2\x80\x99t imagine how\r\nproud some of them are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo that they suffer from being excluded as inferiors?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I assure you they don\xe2\x80\x99t feel themselves inferior! They consider\r\nthemselves as good as anybody. There are some very interesting characters\r\namong them. Now, there is a young girl sitting at the first window, with\r\nher profile outlined by the light, whom I feel it an honor to speak to.\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s her brother, standing there with her--that tall, gaunt young man\r\nwith a Roman face; it\xe2\x80\x99s such a common type here in the mountains. Their\r\nfather was a soldier, and he distinguished himself so in one of the last\r\nbattles that he was promoted. He was badly wounded, but he never took a\r\npension; he just came back to his farm and worked on till he died. Now the\r\nson has the farm, and he and his sister live there with their mother. The\r\ndaughter takes in sewing, and in that way they manage to make both ends\r\nmeet. The girl is really a first-rate seamstress, and so cheap! I give her\r\na good deal of my work in the summer, and we are quite friends. She\xe2\x80\x99s very\r\nfond of reading; the mother is an invalid, but she reads aloud while the\r\ndaughter sews, and you\xe2\x80\x99ve no idea how many books they get through. When\r\nshe comes for sewing, I like to talk with her about them; I always have\r\nher sit down; it\xe2\x80\x99s hard to realize that she isn\xe2\x80\x99t a lady. I\xe2\x80\x99m a good deal\r\ncriticised, I know, and I suppose I do spoil her a little; it puts notions\r\ninto such people\xe2\x80\x99s heads, if you meet them in that way; they\xe2\x80\x99re pretty\r\nfree and independent as it is. But when I\xe2\x80\x99m with Lizzie I forget that\r\nthere is any difference between us; I can\xe2\x80\x99t help loving the child. You\r\nmust take Mr. Homos to see them, Mr. Twelvemough. They\xe2\x80\x99ve got the father\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsword hung up over the head of the mother\xe2\x80\x99s bed; it\xe2\x80\x99s very touching. But\r\nthe poor little place is so bare!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Makely sighed, and there fell a little pause, which she broke with a\r\nquestion she had the effect of having kept back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is one thing I should like to ask you, too, Mr. Homos. Is it true\r\nthat everybody in Altruria does some kind of manual labor?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, certainly,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, quite as if he had been an American.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLadies, too? Or perhaps you have none.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI thought this rather offensive, but I could not see that the Altrurian\r\nhad taken it ill. \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps we had better try to understand each other\r\nclearly before I answer that question. You have no titles of nobility as\r\nthey have in England--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, indeed! I hope we have outgrown those superstitions,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs.\r\nMakely, with a republican fervor that did my heart good. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is a word\r\nthat we apply first of all to the moral qualities of a person.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you said just now that you sometimes forgot that your seamstress was\r\nnot a lady. Just what did you mean by that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Makely hesitated. \xe2\x80\x9cI meant--I suppose I meant--that she had not the\r\nsurroundings of a lady; the social traditions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen it has something to do with social as well as moral qualities--with\r\nranks and classes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClasses, yes; but, as you know, we have no ranks in America.\xe2\x80\x9d The\r\nAltrurian took off his hat and rubbed an imaginable perspiration from his\r\nforehead. He sighed deeply. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is all very difficult.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Makely assented, \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose it is. All foreigners find it so.\r\nIn fact, it is something that you have to live into the notion of; it\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t be explained.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, my dear madam, will you tell me without further question what\r\nyou understand by a lady, and let me live into the notion of it at my\r\nleisure?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will do my best,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it would be so much easier to\r\ntell you _who_ was or who was not a lady. However, your acquaintance is so\r\nlimited yet that I must try to do something in the abstract and impersonal\r\nfor you. In the first place, a lady must be above the sordid anxieties in\r\nevery way. She need not be very rich, but she must have enough, so that\r\nshe need not be harassed about making both ends meet, when she ought to be\r\ndevoting herself to her social duties. The time is past with us when a\r\nlady could look after the dinner, and perhaps cook part of it herself, and\r\nthen rush in to receive her guests and do the amenities. She must have a\r\ncertain kind of house, so that her entourage won\xe2\x80\x99t seem cramped and mean,\r\nand she must have nice frocks, of course, and plenty of them. She needn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbe of the smart set; that isn\xe2\x80\x99t at all necessary; but she can\xe2\x80\x99t afford to\r\nbe out of the fashion. Of course, she must have a certain training. She\r\nmust have cultivated tastes; she must know about art and literature and\r\nmusic, and all those kind of things, and, though it isn\xe2\x80\x99t necessary to go\r\nin for anything in particular, it won\xe2\x80\x99t hurt her to have a fad or two. The\r\nnicest kind of fad is charity; and people go in for that a great deal. I\r\nthink sometimes they use it to work up with, and there are some who use\r\nreligion in the same way; I think it\xe2\x80\x99s horrid; but it\xe2\x80\x99s perfectly safe;\r\nyou can\xe2\x80\x99t accuse them of doing it. I\xe2\x80\x99m happy to say, though, that mere\r\nchurch association doesn\xe2\x80\x99t count socially so much as it used to. Charity\r\nis a great deal more insidious. But you see how hard it is to define\r\na lady. So much has to be left to the nerves, in all these things. And\r\nthen it\xe2\x80\x99s changing all the time; Europe\xe2\x80\x99s coming in, and the old American\r\nideals are passing away. Things that people did ten years ago would be\r\nimpossible now, or at least ridiculous. You wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be considered vulgar,\r\nquite, but you would certainly be considered a back number, and that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nalmost as bad. Really,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe I can tell you\r\nwhat a lady is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe all laughed together at her frank confession. The Altrurian asked: \xe2\x80\x9cBut\r\ndo I understand that one of her conditions is that she shall have nothing\r\nwhatever to do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing to _do_!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mrs. Makely. \xe2\x80\x9cA lady is busy from morning till\r\nnight. She always goes to bed perfectly worn out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut with what?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Altrurian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith making herself agreeable and her house attractive, with going to\r\nlunches and teas and dinners and concerts and theatres and art\r\nexhibitions, and charity meetings and receptions, and with writing a\r\nthousand and one notes about them, and accepting and declining, and giving\r\nlunches and dinners, and making calls and receiving them, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nwhat all. It\xe2\x80\x99s the most hideous slavery!\xe2\x80\x9d Her voice rose into something\r\nlike a shriek; one could see that her nerves were going at the mere\r\nthought of it all. \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t have a moment to yourself; your life isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyour own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the lady isn\xe2\x80\x99t allowed to do any useful kind of work?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Work_! Don\xe2\x80\x99t you call all that work, and _useful_? I\xe2\x80\x99m sure I envy the\r\ncook in my kitchen at times; I envy the woman that scrubs my floors. Stop!\r\nDon\xe2\x80\x99t ask why I don\xe2\x80\x99t go into my kitchen, or get down on my knees with the\r\nmop. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t possible. You simply can\xe2\x80\x99t. Perhaps you could if you were\r\nvery _grande dame_, but if you\xe2\x80\x99re anywhere near the line of necessity, or\r\never have been, you can\xe2\x80\x99t. Besides, if we did do our own household work,\r\nas I understand your Altrurian ladies do, what would become of the servant\r\nclass? We should be taking away their living, and that would be wicked.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would certainly be wrong to take away the living of a\r\nfellow-creature,\xe2\x80\x9d the Altrurian gravely admitted, \xe2\x80\x9cand I see the obstacle\r\nin your way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a mountain,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lady, with exhaustion in her voice, but a\r\nreturning amiability; his forbearance must have placated her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay I ask what the use of your society life is?\xe2\x80\x9d he ventured, after a\r\nmoment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUse? Why should it have any? It kills time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you are shut up to a hideous slavery without use, except to kill\r\ntime, and you cannot escape from it without taking away the living of\r\nthose dependent on you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d I put in, \xe2\x80\x9cand that is a difficulty that meets us at every turn. It\r\nis something that Matthew Arnold urged with great effect in his paper on\r\nthat crank of a Tolstoy. He asked what would become of the people who need\r\nthe work if we served and waited on ourselves, as Tolstoy preached. The\r\nquestion is unanswerable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is true; in your conditions, it is unanswerable,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nAltrurian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, \xe2\x80\x9cthat, under the circumstances, we do pretty\r\nwell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t presume to censure you. And if you believe that your\r\nconditions are the best--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe believe them the best in the best of all possible worlds,\xe2\x80\x9d I said,\r\ndevoutly; and it struck me that, if ever we came to have a national\r\nchurch, some such affirmation as that concerning our economical conditions\r\nought to be in the confession of faith.\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian\xe2\x80\x99s mind had not followed mine so far. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd your young girls,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n he asked of Mrs. Makely--\xe2\x80\x9chow is their time occupied?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean after they come out in society?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe seemed to reflect. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know that it is very differently occupied.\r\nOf course, they have their own amusements; they have their dances, and\r\nlittle clubs, and their sewing-societies. I suppose that even an Altrurian\r\nwould applaud their sewing for the poor?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Makely asked, rather\r\nsatirically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered; and then he asked: \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it taking work away from\r\nsome needy seamstress, though? But I suppose you excuse it to the\r\nthoughtlessness of youth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Makely did not say, and he went on: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat I find it so hard to\r\nunderstand is how you ladies can endure a life of mere nervous exertion,\r\nsuch as you have been describing to me. I don\xe2\x80\x99t see how you keep well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe _don\xe2\x80\x99t_ keep well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, with the greatest amusement. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t suppose that when you get above the working classes, till you reach\r\nthe very rich, you would find a perfectly well woman in America.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t that rather extreme?\xe2\x80\x9d I ventured to ask.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s shamefully moderate,\xe2\x80\x9d and she seemed to\r\ndelight in having made out such a bad case for her sex. You can\xe2\x80\x99t stop a\r\nwoman of that kind when she gets started; I had better left it alone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, \xe2\x80\x9cif you are forbidden by motives of humanity\r\nfrom doing any sort of manual labor, which you must leave to those who\r\nlive by it, I suppose you take some sort of exercise?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, shaking her head gayly, \xe2\x80\x9cwe prefer to take\r\nmedicine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must approve of that,\xe2\x80\x9d I said to the Altrurian, \xe2\x80\x9cas you consider\r\nexercise for its own sake insane or immoral. But, Mrs. Makely,\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\nentreated, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\xe2\x80\x99re giving me away at a tremendous rate. I have just been\r\ntelling Mr. Homos that you ladies go in for athletics so much now in your\r\nsummer outings that there is danger of your becoming physically as well as\r\nintellectually superior to us poor fellows. Don\xe2\x80\x99t take that consolation\r\nfrom me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t, altogether,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t have the heart to, after the\r\npretty way you\xe2\x80\x99ve put it. I don\xe2\x80\x99t call it very athletic, sitting around on\r\nhotel piazzas all summer long, as nineteen-twentieths of us do. But I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t deny that there is a Remnant, as Matthew Arnold calls them, who do\r\ngo in for tennis and boating and bathing and tramping and climbing.\xe2\x80\x9d She\r\npaused, and then she concluded, gleefully: \xe2\x80\x9cAnd you ought to see what\r\nwrecks they get home in the fall!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe joke was on me; I could not help laughing, though I felt rather\r\nsheepish before the Altrurian. Fortunately, he did not pursue the inquiry;\r\nhis curiosity had been given a slant aside from it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut your ladies,\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, \xe2\x80\x9cthey have the summer for rest, however they\r\nuse it. Do they generally leave town? I understood Mr. Twelvemough to say\r\nso,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, with a deferential glance at me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you may say it is the universal custom in the class that can afford\r\nit,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely. She proceeded as if she felt a tacit censure in his\r\nquestion. \xe2\x80\x9cIt wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be the least use for us to stay and fry through our\r\nsummers in the city simply because our fathers and brothers had to.\r\nBesides, we are worn out, at the end of the season, and they want us to\r\ncome away as much as we want to come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, I have always heard that the Americans are beautiful in their\r\nattitude toward women.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are perfect dears,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, \xe2\x80\x9cand here comes one of the\r\nbest of them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt that moment her husband came up and laid her shawl across her\r\nshoulders. \xe2\x80\x9cWhose character is it you\xe2\x80\x99re blasting?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, jocosely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere in the world did you find it?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, meaning the shawl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was where you left it--on the sofa, in the side parlor. I had to take\r\nmy life in my hand when I crossed among all those waltzers in there. There\r\nmust have been as many as three couples on the floor. Poor girls! I pity\r\nthem, off at these places. The fellows in town have a good deal better\r\ntime. They\xe2\x80\x99ve got their clubs, and they\xe2\x80\x99ve got the theatre, and when the\r\nweather gets too much for them they can run off down to the shore for the\r\nnight. The places anywhere within an hour\xe2\x80\x99s ride are full of fellows. The\r\ngirls don\xe2\x80\x99t have to dance with one another there, or with little boys. Of\r\ncourse, that\xe2\x80\x99s all right if they like it better.\xe2\x80\x9d He laughed at his wife,\r\nand winked at me, and smoked swiftly, in emphasis of his irony.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen the young gentlemen whom the young ladies here usually meet in\r\nsociety are all at work in the cities?\xe2\x80\x9d the Altrurian asked him, rather\r\nneedlessly, as I had already said so.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, those who are not out West, growing up with the country, except, of\r\ncourse, the fellows who have inherited a fortune. They\xe2\x80\x99re mostly off on\r\nyachts.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why do your young men go West to grow up with the country?\xe2\x80\x9d pursued\r\nmy friend.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause the East is _grown_ up. They have got to hustle, and the West is\r\nthe place to hustle. To make money,\xe2\x80\x9d added Makely, in response to a\r\npuzzled glance of the Altrurian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSometimes,\xe2\x80\x9d said his wife, \xe2\x80\x9cI almost hate the name of money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, so long as you don\xe2\x80\x99t hate the thing, Peggy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, we must have it, I suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d she sighed. \xe2\x80\x9cThey used to say about the\r\ngirls who grew into old maids just after the Rebellion that they had lost\r\ntheir chance in the war for the Union. I think quite as many lose their\r\nchance now in the war for the dollar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMars hath slain his thousands, but Mammon hath slain his tens of\r\nthousands,\xe2\x80\x9d I suggested, lightly; we all like to recognize the facts, so\r\nlong as we are not expected to do anything about them; then, we deny them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, quite as bad as that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, my dear, you are expensive, you know,\xe2\x80\x9d said her husband, \xe2\x80\x9cand if we\r\nwant to have you--why, we\xe2\x80\x99ve got to hustle first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t blame you, you poor things! There\xe2\x80\x99s nothing to be done about\r\nit; it\xe2\x80\x99s just got to go on and on; I don\xe2\x80\x99t see how it\xe2\x80\x99s ever to end.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian had been following us with that air of polite mystification\r\nwhich I had begun to dread in him. \xe2\x80\x9cThen, in your good society you\r\npostpone, and even forego, the happiness of life in the struggle to be\r\nrich?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you see,\xe2\x80\x9d said Makely, \xe2\x80\x9ca fellow don\xe2\x80\x99t like to ask a girl to share\r\na home that isn\xe2\x80\x99t as nice as the home she has left.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSometimes,\xe2\x80\x9d his wife put in, rather sadly, \xe2\x80\x9cI think that it\xe2\x80\x99s all a\r\nmistake, and that we\xe2\x80\x99d be willing to share the privations of the man we\r\nloved.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Makely, with a laugh, \xe2\x80\x9cwe wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like to risk it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI laughed with him, but his wife did not, and in the silence that ensued\r\nthere was nothing to prevent the Altrurian from coming in with another of\r\nhis questions: \xe2\x80\x9cHow far does this state of things extend downward? Does it\r\ninclude the working classes, too?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no!\xe2\x80\x9d we all answered together, and Mrs. Makely said: \xe2\x80\x9cWith your\r\nAltrurian ideas, I suppose you would naturally sympathize a great deal\r\nmore with the lower classes, and think they had to endure all the\r\nhardships in our system; but if you could realize how the struggle goes on\r\nin the best society, and how we all have to fight for what we get, or\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t get, you would be disposed to pity our upper classes, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure I should,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian.\r\n\r\nMakely remarked: \xe2\x80\x9cI used to hear my father say that slavery was harder on\r\nthe whites than it was on the blacks, and that he wanted it done away with\r\nfor the sake of the masters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMakely rather faltered in conclusion, as if he were not quite satisfied\r\nwith his remark, and I distinctly felt a want of proportion in it; but I\r\ndid not wish to say anything. His wife had no reluctance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, there\xe2\x80\x99s no comparison between the two things, but the struggle\r\ncertainly doesn\xe2\x80\x99t affect the working classes as it does us. They go on\r\nmarrying and giving in marriage in the old way. They have nothing to lose,\r\nand so they can afford it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlessed am dem what don\xe2\x80\x99t expect nuffin! Oh, I tell you, it\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nworking-man\xe2\x80\x99s country,\xe2\x80\x9d said Makely, through his cigar-smoke. \xe2\x80\x9cYou ought\r\nto see them in town, these summer nights, in the parks and squares and\r\ncheap theatres. Their girls are not off for their health, anywhere, and\r\ntheir fellows are not off growing up with the country. Their day\xe2\x80\x99s work is\r\nover, and they\xe2\x80\x99re going in for a good time. And, then, walk through the\r\nstreets where they live, and see them out on the stoops with their wives\r\nand children! I tell you, it\xe2\x80\x99s enough to make a fellow wish he was poor\r\nhimself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s astonishing how strong and well those women\r\nkeep, with their great families and their hard work. Sometimes I really\r\nenvy them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, \xe2\x80\x9cthat they are aware of the\r\nsacrifices which the ladies of the upper classes make in leaving all the\r\nwork to them, and suffering from the nervous debility which seems to be\r\nthe outcome of your society life?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have not the remotest idea of it. They have no conception of what a\r\nsociety woman goes through with. They think we do nothing. They envy us,\r\ntoo, and sometimes they\xe2\x80\x99re so ungrateful and indifferent, if you try to\r\nhelp them, or get on terms with them, that I believe they hate us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that comes from ignorance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, though I don\xe2\x80\x99t know that they are really any more ignorant of us\r\nthan we are of them. It\xe2\x80\x99s the other half on both sides.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t that a pity, rather?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course it\xe2\x80\x99s a pity, but what can you do? You can\xe2\x80\x99t know what people\r\nare like unless you live like them, and then the question is whether the\r\ngame is worth the candle. I should like to know how you manage in\r\nAltruria.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, we have solved the problem in the only way, as you say, that it can\r\nbe solved. We all live alike.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t that a little, just a very trifling little bit, monotonous?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs.\r\nMakely asked, with a smile. \xe2\x80\x9cBut there is everything, of course, in being\r\nused to it. To an unregenerate spirit--like mine, for example--it seems\r\nintolerable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why? When you were younger, before you were married, you all lived at\r\nhome together--or, perhaps, you were an only child?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no indeed! There were ten of us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you all lived alike, and shared equally?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but we were a family.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe do not conceive of the human race except as a family.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, excuse me, Mr. Homos, that is all nonsense. You cannot have the\r\nfamily feeling without love, and it is impossible to love other people.\r\nThat talk about the neighbor, and all that, is all well enough--\xe2\x80\x9d She\r\nstopped herself, as if she dimly remembered who began that talk, and then\r\nwent on: \xe2\x80\x9cOf course, I accept it as a matter of faith, and the spirit of\r\nit, nobody denies that; but what I mean is, that you must have frightful\r\nquarrels all the time.\xe2\x80\x9d She tried to look as if this were where she really\r\nmeant to bring up, and he took her on the ground she had chosen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, we have quarrels. Hadn\xe2\x80\x99t you at home?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe fought like little cats and dogs, at times.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMakely and I burst into a laugh at her magnanimous frankness. The\r\nAltrurian remained serious. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, because you lived alike, you knew each\r\nother, and so you easily made up your quarrels. It is quite as simple with\r\nus, in our life as a human family.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis notion of a human family seemed to amuse Mrs. Makely more and more;\r\nshe laughed and laughed again. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must excuse me,\xe2\x80\x9d she panted, at last,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut I cannot imagine it! No, it is too ludicrous. Just fancy the jars of\r\nan ordinary family multiplied by the population of a whole continent! Why,\r\nyou must be in a perpetual squabble. You can\xe2\x80\x99t have any peace of your\r\nlives. It\xe2\x80\x99s worse, far worse, than our way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, madam,\xe2\x80\x9d he began, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are supposing our family to be made up of\r\npeople with all the antagonistic interests of your civilization. As a\r\nmatter of fact--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no! _I know human nature_, Mr. Homos!\xe2\x80\x9d She suddenly jumped up and\r\ngave him her hand. \xe2\x80\x9cGood-night,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, sweetly, and as she drifted off\r\non her husband\xe2\x80\x99s arm she looked back at us and nodded in gay triumph.\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian turned upon me with unabated interest. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd have you no\r\nprovision in your system for finally making the lower classes understand\r\nthe sufferings and sacrifices of the upper classes in their behalf? Do you\r\nexpect to do nothing to bring them together in mutual kindness?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, not this evening,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, throwing the end of my cigar away. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m\r\ngoing to bed--aren\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, good-night. Are you sure you can find your room?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes. Good-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVI\r\n\r\nI left my guest abruptly, with a feeling of vexation not very easily\r\ndefinable. His repetition of questions about questions which society has\r\nso often answered, and always in the same way, was not so bad in him as it\r\nwould have been in a person of our civilization; he represented a wholly\r\ndifferent state of things, the inversion of our own, and much could be\r\nforgiven him for that reason, just as in Russia much could be forgiven to\r\nan American if he formulated his curiosity concerning imperialism from a\r\npurely republican experience. I knew that in Altruria, for instance, the\r\npossession of great gifts, of any kind of superiority, involved the sense\r\nof obligation to others, and the wish to identify one\xe2\x80\x99s self with the\r\ngreat mass of men, rather than the ambition to distinguish one\xe2\x80\x99s self from\r\nthem; and that the Altrurians honored their gifted men in the measure they\r\ndid this. A man reared in such a civilization must naturally find it\r\ndifficult to get our point of view; with social inclusion as the ideal, he\r\nwould with difficulty conceive of our ideal of social exclusion; but I\r\nthink we had all been very patient with him; we should have made short\r\nwork with an American who had approached us with the same inquiries.\r\nEven from a foreigner, the citizen of a republic founded on the notion,\r\nelsewhere exploded ever since Cain, that one is his brother\xe2\x80\x99s keeper,\r\nthe things he asked seemed inoffensive only because they were puerile;\r\nbut they certainly were puerile. I felt that it ought to have been\r\nself-evident to him that when a commonwealth of sixty million Americans\r\nbased itself upon the great principle of self-seeking, self-seeking was\r\nthe best thing, and, whatever hardship it seemed to work, it must carry\r\nwith it unseen blessings in tenfold measure. If a few hundred thousand\r\nfavored Americans enjoyed the privilege of socially contemning all the\r\nrest, it was as clearly right and just that they should do so as that four\r\nthousand American millionaires should be richer than all the other\r\nAmericans put together.
