Such a status, growing out of our political\r\nequality and our material prosperity, must evince a divine purpose to any\r\none intimate with the designs of Providence, and it seemed a kind of\r\nimpiety to doubt its perfection. I excused the misgivings which I could\r\nnot help seeing in the Altrurian to his alien traditions, and I was aware\r\nthat my friends had done so, too. But, if I could judge from myself, he\r\nmust have left them all sensible of their effort; and this was not\r\npleasant. I could not blink the fact that although I had openly disagreed\r\nwith him on every point of ethics and economics, I was still responsible\r\nfor him as a guest. It was as if an English gentleman had introduced a\r\nblatant American Democrat into Tory society; or, rather, as if a\r\nSoutherner of the olden time had harbored a Northern Abolitionist and\r\npermitted him to inquire into the workings of slavery among his neighbors.\r\nPeople would tolerate him as my guest for a time, but there must be an end\r\nof their patience with the tacit enmity of his sentiments and the explicit\r\nvulgarity of his ideals, and when the end came I must be attainted with\r\nhim.\r\n\r\nI did not like the notion of this, and I meant to escape it if I could. I\r\nconfess that I would have willingly disowned him, as I had already\r\ndisavowed his opinions, but there was no way of doing it short of telling\r\nhim to go away, and I was not ready to do that. Something in the man, I do\r\nnot know what, mysteriously appealed to me. He was not contemptibly\r\npuerile without being lovably childlike, and I could only make up my mind\r\nto be more and more frank with him and to try and shield him, as well as\r\nmyself, from the effects I dreaded.\r\n\r\nI fell asleep planning an excursion farther into the mountains, which\r\nshould take up the rest of the week that I expected him to stay with me,\r\nand would keep him from following up his studies of American life where\r\nthey would be so injurious to both of us as they must in our hotel. A\r\nknock at my door roused me, and I sent a drowsy \xe2\x80\x9cCome in!\xe2\x80\x9d toward it from\r\nthe bedclothes without looking that way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-morning!\xe2\x80\x9d came back in the rich, gentle voice of the Altrurian. I\r\nlifted my head with a jerk from the pillow, and saw him standing against\r\nthe closed door, with my shoes in his hand. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, I am sorry I waked you. I\r\nthought--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all, not at all,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s quite time, I dare say. But you\r\noughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to have taken the trouble to bring my shoes in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wasn\xe2\x80\x99t altogether disinterested in it,\xe2\x80\x9d he returned. \xe2\x80\x9cI wished you to\r\ncompliment me on them. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you think they are pretty well done, for an\r\namateur?\xe2\x80\x9d He came toward my bed, and turned them about in his hands, so\r\nthat they would catch the light, and smiled down upon me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand,\xe2\x80\x9d I began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI blacked them, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou blacked them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he returned, easily. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought I would go into the baggage-room,\r\nafter we parted last night, to look for a piece of mine that had not been\r\ntaken to my room, and I found the porter there, with his wrist bound up.\r\nHe said he had strained it in handling a lady\xe2\x80\x99s Saratoga--he said a\r\nSaratoga was a large trunk--and I begged him to let me relieve him at the\r\nboots he was blacking. He refused at first, but I insisted upon trying my\r\nhand at a pair, and then he let me go on with the men\xe2\x80\x99s boots; he said he\r\ncould varnish the ladies\xe2\x80\x99 without hurting his wrist. It needed less skill\r\nthan I supposed, and after I had done a few pairs he said I could black\r\nboots as well as he.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid anybody see you?\xe2\x80\x9d I gasped, and I felt a cold perspiration break out\r\non me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, we had the whole midnight hour to ourselves. The porter\xe2\x80\x99s work with\r\nthe baggage was all over, and there was nothing to interrupt the\r\ndelightful chat we fell into. He is a very intelligent man, and he told me\r\nall about that custom of feeing which you deprecate. He says that the\r\nservants hate it as much as the guests; they have to take the tips now\r\nbecause the landlords figure on them in the wages, and they cannot live\r\nwithout them. He is a fine, manly fellow, and--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Homos,\xe2\x80\x9d I broke in, with the strength I found in his assurance that\r\nno one had seen him helping the porter black boots, \xe2\x80\x9cI want to speak very\r\nseriously with you, and I hope you will not be hurt if I speak very\r\nplainly about a matter in which I have your good solely at heart.\xe2\x80\x9d This\r\nwas not quite true, and I winced inwardly a little when he thanked me with\r\nthat confounded sincerity of his which was so much like irony; but I went\r\non: \xe2\x80\x9cIt is my duty to you, as my guest, to tell you that this thing of\r\ndoing for others is not such a simple matter here, as your peculiar\r\ntraining leads you to think. You have been deceived by a superficial\r\nlikeness; but, really, I do not understand how you could have read all you\r\nhave done about us and not realize before coming here that America and\r\nAltruria are absolutely distinct and diverse in their actuating\r\nprinciples. They are both republics, I know; but America is a republic\r\nwhere every man is for himself, and you cannot help others as you do at\r\nhome; it is dangerous--it is ridiculous. You must keep this fact in mind,\r\nor you will fall into errors that will be very embarrassing to you in your\r\nstay among us, and,\xe2\x80\x9d I was forced to add, \xe2\x80\x9cto all your friends. Now, I\r\ncertainly hoped, after what I had said to you and what my friends had\r\nexplained of our civilization, that you would not have done a thing of\r\nthis kind. I will see the porter, as soon as I am up, and ask him not to\r\nmention the matter to any one, but, I confess, I don\xe2\x80\x99t like to take an\r\napologetic tone with him; your conditions are so alien to ours that they\r\nwill seem incredible to him, and he will think I am stuffing him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe he will think that,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, \xe2\x80\x9cand I hope you\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t find the case so bad as it seems to you. I am extremely sorry to\r\nhave done wrong--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, the thing wasn\xe2\x80\x99t wrong in itself. It was only wrong under the\r\ncircumstances. Abstractly, it is quite right to help a fellow-being who\r\nneeds help; no one denies that, even in a country where every one is for\r\nhimself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am so glad to hear it,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian. \xe2\x80\x9cThen, at least, I have not\r\ngone radically astray; and I do not think you need take the trouble to\r\nexplain the Altrurian ideas to the porter. I have done that already, and\r\nthey seemed quite conceivable to him; he said that poor folks had to act\r\nupon them, even here, more or less, and that if they did not act upon them\r\nthere would be no chance for them at all. He says they have to help one\r\nanother very much as we do at home, and that it is only the rich folks\r\namong you who are independent. I really don\xe2\x80\x99t think you need speak to him\r\nat all, unless you wish; and I was very careful to guard my offer of help\r\nat the point where I understood from you and your friends that it might do\r\nharm. I asked him if there was not some one who would help him out with\r\nhis boot-blacking for money, because in that case I should be glad to pay\r\nhim; but he said there was no one about who would take the job; that he\r\nhad to agree to black the boots, or else he would not have got the place\r\nof porter, but that all the rest of the help would consider it a disgrace,\r\nand would not help him for love or money. So it seemed quite safe to offer\r\nhim my services.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI felt that the matter was almost hopeless, but I asked: \xe2\x80\x9cAnd what he\r\nsaid--didn\xe2\x80\x99t that suggest anything else to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow anything else?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Altrurian, in his turn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t it occur to you that if none of his fellow-servants were willing\r\nto help him black boots, and if he did it only because he was obliged to,\r\nit was hardly the sort of work for you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, no,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, with absolute simplicity. He must have\r\nperceived the despair I fell into at this answer, for he asked: \xe2\x80\x9cWhy\r\nshould I have minded doing for others what I should have been willing to\r\ndo for myself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are a great many things we are willing to do for ourselves that we\r\nare not willing to do for others. But even on that principle, which I\r\nthink false and illogical, you could not be justified. A gentleman is not\r\nwilling to black _his own_ boots. It is offensive to his feelings, to his\r\nself-respect; it is something he will not do if he can get anybody else to\r\ndo it for him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen in America,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, \xe2\x80\x9cit is not offensive to the\r\nfeelings of a gentleman to let another do for him what he would not do for\r\nhimself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d he returned, \xe2\x80\x9cthen we understand something altogether different by\r\nthe word gentleman in Altruria. I see, now, how I have committed a\r\nmistake. I shall be more careful hereafter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI thought I had better leave the subject, and, \xe2\x80\x9cBy-the-way,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9chow\r\nwould you like to take a little tramp with me to-day farther up into the\r\nmountains?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should be delighted,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, so gratefully that I was\r\nashamed to think why I was proposing the pleasure to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, I shall be ready to start as soon as we have had breakfast. I\r\nwill join you down-stairs in half an hour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe left me at this hint, though really I was half afraid he might stay and\r\noffer to lend me a hand at my toilet, in the expression of his national\r\ncharacter. I found him with Mrs. Makely, when I went down, and she began,\r\nwith a parenthetical tribute to the beauty of the mountains in the morning\r\nlight: \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be surprised to see me up at this unnatural hour. I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow whether it was the excitement of our talk last night, or what it was,\r\nbut my sulphonal wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t act, though I took fifteen grains, and I was up\r\nwith the lark, or should have been, if there had been any lark outside of\r\nliterature to be up with. However, this air is so glorious that I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmind losing a night\xe2\x80\x99s sleep now and then. I believe that with a little\r\npractice one could get along without any sleep at all here; at least, _I_\r\ncould. I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry to say poor Mr. Makely _can\xe2\x80\x99t_, apparently. He\xe2\x80\x99s making\r\nup for his share of my vigils, and I\xe2\x80\x99m going to breakfast without him. Do\r\nyou know, I\xe2\x80\x99ve done a very bold thing: I\xe2\x80\x99ve got the head-waiter to give\r\nyou places at our table; I know you\xe2\x80\x99ll hate it, Mr. Twelvemough, because\r\nyou naturally want to keep Mr. Homos to yourself, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t blame you at\r\nall; but I\xe2\x80\x99m simply not going to _let_ you, and that\xe2\x80\x99s all there is about\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe pleasure I felt at this announcement was not unmixed, but I tried to\r\nkeep Mrs. Makely from thinking so, and I was immensely relieved when she\r\nfound a chance to say to me, in a low voice: \xe2\x80\x9cI know just how you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nfeeling, Mr. Twelvemough, and I\xe2\x80\x99m going to help you keep him from doing\r\nanything ridiculous, if I can. I _like_ him, and I think it\xe2\x80\x99s a perfect\r\nshame to have people laughing at him. I know we can manage him between\r\nus.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe so far failed, however, that the Altrurian shook hands with the\r\nhead-waiter when he pressed open the wire-netting door to let us into the\r\ndining-room, and made a bow to our waitress of the sort one makes to a\r\nlady. But we thought it best to ignore these little errors of his and\r\nreserve our moral strength for anything more spectacular. Fortunately we\r\ngot through our breakfast with nothing worse than his jumping up and\r\nstooping to hand the waitress a spoon she let fall; but this could easily\r\npass for some attention to Mrs. Makely at a little distance. There were\r\nnot many people down to breakfast yet; but I could see that there was a\r\ngood deal of subdued sensation among the waitresses, standing with folded\r\narms behind their tables, and that the head-waiter\xe2\x80\x99s handsome face was red\r\nwith anxiety.\r\n\r\nMrs. Makely asked if we were going to church. She said she was driving\r\nthat way, and would be glad to drop us. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not going myself,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nexplained, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t make anything of the sermon, with my head\r\nin the state it is, and I\xe2\x80\x99m going to compromise on a good action. I want\r\nto carry some books and papers over to Mrs. Camp. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you think that\r\nwill be quite as acceptable, Mr. Homos?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should venture to hope it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with a tolerant seriousness not\r\naltogether out of keeping with her lightness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is Mrs. Camp?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, not caring to commit myself on the question.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLizzie\xe2\x80\x99s mother. You know I told you about them last night. I think she\r\nmust have got through the books I lent her, and I know Lizzie didn\xe2\x80\x99t like\r\nto ask me for more, because she saw me talking with you, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t want\r\nto interrupt us. Such a nice girl! I think the Sunday papers must have\r\ncome, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll take them over, too; Mrs. Camp is always so glad to get\r\nthem, and she is so delightful when she gets going about public events.\r\nBut perhaps you don\xe2\x80\x99t approve of Sunday papers, Mr. Homos.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure I don\xe2\x80\x99t know, madam. I haven\xe2\x80\x99t seen them yet. You know this is\r\nthe first Sunday I\xe2\x80\x99ve been in America.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry to say you won\xe2\x80\x99t see the old Puritan Sabbath,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs.\r\nMakely, with an abrupt deflection from the question of the Sunday papers.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThough you ought to, up in these hills. The only thing left of it is\r\nrye-and-Indian bread, and these baked beans and fish-balls.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut they are very good?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I dare say they are not the worst of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was a woman who tended to levity, and I was a little afraid she might\r\nbe going to say something irreverent; but, if she were, she was\r\nforestalled by the Altrurian asking: \xe2\x80\x9cWould it be very indiscreet, madam,\r\nif I were to ask you some time to introduce me to that family?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Camps?\xe2\x80\x9d she returned. \xe2\x80\x9cNot at all. I should be perfectly delighted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n The thought seemed to strike her, and she asked: \xe2\x80\x9cWhy not go with me this\r\nmorning, unless you are inflexibly bent on going to church, you and Mr.\r\nTwelvemough?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian glanced at me, and I said I should be only too glad, if I\r\ncould carry some books, so that I could compromise on a good action, too.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake one of your own,\xe2\x80\x9d she instantly suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be too severe upon it?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Mrs. Camp might,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Makely consented, with a smile. \xe2\x80\x9cShe goes\r\nin for rather serious fiction; but I think Lizzie would enjoy a good,\r\nold-fashioned love-story, where everybody got married, as they do in your\r\ncharming books.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI winced a little, for every one likes to be regarded seriously, and I did\r\nnot enjoy being remanded to the young-girl public; but I put a bold face\r\non it, and said: \xe2\x80\x9cMy good action shall be done in behalf of Miss Lizzie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHalf an hour later, Mrs. Makely having left word with the clerk where we\r\nwere gone, so that her husband need not be alarmed when he got up, we were\r\nstriking into the hills on a two-seated buckboard, with one of the best\r\nteams of our hotel, and one of the most taciturn drivers. Mrs. Makely had\r\nthe Altrurian get into the back seat with her, and, after some attempts to\r\nmake talk with the driver, I leaned over and joined in their talk. The\r\nAltrurian was greatly interested, not so much in the landscape--though he\r\nowned its beauty when we cried out over it from point to point--but in the\r\nhuman incidents and features. He noticed the cattle in the fields, and the\r\nhorses we met on the road, and the taste and comfort of the buildings, the\r\nvariety of the crops, and the promise of the harvest. I was glad of the\r\nrespite his questions gave me from the study of the intimate character of\r\nour civilization, for they were directed now at these more material facts,\r\nand I willingly joined Mrs. Makely in answering them. We explained that\r\nthe finest teams we met were from the different hotels or boarding-houses,\r\nor at least from the farms where the people took city people to board; and\r\nthat certain shabby equipages belonged to the natives who lived solely by\r\ncultivating the soil. There was not very much of the soil cultivated, for\r\nthe chief crop was hay, with here and there a patch of potatoes or beans,\r\nand a few acres in sweet-corn. The houses of the natives, when they were\r\nfor their use only, were no better than their turnouts; it was where the\r\ncity boarder had found shelter that they were modern and pleasant. Now and\r\nthen we came to a deserted homestead, and I tried to make the Altrurian\r\nunderstand how farming in New England had yielded to the competition of\r\nthe immense agricultural operations of the West. \xe2\x80\x9cYou know,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\r\nagriculture is really an operation out there, as much as coal-mining is in\r\nPennsylvania, or finance in Wall Street; you have no idea of the vastness\r\nof the scale.\xe2\x80\x9d Perhaps I swelled a little with pride in my celebration of\r\nthe national prosperity, as it flowed from our Western farms of five and\r\nten and twenty thousand acres; I could not very well help putting on the\r\npedal in these passages. Mrs. Makely listened almost, as eagerly, as the\r\nAltrurian, for, as a cultivated American woman, she was necessarily\r\nquite ignorant of her own country, geographically, politically, and\r\nhistorically. \xe2\x80\x9cThe only people left in the hill country of New England,\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\nconcluded, \xe2\x80\x9care those who are too old or too lazy to get away. Any young\r\nman of energy would be ashamed to stay, unless he wanted to keep a\r\nboarding-house or live on the city vacationists in summer. If he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t,\r\nhe goes West and takes up some of the new land, and comes back in\r\nmiddle-life and buys a deserted farm to spend his summers on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear me!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian. \xe2\x80\x9cIs it so simple as that? Then we can\r\nhardly wonder at their owners leaving these worn-out farms; though I\r\nsuppose it must be with the pang of exile, sometimes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I fancy there isn\xe2\x80\x99t much sentiment involved,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered, lightly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhoa!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, speaking to the horses before she spoke to the\r\ndriver, as some women will. He pulled them up, and looked round at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t that Reuben Camp, _now_, over there by that house?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, as\r\nif we had been talking of him; that is another way some women have.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x9d said the driver.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, well, then!\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cReuben!\xe2\x80\x9d she called to the young man, who was\r\nprowling about the door-yard of a sad-colored old farm-house, and peering\r\ninto a window here and there. \xe2\x80\x9cCome here a moment--won\xe2\x80\x99t you, please?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe lifted his head and looked round, and, when he had located the appeal\r\nmade to him, he came down the walk to the gate and leaned over it, waiting\r\nfor further instructions. I saw that it was the young man whom we had\r\nnoticed with the girl Mrs. Makely called Lizzie on the hotel piazza the\r\nnight before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know whether I should find Lizzie at home this morning?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, she\xe2\x80\x99s there with mother,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young fellow, with neither liking\r\nnor disliking in his tone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m so glad!\xe2\x80\x9d said the lady. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t know but she might be at\r\nchurch. What in the world has happened here? Is there anything unusual\r\ngoing on inside?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I was just looking to see if it was all right. The folks wanted I\r\nshould come round.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, where are they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, they\xe2\x80\x99re gone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; gone West. They\xe2\x80\x99ve left the old place, because they couldn\xe2\x80\x99t make a\r\nliving here any longer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, this is quite a case in point,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, Mr. Homos, here is a\r\nchance to inform yourself at first hand about a very interesting fact of\r\nour civilization\xe2\x80\x9d; and I added, in a low voice, to Mrs. Makely: \xe2\x80\x9cWon\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nintroduce us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes. Mr. Camp, this is Mr. Twelvemough, the author--you know his\r\nbooks, of course; and Mr. Homos, a gentleman from Altruria.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young fellow opened the gate he leaned on and came out to us. He took\r\nno notice of me, but he seized the Altrurian\xe2\x80\x99s hand and wrung it. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nheard of _you_\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Makely, were you going to our place?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, yes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo do, then. Mother would give almost anything to see Mr. Homos. We\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nheard of Altruria, over our way,\xe2\x80\x9d he added to our friend. \xe2\x80\x9cMother\xe2\x80\x99s been\r\nreading up all she can about it. She\xe2\x80\x99ll want to talk with you, and she\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t give the rest of us much of a chance, I guess.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I shall be glad to see her,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, \xe2\x80\x9cand to tell her\r\neverything I can. But won\xe2\x80\x99t you explain to me first something about your\r\ndeserted farms here? It\xe2\x80\x99s quite a new thing to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t a new thing to us,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young fellow, with a short laugh.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd there isn\xe2\x80\x99t much to explain about it. You\xe2\x80\x99ll see them all through New\r\nEngland. When a man finds he can\xe2\x80\x99t get his funeral expenses out of the\r\nland, he don\xe2\x80\x99t feel like staying to be buried in it, and he pulls up and\r\ngoes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut people used to get their living expenses here,\xe2\x80\x9d I suggested. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t they now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, they didn\xe2\x80\x99t use to have Western prices to fight with; and then the\r\nland wasn\xe2\x80\x99t worn out so, and the taxes were not so heavy. How would you\r\nlike to pay twenty to thirty dollars on the thousand, and assessed up to\r\nthe last notch, in the city?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, what in the world makes your taxes so heavy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSchools and roads. We\xe2\x80\x99ve got to have schools, and you city folks want\r\ngood roads when you come here in the summer, don\xe2\x80\x99t you? Then the season is\r\nshort, and sometimes we can\xe2\x80\x99t make a crop. The frost catches the corn in\r\nthe field, and you have your trouble for your pains. Potatoes are the only\r\nthing we can count on, except grass, and, when everybody raises potatoes,\r\nyou know where the price goes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but now, Mr. Camp,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, leaning over toward him, and\r\nspeaking in a cosey and coaxing tone, as if he must not really keep the\r\ntruth from an old friend like her, \xe2\x80\x9cisn\xe2\x80\x99t it a good deal because the\r\nfarmers\xe2\x80\x99 daughters want pianos, and the farmers\xe2\x80\x99 sons want buggies? I\r\nheard Professor Lumen saying, the other day, that, if the farmers were\r\nwilling to work as they used to work, they could still get a good living\r\noff their farms, and that they gave up their places because they were too\r\nlazy, in many cases, to farm them properly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99d better not let _me_ hear him saying that,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young fellow,\r\nwhile a hot flush passed over his face. He added, bitterly: \xe2\x80\x9cIf he wants\r\nto see how easy it is to make a living up here, he can take this place and\r\ntry for a year or two; he can get it cheap. But I guess he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t want\r\nit the year round; he\xe2\x80\x99d only want it a few months in the summer, when he\r\ncould enjoy the sightliness of it, and see me working over there on my\r\nfarm, while he smoked on his front porch.\xe2\x80\x9d He turned round and looked at\r\nthe old house in silence a moment. Then, as he went on, his voice lost its\r\nangry ring. \xe2\x80\x9cThe folks here bought this place from the Indians, and they\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nbeen here more than two hundred years. Do you think they left it because\r\nthey were too lazy to run it, or couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get pianos and buggies out of\r\nit, or were such fools as not to know whether they were well off? It was\r\ntheir _home_; they were born and lived and died here. There is the family\r\nburying-ground over there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNeither Mrs. Makely nor myself was ready with a reply, and we left the\r\nword with the Altrurian, who suggested: \xe2\x80\x9cStill, I suppose they will be\r\nmore prosperous in the West on the new land they take up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young fellow leaned his arms on the wheel by which he stood. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do\r\nyou mean by taking up new land?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, out of the public domain--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere _ain\xe2\x80\x99t_ any public domain that\xe2\x80\x99s worth having. All the good land is\r\nin the hands of railroads and farm syndicates and speculators; and if you\r\nwant a farm in the West you\xe2\x80\x99ve got to buy it; the East is the only place\r\nwhere folks give them away, because they ain\xe2\x80\x99t worth keeping. If you\r\nhaven\xe2\x80\x99t got the ready money, you can buy one on credit, and pay ten,\r\ntwenty, and thirty per cent. interest, and live in a dugout on the\r\nplains--till your mortgage matures.\xe2\x80\x9d The young man took his arms from the\r\nwheel and moved a few steps backward, as he added: \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll see you over at\r\nthe house later.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe driver touched his horses, and we started briskly off again. But I\r\nconfess I had quite enough of his pessimism, and as we drove away I leaned\r\nback toward the Altrurian and said: \xe2\x80\x9cNow, it is all perfect nonsense to\r\npretend that things are at that pass with us. There are more millionaires\r\nin America, probably, than there are in all the other civilized countries\r\nof the globe, and it is not possible that the farming population should be\r\nin such a hopeless condition. All wealth comes out of the earth, and you\r\nmay be sure they get their full share of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad to hear you say so,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the meaning\r\nof this new party in the West that seems to have held a convention lately?\r\nI read something of it in the train yesterday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that is a lot of crazy Hayseeds, who don\xe2\x80\x99t want to pay back the money\r\nthey have borrowed, or who find themselves unable to meet their interest.\r\nIt will soon blow over. We are always having these political flurries. A\r\ngood crop will make it all right with them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut is it true that they have to pay such rates of interest as our young\r\nfriend mentioned?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, seeing the thing in the humorous light which softens for\r\nus Americans so many of the hardships of others, \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose that man likes\r\nto squeeze his brother man when he gets him in his grip. That\xe2\x80\x99s human\r\nnature, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Altrurian.\r\n\r\nIt seemed to me that he had asked something like that before when I\r\nalleged human nature in defence of some piece of every-day selfishness.\r\nBut I thought best not to notice it, and I went on: \xe2\x80\x9cThe land is so rich\r\nout there that a farm will often pay for itself with a single crop.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it possible?\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Altrurian. \xe2\x80\x9cThen I suppose it seldom really\r\nhappens that a mortgage is foreclosed, in the way our young friend\r\ninsinuated?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I can\xe2\x80\x99t say that exactly\xe2\x80\x9d; and, having admitted so much, I did not\r\nfeel bound to impart a fact that popped perversely into my mind. I was\r\nonce talking with a Western money-lender, a very good sort of fellow,\r\nfrank and open as the day; I asked him whether the farmers generally paid\r\noff their mortgages, and he answered me that if the mortgage was to the\r\nvalue of a fourth of the land, the farmer might pay it off, but if it were\r\nto a half, or a third even, he never paid it, but slaved on and died in\r\nhis debts. \xe2\x80\x9cYou may be sure, however,\xe2\x80\x9d I concluded, \xe2\x80\x9cthat our young friend\r\ntakes a jaundiced view of the situation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, really,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, \xe2\x80\x9cI must insist upon dropping this\r\neverlasting talk about money. I think it is perfectly disgusting, and I\r\nbelieve it was Mr. Makely\xe2\x80\x99s account of his speculations that kept me awake\r\nlast night. My brain got running on figures till the dark seemed to be all\r\nsown with dollar-marks, like the stars in the Milky Way. I--ugh! What in\r\nthe world is it? Oh, you dreadful little things!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Makely passed swiftly from terror to hysterical laughter as the\r\ndriver pulled up short and a group of barefooted children broke in front\r\nof his horses and scuttled out of the dust into the road-side bushes like\r\na covey of quails. There seemed to be a dozen of them, nearly all the same\r\nin size, but there turned out to be only five or six; or at least no more\r\nshowed their gleaming eyes and teeth through the underbrush in quiet\r\nenjoyment of the lady\xe2\x80\x99s alarm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you know that you might have got killed?\xe2\x80\x9d she demanded, with that\r\nseverity good women feel for people who have just escaped with their\r\nlives. \xe2\x80\x9cHow lovely the dirty little dears are!\xe2\x80\x9d she added, in the next\r\nwave of emotion. One bold fellow of six showed a half-length above the\r\nbushes, and she asked: \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you know that you oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to play in the\r\nroad when there are so many teams passing? Are all those your brothers and\r\nsisters?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe ignored the first question. \xe2\x80\x9cOne\xe2\x80\x99s my cousin.\xe2\x80\x9d I pulled out a\r\nhalf-dozen coppers, and held my hand toward him. \xe2\x80\x9cSee if there is one\r\nfor each.\xe2\x80\x9d They had no difficulty in solving the simple mathematical\r\nproblem except the smallest girl, who cried for fear and baffled longing.\r\nI tossed the coin to her, and a little fat dog darted out at her feet and\r\ncaught it up in his mouth. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, good gracious!\xe2\x80\x9d I called out in my light,\r\nhumorous way. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you suppose he\xe2\x80\x99s going to spend it for candy?\xe2\x80\x9d The\r\nlittle people thought that a famous joke, and they laughed with the\r\ngratitude that even small favors inspire. \xe2\x80\x9cBring your sister here,\xe2\x80\x9d I said\r\nto the boldest boy, and, when he came up with the little woman, I put\r\nanother copper into her hand. \xe2\x80\x9cLook out that the greedy dog doesn\xe2\x80\x99t get\r\nit,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, and my gayety met with fresh applause. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere do you live?\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\nasked, with some vague purpose of showing the Altrurian the kindliness\r\nthat exists between our upper and lower classes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOver there,\xe2\x80\x9d said the boy. I followed the twist of his head, and glimpsed\r\na wooden cottage on the border of the forest, so very new that the\r\nsheathing had not yet been covered with clapboards. I stood up in the\r\nbuckboard and saw that it was a story and a half high, and could have had\r\nfour or five rooms in it. The bare, curtainless windows were set in the\r\nunpainted frames, but the front door seemed not to be hung yet. The people\r\nmeant to winter there, however, for the sod was banked up against the\r\nwooden underpinning; a stovepipe stuck out of the roof of a little wing\r\nbehind. While I gazed a young-looking woman came to the door, as if she\r\nhad been drawn by our talk with the children, and then she jumped down\r\nfrom the threshold, which still wanted a doorstep, and came slowly out to\r\nus. The children ran to her with their coppers, and then followed her back\r\nto us.\r\n\r\nMrs. Makely called to her before she reached us: \xe2\x80\x9cI hope you weren\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nfrightened. We didn\xe2\x80\x99t drive over any of them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t frightened,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young woman. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a very safe place\r\nto bring up children, in the country, and I never feel uneasy about them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, if they are not under the horses\xe2\x80\x99 feet,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, mingling\r\ninstruction and amusement very judiciously in her reply. \xe2\x80\x9cAre they all\r\nyours?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly five,\xe2\x80\x9d said the mother, and she pointed to the alien in her flock.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s my sister\xe2\x80\x99s. She lives just below here.\xe2\x80\x9d Her children had grouped\r\nthemselves about her, and she kept passing her hands caressingly over\r\ntheir little heads as she talked. \xe2\x80\x9cMy sister has nine children, but she\r\nhas the rest at church with her to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t speak like an American,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Makely suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, we\xe2\x80\x99re English. Our husbands work in the quarry. That\xe2\x80\x99s _my_ little\r\npalace.\xe2\x80\x9d The woman nodded her head toward the cottage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s going to be very nice,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, with an evident perception\r\nof her pride in it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, if we ever get money to finish it. Thank you for the children.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it was this gentleman.\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Makely indicated me, and I bore the\r\nmerit of my good action as modestly as I could.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen thank _you_, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young woman, and she asked Mrs. Makely:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re not living about here, ma\xe2\x80\x99am?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, we\xe2\x80\x99re staying at the hotel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt the hotel! It must be very dear, there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it is expensive,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, with a note of that satisfaction\r\nin her voice which we all feel in spending a great deal of money.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I suppose you can afford it,\xe2\x80\x9d said the woman, whose eye was running\r\nhungrily over Mrs. Makely\xe2\x80\x99s pretty costume. \xe2\x80\x9cSome are poor, and some are\r\nrich. That\xe2\x80\x99s the way the world has to be made up, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, very dryly, and the talk languished from this\r\npoint, so that the driver felt warranted in starting up his horses. When\r\nwe had driven beyond earshot she said: \xe2\x80\x9cI knew she was not an American,\r\nas soon as she spoke, by her accent, and then those foreigners have no\r\nself-respect. That was a pretty bold bid for a contribution to finish up\r\nher \xe2\x80\x98little palace\xe2\x80\x99! I\xe2\x80\x99m glad you didn\xe2\x80\x99t give her anything, Mr.\r\nTwelvemough. I was afraid your sympathies had been wrought upon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, not at all,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered. \xe2\x80\x9cI saw the mischief I had done with the\r\nchildren.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian, who has not asked anything for a long time, but had\r\nlistened with eager interest to all that passed, now came up smiling with\r\nhis question: \xe2\x80\x9cWill you kindly tell me what harm would have been done by\r\noffering the woman a little money to help finish up her cottage?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI did not allow Mrs. Makely to answer, I was so eager to air my political\r\neconomy. \xe2\x80\x9cThe very greatest harm. It would have pauperized her. You have\r\nno idea how quickly they give way to the poison of that sort of thing. As\r\nsoon as they get any sort of help they expect more; they count upon it,\r\nand they begin to live upon it. The sight of those coppers which I gave\r\nher children--more out of joke than charity--demoralized the woman. She\r\ntook us for rich people, and wanted us to build her a house. You have to\r\nguard against every approach to a thing of that sort.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, \xe2\x80\x9cthat an American would have hinted,\r\nas she did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98No, an American would not have done that, I\xe2\x80\x99m thankful to say. They take\r\nfees, but they don\xe2\x80\x99t ask charity, yet.\xe2\x80\x9d We went on to exult in the noble\r\nindependence of the American character in all classes, at some length. We\r\ntalked at the Altrurian, but he did not seem to hear us. At last he asked,\r\nwith a faint sigh: \xe2\x80\x9cThen, in your conditions, a kindly impulse to aid one\r\nwho needs your help is something to be guarded against as possibly\r\npernicious?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd now you see what difficulties beset us in dealing\r\nwith the problem of poverty. We cannot let people suffer, for that would\r\nbe cruel; and we cannot relieve their need without pauperizing them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is a terrible quandary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you would tell us just how you manage\r\nwith the poor in Altruria.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have none,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the comparatively poor--you have some people who are richer than\r\nothers?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. We should regard that as the worst incivism.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is incivism?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI interpreted, \xe2\x80\x9cBad citizenship.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, if you will excuse me, Mr. Homos,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI think that is\r\nsimply impossible. There _must_ be rich and there _must_ be poor. There\r\nalways have been, and there always will be. That woman said it as well as\r\nanybody. Didn\xe2\x80\x99t Christ Himself say, \xe2\x80\x98The poor ye have always with you\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVII\r\n\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian looked at Mrs. Makely with an amazement visibly heightened\r\nby the air of complacency she put on after delivering this poser: \xe2\x80\x9cDo you\r\nreally think Christ meant that you _ought_ always to have the poor with\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, of course!\xe2\x80\x9d she answered, triumphantly. \xe2\x80\x9cHow else are the sympathies\r\nof the rich to be cultivated? The poverty of some and the wealth of\r\nothers, isn\xe2\x80\x99t that what forms the great tie of human brotherhood? If we\r\nwere all comfortable, or all shared alike, there could not be anything\r\nlike charity, and Paul said, \xe2\x80\x98The greatest of these is charity.\xe2\x80\x99 I believe\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x98love\xe2\x80\x99 in the new version, but it comes to the same thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian gave a kind of gasp, and then lapsed into a silence that\r\nlasted until we came in sight of the Camp farm-house. It stood on the\r\ncrest of a road-side upland, and looked down the beautiful valley, bathed\r\nin Sabbath sunlight, and away to the ranges of hills, so far that it was\r\nhard to say whether it was sun or shadow that dimmed their distance.\r\nDecidedly, the place was what the country people call sightly. The old\r\nhouse, once painted a Brandon red, crouched low to the ground, with its\r\nlean-to in the rear, and its flat-arched wood-sheds and wagon-houses\r\nstretching away at the side of the barn, and covering the approach to it\r\nwith an unbroken roof. There were flowers in the beds along the\r\nunderpinning of the house, which stood close to the street, and on one\r\nside of the door was a clump of Spanish willow; an old-fashioned June rose\r\nclimbed over it from the other. An aged dog got stiffly to his feet from\r\nthe threshold stone and whimpered as our buckboard drew up; the poultry\r\npicking about the path and among the chips lazily made way for us, and as\r\nour wheels ceased to crunch upon the gravel we heard hasty steps, and\r\nReuben Camp came round the corner of the house in time to give Mrs. Makely\r\nhis hand and help her spring to the ground, which she did very lightly;\r\nher remarkable mind had kept her body in a sort of sympathetic activity,\r\nand at thirty-five she had the gracile ease and self-command of a girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Reuben,\xe2\x80\x9d she sighed, permitting herself to call him by his first\r\nname, with the emotion which expressed itself more definitely in the words\r\nthat followed, \xe2\x80\x9chow I envy you all this dear, old, homelike place! I never\r\ncome here without thinking of my grandfather\xe2\x80\x99s farm in Massachusetts,\r\nwhere I used to go every summer when I was a little girl. If I had a place\r\nlike this, I should never leave it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Mrs. Makely,\xe2\x80\x9d said young Camp, \xe2\x80\x9cyou can have this place cheap, if\r\nyou really want it. Or almost any other place in the neighborhood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say such a thing!\xe2\x80\x9d she returned. \xe2\x80\x9cIt makes one feel as if the\r\nfoundations of the great deep were giving way. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what that\r\nmeans exactly, but I suppose it\xe2\x80\x99s equivalent to mislaying George\xe2\x80\x99s hatchet\r\nand going back on the Declaration generally; and I don\xe2\x80\x99t like to hear you\r\ntalk so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCamp seemed to have lost his bitter mood, and he answered, pleasantly:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Declaration is all right, as far as it goes, but it don\xe2\x80\x99t help us to\r\ncompete with the Western farm operations.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you believe every one was born free and equal, don\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs.\r\nMakely asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, I believe that; but--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen why do you object to free and equal competition?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young fellow laughed, and said, as he opened the door for us: \xe2\x80\x9cWalk\r\nright into the parlor, please. Mother will be ready for you in a minute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n He added: \xe2\x80\x9cI guess she\xe2\x80\x99s putting on her best cap for you, Mr. Homos. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\na great event for her, your coming here. It is for all of us. We\xe2\x80\x99re glad\r\nto have you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\xe2\x80\x99m glad to be here,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, as simply as the other. He\r\nlooked about the best room of a farm-house that had never adapted itself\r\nto the tastes or needs of the city boarder, and was as stiffly repellent\r\nin its upholstery and as severe in its decoration as hair-cloth chairs and\r\ndark-brown wall-paper of a trellis pattern, with drab roses, could make\r\nit. The windows were shut tight, and our host did not offer to open them.\r\nA fly or two crossed the doorway into the hall, but made no attempt\r\nto penetrate the interior, where we sat in an obscurity that left the\r\nhigh-hung family photographs on the walls vague and uncertain. I made a\r\nmental note of it as a place where it would be very characteristic to have\r\na rustic funeral take place; and I was pleased to have Mrs. Makely drop\r\ninto a sort of mortuary murmur, as she said: \xe2\x80\x9cI hope your mother is as\r\nwell as usual this morning?\xe2\x80\x9d I perceived that this murmur was produced by\r\nthe sepulchral influence of the room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Camp, and at that moment a door opened from the room across\r\nthe hall, and his sister seemed to bring in some of the light from it to\r\nus where we sat. She shook hands with Mrs. Makely, who introduced me to\r\nher, and then presented the Altrurian. She bowed very civilly to me, but\r\nwith a touch of severity, such as country people find necessary for the\r\nassertion of their self-respect with strangers. I thought it very pretty,\r\nand instantly saw that I could work it into some picture of character; and\r\nI was not at all sorry that she made a difference in favor of the\r\nAltrurian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMother will be so glad to see you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to him, and, \xe2\x80\x9cWon\xe2\x80\x99t you come\r\nright in?\xe2\x80\x9d she added to us all.\r\n\r\nWe followed her and found ourselves in a large, low, sunny room on\r\nthe southeast corner of the house, which had no doubt once been the\r\nliving-room, but which was now given up to the bedridden invalid; a door\r\nopened into the kitchen behind, where the table was already laid for the\r\nmidday meal, with the plates turned down in the country fashion, and some\r\nnetting drawn over the dishes to keep the flies away.\r\n\r\nMrs. Makely bustled up to the bedside with her energetic, patronizing\r\ncheerfulness. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, Mrs. Camp, I am glad to see you looking so well this\r\nmorning. I\xe2\x80\x99ve been meaning to run over for several days past, but I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t find a moment till this morning, and I knew you didn\xe2\x80\x99t object to\r\nSunday visits.\xe2\x80\x9d She took the invalid\xe2\x80\x99s hand in hers, and, with the air of\r\nshowing how little she felt any inequality between them, she leaned over\r\nand kissed her, where Mrs. Camp sat propped against her pillows. She had a\r\nlarge, nobly moulded face of rather masculine contour, and at the same\r\ntime the most motherly look in the world. Mrs. Makely bubbled and babbled\r\non, and every one waited patiently till she had done, and turned and said,\r\ntoward the Altrurian: \xe2\x80\x9cI have ventured to bring my friend, Mr. Homos, with\r\nme. He is from Altruria.\xe2\x80\x9d Then she turned to me and said: \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Twelvemough\r\nyou know already through his delightful books\xe2\x80\x9d; but, although she paid me\r\nthis perfunctory compliment it was perfectly apparent to me that in the\r\nesteem of this disingenuous woman the distinguished stranger was a far\r\nmore important person than the distinguished author. Whether Mrs. Camp\r\nread my perception of this fact in my face or not I cannot say, but she\r\nwas evidently determined that I should not feel a difference in her. She\r\nheld out her hand to me first, and said that I never could know how many\r\nheavy hours I had helped to lighten for her, and then she turned to the\r\nAltrurian and took his hand. \xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with a long, deep-drawn sigh,\r\nas if that were the supreme moment of her life. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd are you really from\r\nAltruria? It seems too good to be true!\xe2\x80\x9d Her devout look and her earnest\r\ntone gave the commonplace words a quality that did not inhere in them, but\r\nMrs. Makely took them on their surface.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d she made haste to interpose, before the Altrurian could\r\nsay anything. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is just the way we all feel about it, Mrs. Camp. I\r\nassure you, if it were not for the accounts in the papers and the talk\r\nabout it everywhere, I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t believe there _was_ any such place as\r\nAltruria; and if it were not for Mr. Twelvemough here--who has to keep all\r\nhis inventions for his novels, as a mere matter of business routine--I\r\nmight really suspect him and Mr. Homos of--well, _working_ us, as my\r\nhusband calls it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian smiled politely, but vaguely, as if he had not quite caught\r\nher meaning, and I made answer for both: \xe2\x80\x9cI am sure, Mrs. Makely, if you\r\ncould understand my peculiar state of mind about Mr. Homos, you would\r\nnever believe that I was in collusion with him. I find him quite as\r\nincredible as you do. There are moments when he seems so entirely\r\nsubjective with me that I feel as if he were no more definite or tangible\r\nthan a bad conscience.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, and she laughed out her delight in my\r\nillustration.\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian must have perceived that we were joking, though the Camps\r\nall remained soberly silent. \xe2\x80\x9cI hope it isn\xe2\x80\x99t so bad as that,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthough I have noticed that I seem to affect you all with a kind of\r\nmisgiving. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know just what it is; but, if I could remove it, I\r\nshould be very glad to do so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Makely very promptly seized her chance: \xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, in the first\r\nplace, my husband and I were talking it over last night after we left you,\r\nand that was one of the things that kept us awake; it turned into money\r\nafterward. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t so much that a whole continent, as big as Australia,\r\nremained undiscovered till within such a very few years, as it is the\r\ncondition of things among you: this sort of all living for one another,\r\nand not each one for himself. My husband says that is simply moonshine;\r\nsuch a thing never was and never can be; it is opposed to human nature,\r\nand would take away incentive and all motive for exertion and advancement\r\nand enterprise. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know _what_ he didn\xe2\x80\x99t say against it; but one\r\nthing, he says it\xe2\x80\x99s perfectly un-American.\xe2\x80\x9d The Altrurian remained silent,\r\ngravely smiling, and Mrs. Makely added, with her most engaging little\r\nmanner: \xe2\x80\x9cI hope you won\xe2\x80\x99t feel hurt, personally or patriotically, by what\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve repeated to you. I know my husband is awfully Philistine, though he\r\n_is_ such a good fellow, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t, by any means, agree with him on all\r\nthose points; but I _would_ like to know what you think of them. The\r\ntrouble is, Mrs. Camp,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, turning to the invalid, \xe2\x80\x9cthat Mr. Homos\r\nis so dreadfully reticent about his own country, and I am so curious to\r\nhear of it at first hands, that I consider it justifiable to use any means\r\nto make him open up about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no offence,\xe2\x80\x9d the Altrurian answered for himself, \xe2\x80\x9cin what Mr.\r\nMakely says, though, from the Altrurian point of view, there is a good\r\ndeal of error.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes it seem so strange to you,\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, addressing himself to Mrs.\r\nCamp, \xe2\x80\x9cthat people should found a civilization on the idea of living for\r\none another instead of each for himself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d she answered. \xe2\x80\x9cPoor people have always had to live that way,\r\nor they could not have lived at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was what I understood your porter to say last night,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nAltrurian to me. He added, to the company generally: \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose that even\r\nin America there are more poor people than there are rich people?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know about that,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose there are more people\r\nindependently rich than there are people independently poor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will let that formulation of it stand. If it is true, I do not see why\r\nthe Altrurian system should be considered so very un-American. Then, as to\r\nwhether there is or ever was really a practical altruism, a civic\r\nexpression of it, I think it cannot be denied that among the first\r\nChristians, those who immediately followed Christ, and might be supposed\r\nto be directly influenced by His life, there was an altruism practised as\r\nradical as that which we have organized into a national polity and a\r\nworking economy in Altruria.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but you know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, with the air of advancing a point\r\nnot to be put aside, \xe2\x80\x9cthey had to drop _that_. It was a dead failure. They\r\nfound that they couldn\xe2\x80\x99t make it go at all among cultivated people, and\r\nthat, if Christianity was to advance, they would have to give up all that\r\ncrankish kind of idolatry of the mere letter. At any rate,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on,\r\nwith the satisfaction we all feel in getting an opponent into close\r\nquarters, \xe2\x80\x9cyou must confess that there is a much greater play of\r\nindividuality here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBefore the Altrurian could reply, young Camp said: \xe2\x80\x9cIf you want to see\r\nAmerican individuality, the real, simon-pure article, you ought to go down\r\nto one of our big factory towns and look at the mill-hands coming home in\r\ndroves after a day\xe2\x80\x99s work, young girls and old women, boys and men, all\r\nfluffed over with cotton, and so dead tired that they can hardly walk.\r\nThey come shambling along with all the individuality of a flock of sheep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, heroically, as if she were one of these, \xe2\x80\x9cmust\r\nbe sacrificed. Of course, some are not so individual as others. A great\r\ndeal depends upon temperament.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA great deal more depends upon capital,\xe2\x80\x9d said Camp, with an offensive\r\nlaugh. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you have capital in America, you can have individuality; if you\r\nhaven\xe2\x80\x99t, you can\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis sister, who had not taken part in the talk before, said, demurely: \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nseems to me you\xe2\x80\x99ve got a good deal of individuality, Reub, and you haven\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ngot a great deal of capital, either,\xe2\x80\x9d and the two young people laughed\r\ntogether.\r\n\r\nMrs. Makely was one of those fatuous women whose eagerness to make a point\r\nexcludes the consideration even of their own advantage. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid, as if speaking for the upper classes, \xe2\x80\x9cwe haven\xe2\x80\x99t got any\r\nindividuality at all. We are as like as so many peas or pins. In fact, you\r\nhave to be so in society. If you keep asserting your own individuality too\r\nmuch, people avoid you. It\xe2\x80\x99s very vulgar and the greatest bore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you don\xe2\x80\x99t find individuality so desirable, after all,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nAltrurian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI perfectly detest it!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the lady, and evidently she had not the\r\nleast notion where she was in the argument. \xe2\x80\x9cFor my part, I\xe2\x80\x99m never happy\r\nexcept when I\xe2\x80\x99ve forgotten myself and the whole individual bother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer declaration seemed somehow to close the incident, and we were all\r\nsilent a moment, which I employed in looking about the room, and taking in\r\nwith my literary sense the simplicity and even bareness of its furnishing.\r\nThere was the bed where the invalid lay, and near the head a table with a\r\npile of books and a kerosene-lamp on it, and I decided that she was a good\r\ndeal wakeful, and that she read by that lamp when she could not sleep at\r\nnight. Then there were the hard chairs we sat on, and some home-made\r\nhooked rugs, in rounds and ovals, scattered about the clean floor; there\r\nwas a small melodeon pushed against the wall; the windows had paper\r\nshades, and I recalled that I had not seen any blinds on the outside of\r\nthe house. Over the head of the bed hung a cavalryman\xe2\x80\x99s sword, with its\r\nbelt--the sword that Mrs. Makely had spoken of. It struck me as a room\r\nwhere a great many things might have happened, and I said: \xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthink, Mrs. Camp, how glad I am to see the inside of your house. It seems\r\nto me so typical.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA pleased intelligence showed itself in her face, and she answered: \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\r\nit is a real old-fashioned farmhouse. We have never taken boarders, and so\r\nwe have kept it as it was built pretty much, and only made such changes in\r\nit as we needed or wanted for ourselves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a pity,\xe2\x80\x9d I went on, following up what I thought a fortunate lead,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthat we city people see so little of the farming life when we come into\r\nthe country. I have been here now for several seasons, and this is the\r\nfirst time I have been inside a farmer\xe2\x80\x99s house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it possible!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Altrurian, with an air of utter astonishment;\r\nand, when I found the fact appeared so singular to him, I began to be\r\nrather proud of its singularity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I suppose that most city people come and go, year after year, in the\r\ncountry, and never make any sort of acquaintance with the people who live\r\nthere the year round. We keep to ourselves in the hotels, or, if we go out\r\nat all, it is to make a call upon some city cottager, and so we do not get\r\nout of the vicious circle of our own over-intimacy with ourselves and our\r\nignorance of others.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you regard that as a great misfortune?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Altrurian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99s inevitable. There is nothing to bring us together, unless it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsome happy accident, like the present. But we don\xe2\x80\x99t have a traveler from\r\nAltruria to exploit every day, and so we have no business to come into\r\npeople\xe2\x80\x99s houses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou would have been welcome in ours long ago, Mr. Twelvemough,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs.\r\nCamp.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, excuse me,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat you say really seems dreadful\r\nto me. Why, it is as if you were not the same race or kind of men!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered. \xe2\x80\x9cIt has sometimes seemed to me as if our big hotel\r\nthere were a ship anchored off some strange coast. The inhabitants come\r\nout with supplies, and carry on their barter with the ship\xe2\x80\x99s steward, and\r\nwe sometimes see them over the side, but we never speak to them or have\r\nanything to do with them. We sail away at the close of the season, and\r\nthat is the end of it till next summer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian turned to Mrs. Camp. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd how do you look at it? How does it\r\nseem to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe we have thought about it very much; but, now that Mr.\r\nTwelvemough has spoken of it, I can see that it does look that way. And it\r\nseems very strange, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t it, for we are all the same people, and have\r\nthe same language and religion and country--the country that my husband\r\nfought for and, I suppose I may say, died for; he was never the same man\r\nafter the war. It does appear as if we had some interests in common, and\r\nmight find it out if we ever came together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a great advantage, the city people going into the country so much as\r\nthey do now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely. \xe2\x80\x9cThey bring five million dollars into the\r\nState of New Hampshire, alone, every summer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked round for the general approval which this fact merited, and\r\nyoung Camp said: \xe2\x80\x9cAnd it shows how worthless the natives are, that they\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t make both ends meet, with all that money, but have to give up their\r\nfarms and go West, after all. I suppose you think it comes from wanting\r\nbuggies and pianos.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it certainly comes from something,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, with the\r\ncourage of her convictions.\r\n\r\nShe was evidently not going to be put down by that sour young fellow, and\r\nI was glad of it, though I must say I thought the thing she left to rankle\r\nin his mind from our former meeting had not been said in very good taste.\r\nI thought, too, that she would not fare best in any encounter of wits with\r\nhim, and I rather trembled for the result. I said, to relieve the strained\r\nsituation: \xe2\x80\x9cI wish there was some way of our knowing each other better.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m sure there\xe2\x80\x99s a great deal of good-will on both sides.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, there isn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Camp, \xe2\x80\x9cor at least I can answer for our side that\r\nthere isn\xe2\x80\x99t. You come into the country to get as much for your money as\r\nyou can, and we mean to let you have as little as we can. That\xe2\x80\x99s the whole\r\nstory, and if Mr. Homos believes anything different, he\xe2\x80\x99s very much\r\nmistaken.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hadn\xe2\x80\x99t formed any conclusion in regard to the matter, which is quite\r\nnew to me,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, mildly. \xe2\x80\x9cBut why is there no basis of\r\nmutual kindness between you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause it\xe2\x80\x99s like everything else with us; it\xe2\x80\x99s a question of supply and\r\ndemand, and there is no room for any mutual kindness in a question of that\r\nkind. Even if there were, there is another thing that would kill it. The\r\nsummer folks, as we call them, look down on the natives, as they call us,\r\nand we know it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Mr. Camp, I am sure that you cannot say _I_ look down on the\r\nnatives,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, with an air of argument.\r\n\r\nThe young fellow laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, you do,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, not unamiably, and he\r\nadded, \xe2\x80\x9cand you\xe2\x80\x99ve got the right to. We\xe2\x80\x99re not fit to associate with you,\r\nand you know it, and we know it. You\xe2\x80\x99ve got more money, and you\xe2\x80\x99ve got\r\nnicer clothes, and you\xe2\x80\x99ve got prettier manners. You talk about things that\r\nmost natives never heard of, and you care for things they never saw. I\r\nknow it\xe2\x80\x99s the custom to pretend differently, but I\xe2\x80\x99m not going to pretend\r\ndifferently.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI recalled what my friend the banker said about throwing away cant, and I\r\nasked myself if I were in the presence of some such free spirit again. I\r\ndid not see how young Camp could afford it; but then I reflected that he\r\nhad really nothing to lose by it, for he did not expect to make anything\r\nout of us; Mrs. Makely would probably not give up his sister as seamstress\r\nif the girl continued to work so well and so cheaply as she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuppose,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, \xe2\x80\x9cthat some old native took you at your word, and\r\ncame to call upon you at the hotel, with his wife, just as one of the city\r\ncottagers would do if he wanted to make your acquaintance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should be perfectly delighted,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, \xe2\x80\x9cand I should receive\r\nthem with the greatest possible cordiality.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe same kind of cordiality that you would show to the cottagers?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose that I should feel that I had more in common with the\r\ncottagers. We should be interested in the same things, and we should\r\nprobably know the same people and have more to talk about--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou would both belong to the same class, and that tells the whole story.\r\nIf you were out West, and the owner of one of those big twenty-thousand-acre\r\nfarms called on you with his wife, would you act toward them as you\r\nwould toward our natives? You wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t. You would all be rich people\r\ntogether, and you would understand one another because you had money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, that is not so,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Makely interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are plenty of rich\r\npeople one wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t wish to know at all, and who really can\xe2\x80\x99t get into\r\nsociety--who are ignorant and vulgar. And then, when you come to money, I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t see but what country people are as glad to get it as anybody.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, gladder,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Mrs. Makely, as if this were a final stroke of logic. The\r\nyoung man did not reply, and Mrs. Makely continued: \xe2\x80\x9cNow I will appeal to\r\nyour sister to say whether she has ever seen any difference in my manner\r\ntoward her from what I show to all the young ladies in the hotel.\xe2\x80\x9d The\r\nyoung girl flushed and seemed reluctant to answer. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Lizzie!\xe2\x80\x9d cried\r\nMrs. Makely, and her tone showed that she was really hurt.\r\n\r\nThe scene appeared to me rather cruel, and I glanced at Mrs. Camp with an\r\nexpectation that she would say something to relieve it. But she did not.\r\nHer large, benevolent face expressed only a quiet interest in the\r\ndiscussion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know very well, Mrs. Makely,\xe2\x80\x9d said the girl, \xe2\x80\x9cyou don\xe2\x80\x99t regard me as\r\nyou do the young ladies in the hotel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was no resentment in her voice or look, but only a sort of regret,\r\nas if, but for this grievance, she could have loved the woman from whom\r\nshe had probably had much kindness. The tears came into Mrs. Makely\xe2\x80\x99s\r\neyes, and she turned toward Mrs. Camp. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd is this the way you _all_ feel\r\ntoward us?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t we?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the invalid, in her turn. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, no, it isn\xe2\x80\x99t the\r\nway all the country people feel. Many of them feel as you would like to\r\nhave them feel; but that is because they do not think. When they think,\r\nthey feel as we do. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t blame you. You can\xe2\x80\x99t help yourselves any\r\nmore than we can. We\xe2\x80\x99re all bound up together in that, at least.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this apparent relenting Mrs. Makely tricked her beams a little, and\r\nsaid, plaintively, as if offering herself for further condolence: \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\r\nthat is what that woman at the little shanty back there said: some have to\r\nbe rich, and some have to be poor; it takes all kinds to make a world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow would you like to be one of those that have to be poor?\xe2\x80\x9d asked young\r\nCamp, with an evil grin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, with unexpected spirit; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I am sure\r\nthat I should respect the feelings of all, rich or poor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry if we have hurt yours, Mrs. Makely,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Camp, with\r\ndignity. \xe2\x80\x9cYou asked us certain questions, and we thought you wished us to\r\nreply truthfully. We could not answer you with smooth things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut sometimes you do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, and the tears stood in her eyes\r\nagain. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd you know how fond I am of you all!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Camp wore a bewildered look. \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps we have said more than we\r\nought. But I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help it, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t see how the children could,\r\nwhen you asked them here, before Mr. Homos.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI glanced at the Altrurian, sitting attentive and silent, and a sudden\r\nmisgiving crossed my mind concerning him. Was he really a man, a human\r\nentity, a personality like ourselves, or was he merely a sort of spiritual\r\nsolvent, sent for the moment to precipitate whatever sincerity there was\r\nin us, and show us what the truth was concerning our relations to one\r\nanother? It was a fantastic conception, but I thought it was one that I\r\nmight employ in some sort of purely romantic design, and I was\r\nprofessionally grateful for it. I said, with a humorous gayety: \xe2\x80\x9cYes, we\r\nall seem to have been compelled to be much more honest than we like; and\r\nif Mr. Homos is going to write an account of his travels when he gets\r\nhome, he can\xe2\x80\x99t accuse us of hypocrisy, at any rate. And I always used to\r\nthink it was one of our virtues! What with Mr. Camp, here, and my friend\r\nthe banker at the hotel, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think he\xe2\x80\x99ll have much reason to complain\r\neven of our reticence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, whatever he says of us,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Mrs. Makely, with a pious glance at\r\nthe sword over the bed, \xe2\x80\x9che will have to say that, in spite of our\r\ndivisions and classes, we are all Americans, and, if we haven\xe2\x80\x99t the same\r\nopinions and ideas on minor matters, we all have the same country.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know about that,\xe2\x80\x9d came from Reuben Camp, with shocking\r\npromptness. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe we all have the same country. America is one\r\nthing for you, and it\xe2\x80\x99s quite another thing for us. America means ease and\r\ncomfort and amusement for you, year in and year out, and if it means work,\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s work that you _wish_ to do. For us, America means work that we _have_\r\nto do, and hard work all the time if we\xe2\x80\x99re going to make both ends meet.\r\nIt means liberty for you; but what liberty has a man got who doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nwhere his next meal is coming from? Once I was in a strike, when I was\r\nworking on the railroad, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen men come and give up their liberty\r\nfor a chance to earn their family\xe2\x80\x99s living. They knew they were right, and\r\nthat they ought to have stood up for their rights; but they had to lie\r\ndown and lick the hand that fed them. Yes, we are all Americans, but I\r\nguess we haven\xe2\x80\x99t all got the same country, Mrs. Makely. What sort of a\r\ncountry has a blacklisted man got?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA blacklisted man?\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you mean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, a kind of man I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen in the mill towns, that the bosses have all\r\ngot on their books as a man that isn\xe2\x80\x99t to be given work on any account;\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s to be punished with hunger and cold, and turned into the street,\r\nfor having offended them; and that\xe2\x80\x99s to be made to suffer through his\r\nhelpless family for having offended them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me, Mr. Camp,\xe2\x80\x9d I interposed, \xe2\x80\x9cbut isn\xe2\x80\x99t a blacklisted man usually\r\na man who has made himself prominent in some labor trouble?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d the young fellow answered, without seeming sensible of the point I\r\nhad made.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d I returned. \xe2\x80\x9cThen you can hardly blame the employers for taking it\r\nout of him in any way they can. That\xe2\x80\x99s human nature.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood heavens!\xe2\x80\x9d the Altrurian cried out. \xe2\x80\x9cIs it possible that in America\r\nit is human nature to take away the bread of a man\xe2\x80\x99s family because he has\r\ngone counter to your interest or pleasure on some economical question?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Mr. Twelvemough seems to think so,\xe2\x80\x9d sneered the young man. \xe2\x80\x9cBut\r\nwhether it\xe2\x80\x99s human nature or not, it\xe2\x80\x99s a fact that they do it, and you can\r\nguess how much a blacklisted man must love the country where such a thing\r\ncan happen to him. What should you call such a thing as blacklisting in\r\nAltruria?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Makely pleaded, \xe2\x80\x9cdo let us get him to talking about\r\nAltruria on any terms. I think all this about the labor question is so\r\ntiresome; don\xe2\x80\x99t you, Mrs. Camp?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Camp did not answer; but the Altrurian said, in reply to her son: \xe2\x80\x9cWe\r\nshould have no name for such a thing, for with us such a thing would be\r\nimpossible. There is no crime so heinous with us that the punishment would\r\ntake away the criminal\xe2\x80\x99s chance of earning his living.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, if he was a criminal,\xe2\x80\x9d said young Camp, \xe2\x80\x9che would be all right\r\n_here_. The state would give him a chance to earn his living then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut if he had no other chance of earning his living, and had committed no\r\noffence against the laws--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen the state would let him take to the road--like that fellow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe pulled aside the shade of the window where he sat, and we saw pausing\r\nbefore the house, and glancing doubtfully at the doorstep, where the dog\r\nlay, a vile and loathsome-looking tramp, a blot upon the sweet and\r\nwholesome landscape, a scandal to the sacred day. His rags burlesqued the\r\nform which they did not wholly hide; his broken shoes were covered with\r\ndust; his coarse hair came in a plume through his tattered hat; his red,\r\nsodden face, at once fierce and timid, was rusty with a fortnight\xe2\x80\x99s beard.\r\nHe offended the eye like a visible stench, and the wretched carrion seemed\r\nto shrink away from our gaze as if he were aware of his loathsomeness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, \xe2\x80\x9cI thought those fellows were arrested now. It\r\nis too bad to leave them at large. They are dangerous.\xe2\x80\x9d Young Camp left\r\nthe room, and we saw him going out toward the tramp.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, that\xe2\x80\x99s quite right,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lady. \xe2\x80\x9cI hope Reuben is going to send\r\nhim about his business. Why, surely, he\xe2\x80\x99s not going to feed the horrid\r\ncreature!\xe2\x80\x9d she added, as Camp, after a moment\xe2\x80\x99s parley with the tramp,\r\nturned with him and disappeared round a corner of the house. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, Mrs.\r\nCamp, I think that is really a very bad example. It\xe2\x80\x99s encouraging them.\r\nVery likely he\xe2\x80\x99ll go to sleep in your barn, and set it on fire with his\r\npipe. What do you do with tramps in Altruria, Mr. Homos?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian seemed not to have heard her. He said to Mrs. Camp: \xe2\x80\x9cThen I\r\nunderstand from something your son let fall that he has not always been at\r\nhome with you here. Does he reconcile himself easily to the country after\r\nthe excitement of town life? I have read that the cities in America are\r\ndraining the country of the young people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think he was sorry to come home,\xe2\x80\x9d said the mother, with a touch\r\nof fond pride. \xe2\x80\x9cBut there was no choice for him after his father died; he\r\nwas always a good boy, and he has not made us feel that we were keeping\r\nhim away from anything better. When his father was alive we let him go,\r\nbecause then we were not so dependent, and I wished him to try his fortune\r\nin the world, as all boys long to do. But he is rather peculiar, and he\r\nseems to have got quite enough of the world. To be sure, I don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s seen the brightest side of it. He first went to work in the mills\r\ndown at Ponkwasset, but he was \xe2\x80\x98laid off\xe2\x80\x99 there when the hard times came\r\nand there was so much overproduction, and he took a job of railroading,\r\nand was braking on a freight-train when his father left us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Makely said, smiling: \xe2\x80\x9cNo, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think that was the brightest\r\noutlook in the world. No wonder he has brought back such gloomy\r\nimpressions. I am sure that if he could have seen life under brighter\r\nauspices he would not have the ideas he has.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery likely,\xe2\x80\x9d said the mother, dryly. \xe2\x80\x9cOur experiences have a great deal\r\nto do with forming our opinions. But I am not dissatisfied with my son\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nideas. I suppose Reuben got a good many of his ideas from his father: he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhis father all over again. My husband thought slavery was wrong, and he\r\nwent into the war to fight against it. He used to say when the war was\r\nover that the negroes were emancipated, but slavery was not abolished\r\nyet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat in the world did he mean by that?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Mrs. Makely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomething you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t understand as we do. I tried to carry on the farm\r\nafter he first went, and before Reuben was large enough to help me much\r\nand ought to be in school, and I suppose I overdid. At any rate, that was\r\nwhen I had my first shock of paralysis. I never was very strong, and I\r\npresume my health was weakened by my teaching school so much, and\r\nstudying, before I was married. But that doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter now, and hasn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nfor many a year. The place was clear of debt then, but I had to get a\r\nmortgage put on it. The savings-bank down in the village took it, and\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99ve been paying the interest ever since. My husband died paying it, and\r\nmy son will pay it all my life, and then I suppose the bank will\r\nforeclose.
