The treasurer was an old playmate of my husband\xe2\x80\x99s, and he said\r\nthat as long as either of us lived the mortgage could lie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow splendid of him!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely. \xe2\x80\x9cI should think you had been very\r\nfortunate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said that you would not see it as we do,\xe2\x80\x9d said the invalid, patiently.\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian asked: \xe2\x80\x9cAre there mortgages on many of the farms in the\r\nneighborhood?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNearly all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Camp. \xe2\x80\x9cWe seem to own them, but in fact they own\r\nus.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Makely hastened to say: \xe2\x80\x9cMy husband thinks it\xe2\x80\x99s the best way to have\r\nyour property. If you mortgage it close up, you have all your capital\r\nfree, and you can keep turning it over. That\xe2\x80\x99s what you ought to do, Mrs.\r\nCamp. But what was the slavery that Captain Camp said was not abolished\r\nyet?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe invalid looked at her a moment without replying, and just then the\r\ndoor of the kitchen opened, and Young Camp came in and began to gather\r\nsome food from the table on a plate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you bring him to the table, Reub?\xe2\x80\x9d his sister called to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, he says he\xe2\x80\x99d rather not come in, as long as we have company. He says\r\nhe isn\xe2\x80\x99t dressed for dinner; left his spike-tail in the city.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young man laughed, and his sister with him.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVIII\r\n\r\n\r\nYoung Camp carried out the plate of victuals to the tramp, and Mrs. Makely\r\nsaid to his mother: \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you would make the tramp do some sort of\r\nwork to earn his breakfast on week-days?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot always,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Camp replied. \xe2\x80\x9cDo the boarders at the hotel always work\r\nto earn their breakfast?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, certainly not,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, with the sharpness of offence. \xe2\x80\x9cBut\r\nthey always pay for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think that paying for a thing is earning it. Perhaps some one\r\nelse earned the money that pays for it. But I believe there is too much\r\nwork in the world. If I were to live my life over again, I should not work\r\nhalf so hard. My husband and I took this place when we were young married\r\npeople, and began working to pay for it. We wanted to feel that it was\r\nours, that we owned it, and that our children should own it afterward. We\r\nboth worked all day long like slaves, and many a moonlight night we were\r\nup till morning, almost, gathering the stones from our fields and burying\r\nthem in deep graves that we had dug for them. But we buried our youth and\r\nstrength and health in those graves, too, and what for? I don\xe2\x80\x99t own the\r\nfarm that we worked so hard to pay for, and my children won\xe2\x80\x99t. That is\r\nwhat it has all come to. We were rightly punished for our greed, I\r\nsuppose. Perhaps no one has a right to own any portion of the earth.\r\nSometimes I think so, but my husband and I earned this farm, and now the\r\nsavings-bank owns it. That seems strange, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t it? I suppose you\xe2\x80\x99ll say\r\nthat the bank paid for it. Well, perhaps so; but the bank didn\xe2\x80\x99t earn it.\r\nWhen I think of that I don\xe2\x80\x99t always think that a person who pays for his\r\nbreakfast has the best right to a breakfast.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI could see the sophistry of all this, but I had not the heart to point it\r\nout; I felt the pathos of it, too. Mrs. Makely seemed not to see the one\r\nnor to feel the other very distinctly. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, but surely,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cif\r\nyou give a tramp his breakfast without making him work for it, you must\r\nsee that it is encouraging idleness. And idleness is very corrupting--the\r\nsight of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean to the country people? Well, they have to stand a good deal of\r\nthat. The summer folks that spend four or five months of the year here\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t seem to do anything from morning till night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but you must recollect that they are _resting_! You have no idea how\r\nhard they all work in town during the winter,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Makely urged, with an\r\nair of argument.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps the tramps are resting, too. At any rate, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think the sight\r\nof idleness in rags, and begging at back doors, is very corrupting to the\r\ncountry people; I never heard of a single tramp who had started from the\r\ncountry; they all come from the cities. It\xe2\x80\x99s the other kind of idleness\r\nthat tempts our young people. The only tramps that my son says he ever\r\nenvies are the well-dressed, strong young fellows from town that go\r\ntramping through the mountains for exercise every summer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe ladies both paused. They seemed to have got to the end of their\r\ntether; at least, Mrs. Makely had apparently nothing else to advance, and\r\nI said, lightly: \xe2\x80\x9cBut that is just the kind of tramps that Mr. Homos would\r\nmost disapprove of. He says that in Altruria they would consider exercise\r\nfor exercise\xe2\x80\x99 sake a wicked waste of force and little short of lunacy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI thought my exaggeration might provoke him to denial, but he seemed not\r\nto have found it unjust. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you know,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to Mrs. Camp, \xe2\x80\x9cin\r\nAltruria every one works with his hands, so that the hard work shall not\r\nall fall to any one class; and this manual labor of each is sufficient to\r\nkeep the body in health, as well as to earn a living. After the three,\r\nhours\xe2\x80\x99 work, which constitutes a day\xe2\x80\x99s work with us, is done, the young\r\npeople have all sorts of games and sports, and they carry them as late\r\ninto life as the temperament of each demands. But what I was saying to Mr.\r\nTwelvemough--perhaps I did not make myself clear--was that we should\r\nregard the sterile putting forth of strength in exercise, if others were\r\neach day worn out with hard manual labor, as insane or immoral. But I can\r\naccount for it differently with you, because I understand that in your\r\nconditions a person of leisure could not do any manual labor without\r\ntaking away the work of some one who needed it to live by; and could not\r\neven relieve an overworked laborer, and give him the money for the work,\r\nwithout teaching him habits of idleness. In Altruria we can all keep\r\nourselves well by doing each his share of hard work, and we can help those\r\nwho are exhausted, when such a thing happens, without injuring them\r\nmaterially or morally.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nYoung Camp entered at this moment, and the Altrurian hesitated. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, do go\r\non!\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Makely entreated. She added to Camp: \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve got him to talking\r\nabout Altruria at last, and we wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have him stopped for worlds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian looked around at all our faces, and no doubt read our eager\r\ncuriosity in them. He smiled and said: \xe2\x80\x9cI shall be very glad, I\xe2\x80\x99m sure.\r\nBut I do not think you will find anything so remarkable in our\r\ncivilization, if you will conceive of it as the outgrowth of the\r\nneighborly instinct. In fact, neighborliness is the essence of\r\nAltrurianism. If you will imagine having the same feeling toward all,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nexplained to Mrs. Makely, \xe2\x80\x9cas you have toward your next-door neighbor--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy next-door neighbor!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I don\xe2\x80\x99t _know_ the people next\r\ndoor! We live in a large apartment house, some forty families, and I\r\nassure you I do not know a soul among them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked at her with a puzzled air, and she continued: \xe2\x80\x9cSometimes it\r\n_does_ seem rather hard. One day the people on the same landing with us\r\nlost one of their children, and I should never have been a whit the wiser\r\nif my cook hadn\xe2\x80\x99t happened to mention it. The servants all know each\r\nother; they meet in the back elevator, and get acquainted. I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nencourage it. You can\xe2\x80\x99t tell what kind of families they belong to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut surely,\xe2\x80\x9d the Altrurian persisted, \xe2\x80\x9cyou have friends in the city whom\r\nyou think of as your neighbors?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I can\xe2\x80\x99t say that I have,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely. \xe2\x80\x9cI have my visiting-list,\r\nbut I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t think of anybody on _that_ as a neighbor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian looked so blank and baffled that I could hardly help\r\nlaughing. \xe2\x80\x9cThen I should not know how to explain Altruria to you, I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nafraid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d she returned, lightly, \xe2\x80\x9cif it\xe2\x80\x99s anything like neighborliness as\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve seen it in small places, deliver me from it! I like being\r\nindependent. That\xe2\x80\x99s why I like the city. You\xe2\x80\x99re let alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was down in New York once, and I went through some of the streets and\r\nhouses where the poor people live,\xe2\x80\x9d said young Camp, \xe2\x80\x9cand they seemed to\r\nknow each other and to be quite neighborly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd would you like to be all messed in with one another that way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n demanded the lady.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I thought it was better than living as we do in the country, so far\r\napart that we never see one another, hardly. And it seems to me better\r\nthan not having any neighbors at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, every one to his taste,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish you would tell\r\nus how people manage with you socially, Mr. Homos.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you know,\xe2\x80\x9d he began, \xe2\x80\x9cwe have neither city nor country in your\r\nsense, and so we are neither so isolated nor so crowded together. You feel\r\nthat you lose a great deal in not seeing one another oftener?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked\r\nCamp.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Folks rust out living alone. It\xe2\x80\x99s Human nature to want to get\r\ntogether.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I understand Mrs. Makely that it is human nature to want to keep\r\napart?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, but to come together independently,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that is what we have contrived in our life at home. I should have\r\nto say, in the first place, that--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me just one moment, Mr. Homos,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely. This perverse\r\nwoman was as anxious to hear about Altruria as any of us, but she was a\r\nwoman who would rather hear the sound of her own voice than any other,\r\neven if she were dying, as she would call it, to hear the other. The\r\nAltrurian stopped politely, and Mrs. Makely went on: \xe2\x80\x9cI have been thinking\r\nof what Mr. Camp was saying about the blacklisted men, and their all\r\nturning into tramps--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I didn\xe2\x80\x99t say that, Mrs. Makely,\xe2\x80\x9d the young fellow protested, in\r\nastonishment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it stands to reason that if the tramps have all been blacklisted\r\nmen--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I didn\xe2\x80\x99t say that, either.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo matter! What I am trying to get at is this: if a workman has made\r\nhimself a nuisance to the employers, haven\xe2\x80\x99t they a right to punish him in\r\nany way they can?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe there\xe2\x80\x99s no law yet against blacklisting,\xe2\x80\x9d said Camp.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, then, I don\xe2\x80\x99t see what they\xe2\x80\x99ve got to complain of. The\r\nemployers surely know their own business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey claim to know the men\xe2\x80\x99s, too. That\xe2\x80\x99s what they\xe2\x80\x99re always saying;\r\nthey will manage their own affairs in their own way. But no man, or\r\ncompany, that does business on a large scale has any affairs that are not\r\npartly other folks\xe2\x80\x99 affairs, too. All the saying in the world won\xe2\x80\x99t make\r\nit different.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, with a force of argument which she\r\nseemed to think was irresistible, \xe2\x80\x9cI think the workmen had better leave\r\nthings to the employers, and then they won\xe2\x80\x99t get blacklisted. It\xe2\x80\x99s as\r\nbroad as it\xe2\x80\x99s long.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI confess that, although I agreed with Mrs. Makely in regard to what the\r\nworkmen had better do, her position had been arrived at by such\r\nextraordinary reasoning that I blushed for her; at the same time, I wanted\r\nto laugh. She continued, triumphantly: \xe2\x80\x9cYou see, the employers have ever\r\nso much more at stake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen men have everything at stake--the work of their hands,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nyoung fellow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but surely,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely, \xe2\x80\x9cyou wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t set that against\r\ncapital? You wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t compare the two?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I should,\xe2\x80\x9d said Camp, and I could see his eye kindle and his jaw\r\nstiffen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I suppose you would say that a man ought to get as much for his work\r\nas an employer gets for his capital. If you think one has as much at stake\r\nas the other, you must think they ought to be paid alike.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is _just_ what I think,\xe2\x80\x9d said Camp, and Mrs. Makely burst into a\r\npeal of amiable laughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, that is too preposterous!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy is it preposterous?\xe2\x80\x9d he demanded, with a quivering nostril.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, simply because it _is_\xe2\x80\x9d said the lady, but she did not say why, and\r\nalthough I thought so, too, I was glad she did not attempt to do it, for\r\nher conclusions seemed to me much better than her reasons.\r\n\r\nThe old wooden clock in the kitchen began to strike, and she rose briskly\r\nto her feet and went and laid the books she had been holding in her lap on\r\nthe table beside Mrs. Camp\xe2\x80\x99s bed. \xe2\x80\x9cWe must really be going,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, as\r\nshe leaned over and kissed the invalid. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is your dinner-time, and we\r\nshall barely get back for lunch if we go by the Loop road; and I want very\r\nmuch to have Mr. Homos see the Witch\xe2\x80\x99s Falls on the way. I have got two or\r\nthree of the books here that Mr. Makely brought me last night--I sha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhave time to read them at once--and I\xe2\x80\x99m smuggling in one of Mr.\r\nTwelvemough\xe2\x80\x99s, that he\xe2\x80\x99s too modest to present for himself.\xe2\x80\x9d She turned a\r\ngay glance upon me, and Mrs. Camp thanked me, and a number of civilities\r\nfollowed from all sides. In the process of their exchange, Mrs. Makely\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nspirits perceptibly rose, and she came away in high good-humor with the\r\nwhole Camp family. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, now, I am sure,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to the Altrurian, as\r\nwe began the long ascent of the Loop road, \xe2\x80\x9cyou must allow that you have\r\nseen some very original characters. But how _warped_ people get living\r\nalone so much! That is the great drawback of the country. Mrs. Camp thinks\r\nthe savings-bank did her a real injury in taking a mortgage on her place,\r\nand Reuben seems to have seen just enough of the outside world to get it\r\nall wrong. But they are the best-hearted creatures in the world, and I\r\nknow you won\xe2\x80\x99t misunderstand them. That unsparing country bluntness--don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou think it\xe2\x80\x99s perfectly delightful? I do like to stir poor Reuben up, and\r\nget him talking. He is a good boy, if he _is_ so wrong-headed, and he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthe most devoted son and brother in the world. Very few young fellows\r\nwould waste their lives on an old farm like that; I suppose, when his\r\nmother dies, he will marry and strike out for himself in some growing\r\nplace.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe did not seem to think the world held out any very bright inducements\r\nfor him to leave home,\xe2\x80\x9d the Altrurian suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, let him get one of these lively, pushing Yankee girls for a wife, and\r\nhe will think very differently,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Makely.\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian disappeared that afternoon, and I saw little or nothing of\r\nhim till the next day at supper. Then he said he had been spending the\r\ntime with young Camp, who had shown him something of the farm-work, and\r\nintroduced him to several of the neighbors; he was very much interested in\r\nit all, because at home he was, at present, engaged in farm-work himself,\r\nand he was curious to contrast the American and Altrurian methods. We\r\nbegan to talk of the farming interest again, later in the day, when the\r\nmembers of our little group came together, and I told them what the\r\nAltrurian had been doing. The doctor had been suddenly called back to\r\ntown; but the minister was there, and the lawyer and the professor and the\r\nbanker and the manufacturer.\r\n\r\nIt was the banker who began to comment on what I said, and he seemed to be\r\nin the frank humor of the Saturday night before. \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nhard life, and they have to look sharp if they expect to make both ends\r\nmeet. I would not like to undertake it myself with their resources.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe professor smiled, in asking the Altrurian: \xe2\x80\x9cDid your agricultural\r\nfriends tell you anything of the little rural traffic in votes that they\r\ncarry on about election time? That is one of the side means they have of\r\nmaking both ends meet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you know, you can buy votes among our virtuous yeomen from two\r\ndollars up at the ordinary elections. When party feeling runs high, and\r\nthere are vital questions at stake, the votes cost more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian looked round at us all aghast. \xe2\x80\x9cDo, you mean that Americans\r\nbuy votes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe professor smiled again. \xe2\x80\x9cOh no; I only mean that they sell them. Well,\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t wonder that they rather prefer to blink the fact; but it is a\r\nfact, nevertheless, and pretty notorious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood heavens!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Altrurian. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd what defence have they for such\r\ntreason? I don\xe2\x80\x99t mean those who sell; from what I have seen of the\r\nbareness and hardship of their lives, I could well imagine that there\r\nmight sometimes come a pinch when they would be glad of the few dollars\r\nthat they could get in that way; but what have those who buy to say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said the professor, \xe2\x80\x9cit isn\xe2\x80\x99t a transaction that\xe2\x80\x99s apt to be\r\ntalked about much on either side.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think,\xe2\x80\x9d the banker interposed, \xe2\x80\x9cthat there is some exaggeration about\r\nthat business; but it certainly exists, and I suppose it is a growing evil\r\nin the country. I fancy it arises, somewhat, from a want of, clear\r\nthinking on the subject. Then there is no doubt but it comes, sometimes,\r\nfrom poverty. A man sells his vote, as a woman sells her person, for\r\nmoney, when neither can turn virtue into cash. They feel that they must\r\nlive, and neither of them would be satisfied if Dr. Johnson told them he\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t see the necessity. In fact, I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t myself, if I were in their\r\nplaces. You can\xe2\x80\x99t have the good of a civilization like ours without having\r\nthe bad; but I am not going to deny that the bad is bad. Some people like\r\nto do that; but I don\xe2\x80\x99t find my account in it. In either case, I confess\r\nthat I think the buyer is worse than the seller--incomparably worse. I\r\nsuppose you are not troubled with either case in Altruria?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, with an utter horror, which no repetition of\r\nhis words can give the sense of. \xe2\x80\x9cIt would be unimaginable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill,\xe2\x80\x9d the banker suggested, \xe2\x80\x9cyou have cakes and ale, and at times the\r\nginger is hot in the mouth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t pretend that we have immunity from error; but upon such terms as\r\nyou have described we have none. It would be impossible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian\xe2\x80\x99s voice expressed no contempt, but only a sad patience, a\r\nmelancholy surprise, such as a celestial angel might feel in being\r\nsuddenly confronted with some secret shame and horror of the Pit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker, \xe2\x80\x9cwith us the only way is to take the business\r\nview and try to strike an average somewhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTalking of business,\xe2\x80\x9d said the professor, turning to the manufacturer,\r\nwho had been quietly smoking, \xe2\x80\x9cwhy don\xe2\x80\x99t some of you capitalists take hold\r\nof farming here in the East, and make a business of it as they do in the\r\nWest?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you,\xe2\x80\x9d said the other; \xe2\x80\x9cif you mean me, I would rather not invest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n He was silent a moment, and then he went on, as if the notion were\r\nbeginning to win upon him: \xe2\x80\x9cIt may come to something like that, though. If\r\nit does, the natural course, I should think, would be through the\r\nrailroads. It would be a very easy matter for them to buy up all the good\r\nfarms along their lines and put tenants on them, and run them in their own\r\ninterest. Really, it isn\xe2\x80\x99t a bad scheme. The waste in the present method\r\nis enormous, and there is no reason why the roads should not own the\r\nfarms, as they are beginning to own the mines. They could manage them\r\nbetter than the small farmers do in every way. I wonder the thing hasn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\noccurred to some smart railroad man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe all laughed a little, perceiving the semi-ironical spirit of his talk;\r\nbut the Altrurian must have taken it in dead earnest: \xe2\x80\x9cBut, in that case,\r\nthe number of people thrown out of work would be very great, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\r\nAnd what would become of them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, they would have whatever their farms brought to make a new start\r\nwith somewhere else; and, besides, that question of what would become of\r\npeople thrown out of work by a given improvement is something that capital\r\ncannot consider. We used to introduce a bit of machinery in the mill,\r\nevery now and then, that threw out a dozen or a hundred people; but we\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t stop for that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you never knew what became of them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSometimes. Generally not. We took it for granted that they would light on\r\ntheir feet somehow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the state--the whole people--the government--did nothing for them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it became a question of the poor-house, yes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr the jail,\xe2\x80\x9d the lawyer suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeaking of the poor-house,\xe2\x80\x9d said the professor, \xe2\x80\x9cdid our exemplary rural\r\nfriends tell you how they sell out their paupers to the lowest bidder, and\r\nget them boarded sometimes as low as a dollar and a quarter a week?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, young Mr. Camp told me of that. He seemed to think it was terrible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid he? Well, I\xe2\x80\x99m glad to hear that of young Mr. Camp. From all that I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nbeen told before, he seems to reserve his conscience for the use of\r\ncapitalists. What does he propose to do about it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe seems to think the state ought to find work for them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, paternalism! Well, I guess the state won\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was his opinion, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems a hard fate,\xe2\x80\x9d said the minister, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the only provision the\r\nlaw makes for people who are worn out by sickness or a life of work should\r\nbe something that assorts them with idiots and lunatics, and brings such\r\nshame upon them that it is almost as terrible as death.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is the only way to encourage independence and individuality,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nprofessor. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course, it has its dark side. But anything else would be\r\nsentimental and unbusinesslike, and, in fact, un-American.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not so sure that it would be un-Christian,\xe2\x80\x9d the minister timidly\r\nventured, in the face of such an authority on political economy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, as to that, I must leave the question to the reverend clergy,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nthe professor.\r\n\r\nA very unpleasant little silence followed. It was broken by the lawyer,\r\nwho put his feet together, and, after a glance down at them, began to say:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was very much interested this afternoon by a conversation I had with\r\nsome of the young fellows in the hotel. You know most of them are\r\ngraduates, and they are taking a sort of supernumerary vacation this\r\nsummer before they plunge into the battle of life in the autumn. They were\r\ntalking of some other fellows, classmates of theirs, who were not so\r\nlucky, but had been obliged to begin the fight at once. It seems that our\r\nfellows here are all going in for some sort of profession: medicine or law\r\nor engineering or teaching or the church, and they were commiserating\r\nthose other fellows not only because they were not having the\r\nsupernumerary vacation, but because they were going into business. That\r\nstruck me as rather odd, and I tried to find out what it meant, and, as\r\nnearly as I could find out, it meant that most college graduates would not\r\ngo into business if they could help it. They seemed to feel a sort of\r\nincongruity between their education and the business life. They pitied the\r\nfellows that had to go in for it, and apparently the fellows that had to\r\ngo in for it pitied themselves, for the talk seemed to have begun about a\r\nletter that one of the chaps here had got from poor Jack or Jim somebody,\r\nwho had been obliged to go into his father\xe2\x80\x99s business, and was groaning\r\nover it. The fellows who were going to study professions were hugging\r\nthemselves at the contrast between their fate and his, and were making\r\nremarks about business that were, to say the least, unbusinesslike. A few\r\nyears ago we should have made a summary disposition of the matter, and I\r\nbelieve some of the newspapers still are in doubt about the value of a\r\ncollege education to men who have got to make their way. What do you\r\nthink?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lawyer addressed his question to the manufacturer, who answered, with\r\na comfortable satisfaction, that he did not think those young men if they\r\nwent into business would find that they knew too much.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut they pointed out,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lawyer, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the great American fortunes\r\nhad been made by men who had never had their educational advantages, and\r\nthey seemed to think that what we call the education of a gentleman was a\r\nlittle too good for money-making purposes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said the other, \xe2\x80\x9cthey can console themselves with the reflection\r\nthat going into business isn\xe2\x80\x99t necessarily making money; it isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nnecessarily making a living, even.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome of them seem to have caught on to that fact; and they pitied Jack or\r\nJim partly because the chances were so much against him. But they pitied\r\nhim mostly because in the life before him he would have no use for his\r\nacademic training, and he had better not gone to college at all. They said\r\nhe would be none the better for it, and would always be miserable when he\r\nlooked back to it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe manufacturer did not reply, and the professor, after a preliminary\r\nhemming, held his peace. It was the banker who took the word: \xe2\x80\x9cWell, so\r\nfar as business is concerned, they were right. It is no use to pretend\r\nthat there is any relation between business and the higher education.\r\nThere is no business man who will pretend that there is not often an\r\nactual incompatibility if he is honest. I know that when we get together\r\nat a commercial or financial dinner we talk as if great merchants and\r\ngreat financiers were beneficent geniuses, who evoked the prosperity of\r\nmankind by their schemes from the conditions that would otherwise have\r\nremained barren. Well, very likely they are, but we must all confess that\r\nthey do not know it at the time. What they are consciously looking out for\r\nthen is the main chance. If general prosperity follows, all well and good;\r\nthey are willing to be given the credit for it. But, as I said, with\r\nbusiness as business, the \xe2\x80\x98education of a gentleman\xe2\x80\x99 has nothing to do.\r\nThat education is always putting the old Ciceronian question: whether the\r\nfellow arriving at a starving city with a cargo of grain is bound to tell\r\nthe people before he squeezes them that there are half a dozen other\r\nfellows with grain just below the horizon. As a gentleman he would have to\r\ntell them, because he could not take advantage of their necessities; but,\r\nas a business man, he would think it bad business to tell them, or no\r\nbusiness at all. The principle goes all through; I say, business is\r\nbusiness; and I am not going to pretend that business will ever be\r\nanything else. In our business battles we don\xe2\x80\x99t take off our hats to the\r\nother side and say, \xe2\x80\x98Gentlemen of the French Guard, have the goodness to\r\nfire.\xe2\x80\x99 That may be war, but it is not business. We seize all the\r\nadvantages we can; very few of us would actually deceive; but if a fellow\r\nbelieves a thing, and we know he is wrong, we do not usually take the\r\ntrouble to set him right, if we are going to lose anything by undeceiving\r\nhim. That would not be business. I suppose you think that is dreadful?\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nturned smilingly to the minister.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish--I wish,\xe2\x80\x9d said the minister, gently, \xe2\x80\x9cit could be otherwise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I wish so, too,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the banker. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it isn\xe2\x80\x99t. Am I right or\r\nam I wrong?\xe2\x80\x9d he demanded of the manufacturer, who laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not conducting this discussion. I will not deprive you of the\r\nfloor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat you say,\xe2\x80\x9d I ventured to put in, \xe2\x80\x9creminds me of the experience of a\r\nfriend of mine, a brother novelist. He wrote a story where the failure of\r\na business man turned on a point just like that you have instanced. The\r\nman could have retrieved himself if he had let some people believe that\r\nwhat was so was not so, but his conscience stepped in and obliged him to\r\nown the truth. There was a good deal of talk about the case, I suppose,\r\nbecause it was not in real life, and my friend heard divers criticisms. He\r\nheard of a group of ministers who blamed him for exalting a case of common\r\nhonesty, as if it were something extraordinary; and he heard of some\r\nbusiness men who talked it over and said he had worked the case up\r\nsplendidly, but he was all wrong in the outcome--the fellow would never\r\nhave told the other fellows. They said it would not have been business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe all laughed except the minister and the Altrurian; the manufacturer\r\nsaid: \xe2\x80\x9cTwenty-five years hence, the fellow who is going into business may\r\npity the fellows who are pitying him for his hard fate now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery possibly, but not necessarily,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course, the\r\nbusiness man is on top, as far as money goes; he is the fellow who makes\r\nthe big fortunes; the millionaire lawyers and doctors and ministers are\r\nexceptional. But his risks are tremendous. Ninety-five times out of a\r\nhundred he fails. To be sure, he picks up and goes on, but he seldom gets\r\nthere, after all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen in your system,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, \xe2\x80\x9cthe great majority of those\r\nwho go into what you call the battle of life are defeated?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe killed, wounded, and missing sum up a frightful total,\xe2\x80\x9d the banker\r\nadmitted. \xe2\x80\x9cBut whatever the end is, there is a great deal of prosperity on\r\nthe way. The statistics are correct, but they do not tell the whole truth.\r\nIt is not so bad as it seems. Still, simply looking at the material\r\nchances, I don\xe2\x80\x99t blame those young fellows for not wanting to go into\r\nbusiness. And when you come to other considerations! We used to cut the\r\nknot of the difficulty pretty sharply; we said a college education was\r\nwrong, or the hot and hot American spread-eaglers did. Business is the\r\nnational ideal, and the successful business man is the American type. It\r\nis a business man\xe2\x80\x99s country.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, if I understand you,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian, \xe2\x80\x9cand I am very anxious to\r\nhave a clear understanding of the matter, the effect of the university\r\nwith you is to unfit a youth for business life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no. It may give him great advantages in it, and that is the theory and\r\nexpectation of most fathers who send their sons to the university. But,\r\nundoubtedly, the effect is to render business life distasteful. The\r\nuniversity nurtures all sorts of lofty ideals, which business has no use\r\nfor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen the effect is undemocratic?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it is simply unbusinesslike. The boy is a better democrat when he\r\nleaves college than he will be later, if he goes into business. The\r\nuniversity has taught him and equipped him to use his own gifts and powers\r\nfor his advancement; but the first lesson of business, and the last, is to\r\nuse other men\xe2\x80\x99s gifts and powers. If he looks about him at all, he sees\r\nthat no man gets rich simply by his own labor, no matter how mighty a\r\ngenius he is, and that, if you want to get rich, you must make other men\r\nwork for you, and pay you for the privilege of doing so. Isn\xe2\x80\x99t that true?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe banker turned to the manufacturer with this question, and the other\r\nsaid: \xe2\x80\x9cThe theory is, that we give people work,\xe2\x80\x9d and they both laughed.\r\n\r\nThe minister said: \xe2\x80\x9cI believe that in Altruria no man works for the profit\r\nof another?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; each works for the profit of all,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the Altrurian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker, \xe2\x80\x9cyou seem to have made it go. Nobody can deny\r\nthat. But we couldn\xe2\x80\x99t make it go here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy? I am very curious to know why our system seems so impossible to\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it is contrary to the American spirit. It is alien to our love of\r\nindividuality.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we prize individuality, too, and we think we secure it under our\r\nsystem. Under yours, it seems to me that while the individuality of the\r\nman who makes other men work for him is safe, except from itself, the\r\nindividuality of the workers--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that is their lookout. We have found that, upon the whole, it is\r\nbest to let every man look out for himself. I know that, in a certain\r\nlight, the result has an ugly aspect; but, nevertheless, in spite of all,\r\nthe country is enormously prosperous. The pursuit of happiness, which is\r\none of the inalienable rights secured to us by the Declaration, is, and\r\nalways has been, a dream; but the pursuit of the dollar yields tangible\r\nproceeds, and we get a good deal of excitement out of it as it goes on.\r\nYou can\xe2\x80\x99t deny that we are the richest nation in the world. Do you call\r\nAltruria a rich country?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI could not quite make out whether the banker was serious or not in all\r\nthis talk; sometimes I suspected him of a fine mockery, but the Altrurian\r\ntook him upon the surface of his words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hardly know whether it is or not. The question of wealth does not enter\r\ninto our scheme. I can say that we all have enough, and that no one is\r\neven in the fear of want.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that is very well. But we should think it was paying too much for it\r\nif we had to give up the hope of ever having more than we wanted,\xe2\x80\x9d and at\r\nthis point the banker uttered his jolly laugh, and I perceived that he had\r\nbeen trying to draw the Altrurian out and practise upon his patriotism. It\r\nwas a great relief to find that he had been joking in so much that seemed\r\na dead give-away of our economical position. \xe2\x80\x9cIn Altruria,\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, \xe2\x80\x9cwho\r\nis your ideal great man? I don\xe2\x80\x99t mean personally, but abstractly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian thought a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cWith us there is so little ambition for\r\ndistinction, as you understand it, that your question is hard to answer.\r\nBut I should say, speaking largely, that it was some man who had been able\r\nfor the time being to give the greatest happiness to the greatest\r\nnumber--some artist or poet or inventor or physician.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was somewhat surprised to have the banker take this preposterous\r\nstatement seriously, respectfully. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, that is quite conceivable with\r\nyour system. What should you say,\xe2\x80\x9d he demanded of the rest of us\r\ngenerally, \xe2\x80\x9cwas our ideal of greatness?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo one replied at once, or at all, till the manufacturer said: \xe2\x80\x9cWe will\r\nlet you continue to run it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it is a very curious inquiry, and I have thought it over a good\r\ndeal. I should say that within a generation our ideal has changed twice.\r\nBefore the war, and during all the time from the Revolution onward, it was\r\nundoubtedly the great politician, the publicist, the statesman. As we grew\r\nolder and began to have an intellectual life of our own, I think the\r\nliterary fellows had a pretty good share of the honors that were going--that\r\nis, such a man as Longfellow was popularly considered a type of\r\ngreatness. When the war came, it brought the soldier to the front, and\r\nthere was a period of ten or fifteen years when he dominated the national\r\nimagination. That period passed, and the great era of material prosperity\r\nset in. The big fortunes began to tower up, and heroes of another sort\r\nbegan to appeal to our admiration. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think there is any doubt but\r\nthe millionaire is now the American ideal. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t very pleasant to think\r\nso, even for people who have got on, but it can\xe2\x80\x99t very hopefully be\r\ndenied. It is the man with the most money who now takes the prize in our\r\nnational cake-walk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Altrurian turned curiously toward me, and I did my best to tell him\r\nwhat a cake-walk was. When I had finished, the banker resumed, only to\r\nsay, as he rose from his chair to bid us good-night: \xe2\x80\x9cIn any average\r\nassembly of Americans the greatest millionaire would take the eyes of all\r\nfrom the greatest statesman, the greatest poet, or the greatest soldier we\r\never had. That,\xe2\x80\x9d he added to the Altrurian, \xe2\x80\x9cwill account to you for many\r\nthings as you travel through our country.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIX\r\n\r\n\r\nThe next time the members of our little group came together, the\r\nmanufacturer began at once upon the banker:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think that our friend the professor, here, would hardly like\r\nthat notion of yours, that business, as business, has nothing to do with\r\nthe education of a gentleman. If this is a business man\xe2\x80\x99s country, and if\r\nthe professor has nothing in stock but the sort of education that business\r\nhas no use for, I should suppose that he would want to go into some other\r\nline.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe banker mutely referred the matter to the professor, who said, with\r\nthat cold grin of his which I hated:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps we shall wait for business to purge and live cleanly. Then it\r\nwill have some use for the education of a gentleman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I have touched the quick in both of you,\r\nwhen I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t the least notion of doing so. But I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t really like to\r\nprophesy which will adapt itself to the other--education or business. Let\r\nus hope there will be mutual concessions. There are some pessimists who\r\nsay that business methods, especially on the large scale of the trusts and\r\ncombinations, have grown worse instead of better; but this may be merely\r\nwhat is called a \xe2\x80\x98transition state.\xe2\x80\x99 Hamlet must be cruel to be kind; the\r\ndarkest hour comes before dawn--and so on. Perhaps when business gets\r\nthe whole affair of life into its hands, and runs the republic, as its\r\nenemies now accuse it of doing, the process of purging and living cleanly\r\nwill begin. I have known lots of fellows who started in life rather\r\nscampishly; but when they felt secure of themselves, and believed that\r\nthey could afford to be honest, they became so. There\xe2\x80\x99s no reason why the\r\nsame thing shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t happen on a large scale. We must never forget that we\r\nare still a very novel experiment, though we have matured so rapidly in\r\nsome respects that we have come to regard ourselves as an accomplished\r\nfact. We are really less so than we were forty years ago, with all the\r\ntremendous changes since the war. Before that we could take certain\r\nmatters for granted. If a man got out of work, he turned his hand to\r\nsomething else; if a man failed in business, he started in again from some\r\nother direction; as a last resort, in both cases, he went West, pre-empted\r\na quarter-section of public land, and grew up with the country. Now the\r\ncountry is grown up; the public land is gone; business is full on all\r\nsides, and the hand that turned itself to something else has lost its\r\ncunning. The struggle for life has changed from a free-fight to an\r\nencounter of disciplined forces, and the free-fighters that are left get\r\nground to pieces between organized labor and organized capital. Decidedly,\r\nwe are in a transition state, and if the higher education tried to adapt\r\nitself to business needs, there are chances that it might sacrifice itself\r\nwithout helping business. After all, how much education does business\r\nneed? Were our great fortunes made by educated men, or men of university\r\ntraining? I don\xe2\x80\x99t know but these young fellows are right about that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that may all be,\xe2\x80\x9d I put in. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it seems to me that you give Mr.\r\nHomos, somehow, a wrong impression of our economic life by your\r\ngeneralizations. You are a Harvard man yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and I am not a rich man. A million or two, more or less; but what is\r\nthat? I have suffered, at the start and all along, from the question as to\r\nwhat a man with the education of a gentleman ought to do in such and such\r\na juncture. The fellows who have not that sort of education have not that\r\nsort of question, and they go in and win.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you admit, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said the professor, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the higher education\r\nelevates a business man\xe2\x80\x99s standard of morals?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUndoubtedly. That is one of its chief drawbacks,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker, with a\r\nlaugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, with the deference due even to a man who had only a\r\nmillion or two, more or less, \xe2\x80\x9cwe must allow _you_ to say such things. But\r\nif the case is so bad with the business men who have made the great\r\nfortunes--the business men who have never had the disadvantage of a\r\nuniversity education--I wish you would explain to Mr. Homos why, in every\r\npublic exigency, we instinctively appeal to the business sense of the\r\ncommunity as if it were the fountain of wisdom, probity, and equity.\r\nSuppose there were some question of vital interest--I won\xe2\x80\x99t say financial,\r\nbut political or moral or social--on which it was necessary to rouse\r\npublic opinion, what would be the first thing to do? To call a meeting\r\nover the signatures of the leading business men, because no other names\r\nappeal with such force to the public. You might get up a call signed by\r\nall the novelists, artists, ministers, lawyers, and doctors in the state,\r\nand it would not have a tithe of the effect, with the people at large,\r\nthat a call signed by a few leading merchants, bank presidents, railroad\r\nmen, and trust officers would have. What is the reason? It seems strange\r\nthat I should be asking you to defend yourself against yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all, my dear fellow, not at all,\xe2\x80\x9d the banker replied, with his\r\ncaressing bonhomie. \xe2\x80\x9cThough I will confess, to begin with, that I do not\r\nexpect to answer your question to your entire satisfaction. I can only do\r\nmy best--on the instalment plan.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe turned to the Altrurian, and then went on: \xe2\x80\x9cAs I said the other night,\r\nthis is a business man\xe2\x80\x99s country. We are a purely commercial people; money\r\nis absolutely to the fore; and business, which is the means of getting the\r\nmost money, is the American ideal. If you like, you may call it the\r\nAmerican fetish; I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind calling it so myself. The fact that business\r\nis our ideal, or our fetish, will account for the popular faith in\r\nbusiness men, who form its priesthood, its hierarchy. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\r\nmyself, any other reason for regarding business men as solider than\r\nnovelists or artists or ministers, not to mention lawyers and doctors.\r\nThey are supposed to have long heads; but it appears that ninety-five\r\ntimes out of a hundred they haven\xe2\x80\x99t. They are supposed to be very\r\nreliable; but it is almost invariably a business man of some sort who gets\r\nout to Canada while the state examiner is balancing his books, and it is\r\nusually the longest headed business men who get plundered by him. No, it\r\nis simply because business is our national ideal that the business man is\r\nhonored above all other men among us. In the aristocratic countries they\r\nforward a public object under the patronage of the nobility and gentry; in\r\na plutocratic country they get the business men to indorse it. I suppose\r\nthat the average American citizen feels that they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t indorse a thing\r\nunless it was safe; and the average American citizen likes to be safe--he\r\nis cautious. As a matter of fact, business men are always taking risks,\r\nand business is a game of chance, in a certain degree. Have I made myself\r\nintelligible?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEntirely so,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian; and he seemed so thoroughly well\r\nsatisfied that he forbore asking any further question.\r\n\r\nNo one else spoke. The banker lighted a cigar, and resumed at the point\r\nwhere he left off when I ventured to enter upon the defence of his class\r\nwith him. I must say that he had not convinced me at all. At that moment\r\nI would rather have trusted him, in any serious matter of practical\r\nconcern, than all the novelists I ever heard of. But I thought I would\r\nleave the word to him, without further attempt to reinstate him in his\r\nself-esteem. In fact, he seemed to be getting along very well without it,\r\nor else he was feeling that mysterious control from the Altrurian which I\r\nhad already suspected him of using. Voluntarily or involuntarily, the\r\nbanker proceeded with his contribution to the Altrurian\xe2\x80\x99s stock of\r\nknowledge concerning our civilization:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe, however, that the higher education is any more of a\r\nfailure, as a provision for a business career, than the lower education is\r\nfor the life of labor. I suppose that the hypercritical observer might say\r\nthat in a wholly commercial civilization like ours the business man really\r\nneeded nothing beyond the three R\xe2\x80\x99s, and the working-man needed no R at\r\nall. As a practical affair, there is a good deal to be said in favor of\r\nthat view. The higher education is part of the social ideal which we have\r\nderived from the past, from Europe. It is part of the provision for the\r\nlife of leisure, the life of the aristocrat, which nobody of our\r\ngeneration leads, except women. Our women really have some use for the\r\neducation of a gentleman, but our men have none. How will that do for a\r\ngeneralization?\xe2\x80\x9d the banker asked of me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d I admitted, with a laugh, \xe2\x80\x9cit is a good deal like one of my own. I\r\nhave always been struck with that phase of our civilization.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then,\xe2\x80\x9d the banker resumed, \xe2\x80\x9ctake the lower education. That is part\r\nof the civic ideal which, I suppose, I may say we evolved from the depths\r\nof our inner consciousness of what an American citizen ought to be. It\r\nincludes instruction in all the R\xe2\x80\x99s, and in several other letters of the\r\nalphabet. It is given free by the state, and no one can deny that it is\r\nthoroughly socialistic in conception and application.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDistinctly so,\xe2\x80\x9d said the professor. \xe2\x80\x9cNow that the text-books are\r\nfurnished by the state, we have only to go a step further and provide a\r\ngood, hot lunch for the children every day, as they do in Paris.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d the banker returned, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I should have much to say\r\nagainst that. It seems as reasonable as anything in the system of\r\neducation which we force upon the working classes. _They_ know perfectly\r\nwell, whether we do or not, that the three R\xe2\x80\x99s will not make their\r\nchildren better mechanics or laborers, and that, if the fight for a mere\r\nliving is to go on from generation to generation, they will have no\r\nleisure to apply the little learning they get in the public schools for\r\ntheir personal culture. In the mean time we deprive the parents of their\r\nchildren\xe2\x80\x99s labor, in order that they may be better citizens for their\r\nschooling, as we imagine; I don\xe2\x80\x99t know whether they are or not. We offer\r\nthem no sort of compensation for their time, and I think we ought to feel\r\nobliged to them for not wanting wages for their children while we are\r\nteaching them to be better citizens.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know,\xe2\x80\x9d said the professor, \xe2\x80\x9cthat has been suggested by some of their\r\nleaders.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, really? Well, that is too good!\xe2\x80\x9d The banker threw back his head and\r\nroared, and we all laughed with him. When we had sobered down again, he\r\nsaid: \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose that when a working-man makes all the use he can of his\r\nlower education he becomes a business man, and then he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t need the\r\nhigher. Professor, you seem to be left out in the cold by our system,\r\nwhichever way you take it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said the professor, \xe2\x80\x9cthe law of supply and demand works both ways:\r\nit creates the demand, if the supply comes first; and if we keep on giving\r\nthe sons of business men the education of a gentleman, we may yet make\r\nthem feel the need of it. We shall evolve a new sort of business man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe sort that can\xe2\x80\x99t make money, or wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t exactly like to, on some\r\nterms?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the banker. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, perhaps we shall work out our democratic\r\nsalvation in that way. When you have educated your new business man to the\r\npoint where he can\xe2\x80\x99t consent to get rich at the obvious cost of others,\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve got him on the way back to work with his hands. He will sink into\r\nthe ranks of labor, and give the fellow with the lower education a chance.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve no doubt he\xe2\x80\x99ll take it. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know but you\xe2\x80\x99re right, professor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lawyer had not spoken as yet. Now he said: \xe2\x80\x9cThen it is education,\r\nafter all, that is to bridge the chasm between the classes and the masses,\r\nthough it seems destined to go a long way around about it. There was a\r\ntime, I believe, when we expected religion to do that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it may still be doing it, for all I know,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\r\ndo you say?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, turning to the minister. \xe2\x80\x9cYou ought to be able to\r\ngive us some statistics on the subject with that large congregation of\r\nyours. You preach to more people than any other pulpit in your city.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe banker named one of the principal cities in the East, and the minister\r\nanswered, with, modest pride: \xe2\x80\x9cI am not sure of that; but our society is\r\ncertainly a very large one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, and how many of the lower classes are there in it--people who work\r\nfor their living with their hands?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe minister stirred uneasily in his chair, and at last he said, with\r\nevident unhappiness: \xe2\x80\x9cThey--I suppose--they have their own churches. I\r\nhave never thought that such a separation of the classes was right; and I\r\nhave had some of the very best people--socially and financially--with me\r\nin the wish that there might be more brotherliness between the rich and\r\npoor among us. But as yet--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stopped; the banker pursued: \xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean there are _no_ working-people\r\nin your congregation?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot think of any,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the minister, so miserably that the\r\nbanker forbore to press the point.\r\n\r\nThe lawyer broke the awkward pause which followed: \xe2\x80\x9cI have heard it\r\nasserted that there is no country in the world where the separation of the\r\nclasses is so absolute as in ours. In fact, I once heard a Russian\r\nrevolutionist, who had lived in exile all over Europe, say that he had\r\nnever seen anywhere such a want of kindness or sympathy between rich and\r\npoor as he had observed in America. I doubted whether he was right. But he\r\nbelieved that, if it ever came to the industrial revolution with us, the\r\nfight would be more uncompromising than any such fight that the world had\r\never seen. There was no respect from low to high, he said, and no\r\nconsideration from high to low, as there were in countries with traditions\r\nand old associations.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker, \xe2\x80\x9cthere may be something in that. Certainly, so\r\nfar as the two forces have come into conflict here, there has been no\r\ndisposition, on either side, to \xe2\x80\x98make war with the water of roses.\xe2\x80\x99 It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nastonishing, in fact, to see how ruthless the fellows who have just got up\r\nare toward the fellows who are still down. And the best of us have been up\r\nonly a generation or two--and the fellows who are still down know it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what do you think would be the outcome of such a conflict?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked,\r\nwith my soul divided between fear of it and the perception of its\r\nexcellence as material. My fancy vividly sketched the outline of a story\r\nwhich should forecast the struggle and its event, somewhat on the plan of\r\nthe Battle of Dorking.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe should beat,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker, breaking his cigar-ash off with his\r\nlittle finger; and I instantly cast him, with his ironic calm, for the\r\npart of a great patrician leader in my \xe2\x80\x9cFall of the Republic.\xe2\x80\x9d Of course,\r\nI disguised him somewhat, and travestied his worldly bonhomie with the\r\nbluff sang-froid of the soldier; these things are easily done.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat makes you think we should beat?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the manufacturer, with a\r\ncertain curiosity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, all the good jingo reasons: we have got the materials for beating.\r\nThose fellows throw away their strength whenever they begin to fight, and\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99ve been so badly generalled, up to the present time, that they have\r\nwanted to fight at the outset of every quarrel. They have been beaten in\r\nevery quarrel, but still they always want to begin by fighting. That is\r\nall right. When they have learned enough to begin by _voting_, then we\r\nshall have to look out. But if they keep on fighting, and always putting\r\nthemselves in the wrong and getting the worst of it, perhaps we can fix\r\nthe voting so we needn\xe2\x80\x99t be any more afraid of that than we are of the\r\nfighting. It\xe2\x80\x99s astonishing how short-sighted they are. They, have no\r\nconception of any cure for their grievances except more wages and fewer\r\nhours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut,\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you really think they have any just grievances?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course not, as a business man,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I were a\r\nworking-man, I should probably think differently. But we will suppose, for\r\nthe sake of argument, that their day is too long and their pay is too\r\nshort. How do they go about to better themselves? They strike. Well, a\r\nstrike is a fight, and in a fight, nowadays, it is always skill and money\r\nthat win. The working-men can\xe2\x80\x99t stop till they have put themselves outside\r\nof the public sympathy which the newspapers say is so potent in their\r\nbehalf; I never saw that it did them the least good. They begin by\r\nboycotting, and breaking the heads of the men who want to work. They\r\ndestroy property, and they interfere with business--the two absolutely\r\nsacred things in the American religion. Then we call out the militia and\r\nshoot a few of them, and their leaders declare the strike off. It is\r\nperfectly simple.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut will it be quite as simple,\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, reluctant in behalf of my\r\nprojected romance, to have the matter so soon disposed of--\xe2\x80\x9cwill it be\r\nquite so simple if their leaders ever persuade the working-men to leave\r\nthe militia, as they threaten to do, from time to time?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, not quite so simple,\xe2\x80\x9d the banker admitted. \xe2\x80\x9cStill, the fight would be\r\ncomparatively simple. In the first place, I doubt--though I won\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\ncertain about it--whether there are a great many working-men in the\r\nmilitia now. I rather fancy it is made up, for the most part, of clerks\r\nand small tradesmen and book-keepers, and such employ\xc3\xa9s of business as\r\nhave time and money for it. I may be mistaken.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo one seemed able to say whether he was mistaken or not; and, after\r\nwaiting a moment, he proceeded: \xe2\x80\x9cI feel pretty sure that it is so in the\r\ncity companies and regiments, at any rate, and that if every working-man\r\nleft them it would not seriously impair their effectiveness. But when the\r\nworking-men have left the militia, what have they done? They have\r\neliminated the only thing that disqualifies it for prompt and unsparing\r\nuse against strikers. As long as they are in it we might have our\r\nmisgivings, but if they were once out of it we should have none. And what\r\nwould they gain? They would not be allowed to arm and organize as an\r\ninimical force. _That_ was settled once for all in Chicago, in the case of\r\nthe International Groups. A few squads of policemen would break them up.\r\nWhy,\xe2\x80\x9d the banker exclaimed, with his good-humored laugh, \xe2\x80\x9chow preposterous\r\nthey are when you come to look at it! They are in the majority, the\r\nimmense majority, if you count the farmers, and they prefer to behave as\r\nif they were the hopeless minority. They say they want an eight-hour law,\r\nand every now and then they strike and try to fight it. Why don\xe2\x80\x99t they\r\n_vote_ it? They could _make_ it the law in six months by such overwhelming\r\nnumbers that no one would dare to evade or defy it. They can make any law\r\nthey want, but they prefer to break such laws as we have. That \xe2\x80\x98alienates\r\npublic sympathy,\xe2\x80\x99 the newspapers say; but the spectacle of their stupidity\r\nand helpless wilfulness is so lamentable that I could almost pity them. If\r\nthey chose, it would take only a few years to transform our government\r\ninto the likeness of anything they wanted. But they would rather not have\r\nwhat they want, apparently, if they can only keep themselves from getting\r\nit, and they have to work hard to do that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat they are misled by the un-American principles\r\nand methods of the Socialists among them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, no,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the banker, \xe2\x80\x9cI shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say that. As far as I\r\nunderstand it, the Socialists are the only fellows among them who propose\r\nto vote their ideas into laws, and nothing can be more American than that.\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe the Socialists stir up the strikes--at least, among our\r\nworking-men; though the newspapers convict them of it, generally without\r\ntrying them. The Socialists seem to accept the strikes as the inevitable\r\noutcome of the situation, and they make use of them as proofs of the\r\nindustrial discontent. But, luckily for the status, our labor leaders are\r\nnot Socialists, for your Socialist, whatever you may say against him, has\r\ngenerally thought himself into a Socialist. He knows that until the\r\nworking-men stop fighting, and get down to voting--until they consent to\r\nbe the majority--there is no hope for them. I am not talking of\r\nanarchists, mind you, but of Socialists, whose philosophy is more law, not\r\nless, and who look forward to an order so just that it can\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\ndisturbed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what,\xe2\x80\x9d the minister faintly said, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you think will be the outcome\r\nof it all?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe had that question the other night, didn\xe2\x80\x99t we? Our legal friend here\r\nseemed to feel that we might rub along indefinitely as we are doing, or\r\nwork out an Altruria of our own; or go back to the patriarchal stage and\r\nown our working-men. He seemed not to have so much faith in the logic of\r\nevents as I have. I doubt if it is altogether a woman\xe2\x80\x99s logic. _Parole\r\nfemmine, fatti maschi,_ and the logic of events isn\xe2\x80\x99t altogether words;\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s full of hard knocks, too. But I\xe2\x80\x99m no prophet. I can\xe2\x80\x99t forecast the\r\nfuture; I prefer to take it as it comes. There\xe2\x80\x99s a little tract of William\r\nMorris\xe2\x80\x99s, though--I forget just what he calls it--that is full of curious\r\nand interesting speculation on this point. He thinks that, if we keep the\r\nroad we are now going, the last state of labor will be like its first, and\r\nit will be owned.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe that will ever happen in America,\xe2\x80\x9d I protested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the banker. \xe2\x80\x9cPractically, it is owned already in a vastly\r\ngreater measure than we recognize. And where would the great harm be? The\r\nnew slavery would not be like the old. There needn\xe2\x80\x99t be irresponsible\r\nwhipping and separation of families, and private buying and selling. The\r\nproletariate would probably be owned by the state, as it was at one time\r\nin Greece; or by large corporations, which would be much more in keeping\r\nwith the genius of our free institutions; and an enlightened public\r\nopinion would cast safeguards about it in the form of law to guard it from\r\nabuse. But it would be strictly policed, localized, and controlled. There\r\nwould probably be less suffering than there is now, when a man may be\r\ncowed into submission to any terms through the suffering of his family;\r\nwhen he may be starved out and turned out if he is unruly. You may be sure\r\nthat nothing of that kind would happen in the new slavery. We have not had\r\nnineteen hundred years of Christianity for nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe banker paused, and, as the silence continued, he broke it with a\r\nlaugh, which was a prodigious relief to my feelings, and I suppose to the\r\nfeelings of all. I perceived that he had been joking, and I was confirmed\r\nin this when he turned to the Altrurian and laid his hand upon his\r\nshoulder. \xe2\x80\x9cYou see,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m a kind of Altrurian myself. What is the\r\nreason why we should not found a new Altruria here on the lines I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\ndrawn? Have you never had philosophers--well, call them philanthropists; I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t mind--of my way of thinking among you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Altrurian. \xe2\x80\x9cAt one time, just before we emerged from\r\nthe competitive conditions, there was much serious question whether\r\ncapital should not own labor instead of labor owning capital. That was\r\nmany hundred years ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am proud to find myself such an advanced thinker,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how came you to decide that labor should own capital?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe voted it,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the Altrurian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said the banker, \xe2\x80\x9cour fellows are still fighting it, and getting\r\nbeaten.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI found him later in the evening talking with Mrs. Makely. \xe2\x80\x9cMy dear sir,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n I said, \xe2\x80\x9cI liked your frankness with my Altrurian friend immensely; and it\r\nmay be well to put the worst foot foremost; but what is the advantage of\r\nnot leaving us a leg to stand upon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was not in the least offended at my boldness, as I had feared he might\r\nbe, but he said, with that jolly laugh of his: \xe2\x80\x9cCapital! Well, perhaps I\r\nhave worked my candor a little too hard; I suppose there is such a thing.\r\nBut don\xe2\x80\x99t you see that it leaves me in the best possible position to carry\r\nthe war into Altruria when we get him to open up about his native land?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! If you can get him to do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we were just talking about that. Mrs. Makely has a plan.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lady, turning an empty chair near her own toward me. \xe2\x80\x9cSit\r\ndown and listen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nX\r\n\r\n\r\nI sat down, and Mrs. Makely continued: \xe2\x80\x9cI have thought it all out, and I\r\nwant you to confess that in all practical matters a woman\xe2\x80\x99s brain is\r\nbetter than a man\xe2\x80\x99s. Mr. Bullion, here, says it is, and I want you to say\r\nso, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d the banker admitted, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen it comes down to business a woman is\r\nworth any two of us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd we have just been agreeing,\xe2\x80\x9d I coincided, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the only gentlemen\r\namong us are women. Mrs. Makely, I admit, without further dispute, that\r\nthe most unworldly woman is worldlier than the worldliest man; and that in\r\nall practical matters we fade into dreamers and doctrinaires beside you.\r\nNow, go on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut she did not mean to let me off so easily. She began to brag herself\r\nup, as women do whenever you make them the slightest concession.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere, you men,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9chave been trying for a whole week to get\r\nsomething out of Mr. Homos about his country, and you have left it to a\r\npoor, weak woman, at last, to think how to manage it. I do believe that\r\nyou get so much interested in your own talk, when you are with him, that\r\nyou don\xe2\x80\x99t let him get in a word, and that\xe2\x80\x99s the reason you haven\xe2\x80\x99t found\r\nout anything about Altruria yet from him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn view of the manner in which she had cut in at Mrs. Camp\xe2\x80\x99s, and stopped\r\nHomos on the very verge of the only full and free confession he had ever\r\nbeen near making about Altruria, I thought this was pretty cool; but, for\r\nfear of worse, I said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re quite right, Mrs. Makely. I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry to say that there has been a\r\nshameful want of self-control among us, and that, if we learn anything at\r\nall from him, it will be because you have taught us how.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe could not resist this bit of taffy. She scarcely gave herself time to\r\ngulp it before she said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s very well to say that now! But where would you have been if I\r\nhadn\xe2\x80\x99t set my wits to work? Now, listen! It just popped into my mind, like\r\nan inspiration, when I was thinking of something altogether different. It\r\nflashed upon me in an instant: a good object, and a public occasion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d I said, finding this explosion and electrical inspiration rather\r\nenigmatical.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you know, the Union Chapel, over in the village, is in a languishing\r\ncondition, and the ladies have been talking all summer about doing\r\nsomething for it, getting up something--a concert or theatricals or a\r\ndance or something--and applying the proceeds to repainting and papering\r\nthe visible church; it needs it dreadfully. But, of course, those things\r\nare not exactly religious, don\xe2\x80\x99t you know; and a fair is so much trouble;\r\nand _such_ a bore, when you get the articles ready, even; and everybody\r\nfeels swindled; and now people frown on raffles, so there is no use\r\nthinking of them. What you want is something striking. We did think of a\r\nparlor-reading, or perhaps ventriloquism; but the performers all charge so\r\nmuch that there wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be anything left after paying expenses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe seemed to expect some sort of prompting at this point; so I said:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated, \xe2\x80\x9cthat is just where your Mr. Homos comes in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! How does he come in there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, get him to deliver a Talk on Altruria. As soon as he knows it\xe2\x80\x99s for\r\na good object, he will be on fire to do it; and they must live so much in\r\ncommon there that the public occasion will be just the thing that will\r\nappeal to him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt did seem a good plan to me, and I said so. But Mrs. Makely was so much\r\nin love with it that she was not satisfied with my modest recognition.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood? It\xe2\x80\x99s magnificent! It\xe2\x80\x99s the very thing! And I have thought it out,\r\ndown to the last detail--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me,\xe2\x80\x9d I interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you think there is sufficient general\r\ninterest in the subject, outside of the hotel, to get a full house for\r\nhim? I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like to see him subjected to the mortification of empty\r\nbenches.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat in the world are you thinking of? Why, there isn\xe2\x80\x99t a farm-house,\r\nanywhere within ten miles, where they haven\xe2\x80\x99t heard of Mr. Homos; and\r\nthere isn\xe2\x80\x99t a servant under this roof, or in any of the boarding-houses,\r\nwho doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know something about Altruria and want to know more. It seems\r\nthat your friend has been much oftener with the porters and the\r\nstable-boys than he has been with us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI had only too great reason to fear so. In spite of my warnings and\r\nentreaties, he had continued to behave toward every human being he met\r\nexactly as if they were equals. He apparently could not conceive of that\r\nsocial difference which difference of occupation creates among us. He\r\nowned that he saw it, and from the talk of our little group he knew it\r\nexisted; but, when I expostulated with him upon some act in gross\r\nviolation of society usage, he only answered that he could not imagine\r\nthat what he saw and knew could actually be. It was quite impossible to\r\nkeep him from bowing with the greatest deference to our waitress; he shook\r\nhands with the head-waiter every morning as well as with me; there was a\r\nfearful story current in the house, that he had been seen running down one\r\nof the corridors to relieve a chambermaid laden with two heavy water-pails\r\nwhich she was carrying to the rooms to fill up the pitchers. This was\r\nprobably not true; but I myself saw him helping in the hotel hay-field one\r\nafternoon, shirt-sleeved like any of the hired men. He said that it was\r\nthe best possible exercise, and that he was ashamed he could give no\r\nbetter excuse for it than the fact that without something of the kind he\r\nshould suffer from indigestion. It was grotesque, and out of all keeping\r\nwith a man of his cultivation and breeding. He was a gentleman and a\r\nscholar, there was no denying, and yet he did things in contravention of\r\ngood form at every opportunity, and nothing I could say had any effect\r\nwith him. I was perplexed beyond measure, the day after I had reproached\r\nhim for his labor in the hay-field, to find him in a group of table-girls,\r\nwho were listening while the head-waiter read aloud to them in the shade\r\nof the house; there was a corner looking toward the stables which was\r\ngiven up to them by tacit consent of the guests during a certain part of\r\nthe afternoon.\r\n\r\nI feigned not to see him, but I could not forbear speaking to him about it\r\nafterward. He took it in good part, but he said he had been rather\r\ndisappointed in the kind of literature they liked and the comments they\r\nmade on it; he had expected that with the education they had received, and\r\nwith their experience of the seriousness of life, they would prefer\r\nsomething less trivial. He supposed, however, that a romantic love-story,\r\nwhere a poor American girl marries an English lord, formed a refuge for\r\nthem from the real world which promised them so little and held them so\r\ncheap. It was quite useless for one to try to make him realize his\r\nbehavior in consorting with servants as a kind of scandal.\r\n\r\nThe worst of it was that his behavior, as I could see, had already begun\r\nto demoralize the objects of his misplaced politeness. At first the\r\nservants stared and resented it, as if it were some tasteless joke; but in\r\nan incredibly short time, when they saw that he meant his courtesy in good\r\nfaith, they took it as their due. I had always had a good understanding\r\nwith the head-waiter, and I thought I could safely smile with him at the\r\nqueer conduct of my friend toward himself and his fellow-servants.\r\nTo my astonishment he said: \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see why he shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t treat them as if\r\nthey were ladies and gentlemen. Doesn\xe2\x80\x99t he treat you and your friends so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was impossible to answer this, and I could only suffer in silence, and\r\nhope the Altrurian would soon go. I had dreaded the moment when the\r\nlandlord should tell me that his room was wanted; now I almost desired it;\r\nbut he never did. On the contrary, the Altrurian was in high favor with\r\nhim. He said he liked to see a man make himself pleasant with everybody;\r\nand that he did not believe he had ever had a guest in the house who was\r\nso popular all round.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Makely went on, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t criticise him--with his\r\npeculiar traditions. I presume I should be just so myself if I had been\r\nbrought up in Altruria, which, thank goodness, I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t. But Mr. Homos is\r\na perfect dear, and all the women in the house are in love with him, from\r\nthe cook\xe2\x80\x99s helpers, up and down. No, the only danger is that there won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbe room in the hotel parlors for all the people that will want to hear\r\nhim, and we shall have to make the admission something that will be\r\nprohibitive in most cases. We shall have to make it a dollar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cI think that will settle the question as far as the\r\nfarming population is concerned. It\xe2\x80\x99s twice as much as they ever pay for a\r\nreserved seat in the circus, and four times as much as a simple admission.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid, Mrs. Makely, you\xe2\x80\x99re going to be very few, though fit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ve thought it all over, and I\xe2\x80\x99m going to put the tickets at one\r\ndollar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good. Have you caught your hare?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I haven\xe2\x80\x99t yet. And I want you to help me catch him. What do you think\r\nis the best way to go about it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe banker said he would leave us to the discussion of that question, but\r\nMrs. Makely could count upon him in everything if she could only get the\r\nman to talk.
